Conditionality of Ruler's Oath on Tenure
Ruling with proof: conditionality of oath on ruler's tenure via dalalat al-hal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a ruler's oath to be informed is implicitly conditioned on his tenure, and explain the role of dalalat al-hal as the proof.

Core Idea
An oath to inform is limited to the ruler's tenure because its purpose is deterrence, which only he can achieve.

A ruler makes a man swear to report every troublemaker entering the city. Is this obligation forever? Hanafi fiqh uses contextual clues (dalalat al-hal) to recognize that the oath is tied to the ruler's ability to act—once he loses power, the oath ceases. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its implications for absolute vs. time-bound oaths and immediate reporting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal question
A ruler (sultan, judge, etc.) swears a man to inform him of every miscreant entering the town. The question: is this oath binding even after the ruler's tenure ends?
2
Recognize the implicit condition from context
The specification to the ruler's tenure is established by dalalat al-hal (contextual evidence). The purpose of the oath is deterrence, which only the ruler can achieve while in power. Therefore, the oath is understood to be limited to his term.
This is a rational inference from the circumstances, not an explicit textual condition.
3
Apply the condition: oath ends upon death or removal
Once the ruler dies or is removed, the purpose of deterrence cannot be fulfilled, so the oath ceases to bind. The man does not break his oath by not informing after that point.
This is the main ruling cited from al-Fath.
4
Distinguish absolute vs. time-bound oaths
For an absolute oath (no time limit), breaking occurs only when despair (ya's) of achieving the purpose sets in, e.g., when the ruler dies or is removed. For a time-bound oath, breaking occurs at the end of the period if it was possible to inform.
From Sharh al-Kanz: In absolute oath, no breaking until death/removal; in time-bound, breaking at time's end with possibility.
5
Consider the effect of a qarina of fawr (immediacy)
If there is an indication (qarina) that immediate reporting was intended, then the man must inform as soon as he knows of a miscreant, and delay would break the oath even in an absolute context.
From al-Nahr and al-Bahr: The rule changes if a qarina of fawr exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحليف الوالي رجلاً ; لِيُعْلِمَهُ بكلِّ داعر أتى البلدة بحال ولايته) : أي يقيدُ تحليف الوالي رجلاً ; ليُعْلِمَهُ بكل مفسد أتى البلد بحال ولايته. [٢] قوله : وتحليف الوالي ; أي الحاكم كالسلطان والقاضي ونحوهما، يعني لو خَلْفَ الوالي رجلاً ليخبره بكل مفسد أتى البلدة، يتقيّد هذا الحلف بحال ولايته، وهذا التخصيص يثبت بدلالة الحال، وهو العلم بأنّ المقصود من هذا الاستحلاف زجره بما يدفع شرّه أو شرّ غيره بزجره ; لأنه إذا انزجر داعر انزجر داعر آخر، وهذا لا يتحقق إلا في حال ولايته ; لأنها حال قدرته على ذلك، فلا يفيد فائدته بعد زوال ولايته، وهو إما بالموت أو بالعزل. كذا في «الفتح». وفي «شرح الكنز» للزَّيْلَعِيّ : «ثمّ إنّ الحالف لو علم بالداعر ولم يعلمه به لم يحنث إلا إذا مات هو أو المستحلف أو عزل ; لأنه لا يحنث في اليمين المطلقة إلا باليأس إلا إذا كانت مؤقتة، فيحنث بمضي الوقت مع الإمكان». انتهى وهذا إذا لم تقم قرينة الفور، وإلا فيلزم إخباره بفور علمه به ، كما في «النهر» و«بحر»
And the ruler's swearing of a man to inform him of every miscreant who came to the town during his tenure: i.e., he restricts the ruler's swearing of a man to inform him of every corrupt person who came to the town during his tenure. [2] His statement: And the ruler's swearing; i.e., the ruler like the sultan, judge, and the like. Meaning, if the ruler swore a man to inform him of every miscreant who came to the town, this oath is restricted to his tenure. This specification is established by contextual evidence (dalalat al-hal), which is knowledge that the purpose of this swearing is to deter him from his evil or the evil of others by deterrence, because if one miscreant is deterred, another miscreant is deterred, and this is only realized during his tenure because it is the state of his ability to do so. So it does not serve its purpose after his tenure ends, whether by death or removal. This is in al-Fath. In Sharh al-Kanz by al-Zayla'i: 'Then if the swearer knew of the miscreant and did not inform him, he does not break his oath unless the swearer or the one who made him swear dies or is removed; because in an absolute oath he does not break except by despair, unless it is time-bound, then he breaks at the passing of time with possibility.' End. And this is if there is no indication of immediacy; otherwise he must inform immediately upon knowing, as in al-Nahr and al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case: a ruler (wali, such as a sultan or judge) makes a man swear to inform him of every miscreant (dā'ir) who enters the town. The ruling is that this oath is implicitly conditioned on the ruler's tenure (wilaya). This specification is derived from contextual evidence (dalalat al-hal): the obvious purpose of the oath is to enable the ruler to deter evil, which he can only do while in power. Therefore, if the ruler dies or is removed from office, the oath does not bind the man anymore. The text also cites al-Zayla'i's Sharh al-Kanz, clarifying that if the man knows of a miscreant but fails to inform, he does not break his oath until the ruler or the one who made him swear dies or is removed, because in an absolute (non-time-bound) oath, breaking occurs only upon despair (of the purpose being achievable). However, if the oath was time-bound, he breaks it when the time passes while it was possible to inform. Additionally, if there is an indication (qarina) of immediacy (fawr), he must inform immediately upon learning. The authority for this is based on rational consideration of the oath's purpose and contextual inference.

Key Terms
الوالي (al-wālī): The ruler, including sultan, judge, and similar authorities with executive power
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual evidence from the circumstances that indicates the intended meaning of an oath
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamīn al-muṭlaqa): An absolute oath without a specified time limit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a ruler swears a man to inform him of miscreants, and the ruler is later removed from office, what happens to the man's obligation?
A) The obligation continues until the man informs.
B) The obligation ceases because the ruler no longer has authority.
C) The man must still inform, but it is not binding.
D) The man must inform within one month of removal.

Key Takeaway
A ruler's oath to be informed is implicitly conditioned on his tenure, as proven by dalalat al-hal; understanding this prevents misinterpretation of ongoing obligations.

Oaths on Actions Conditioned on Life
Explains that oaths involving actions like hitting or clothing are conditioned on the subject's life

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why certain oath-related actions (hitting, clothing, speech, etc.) are conditioned on the subject's life and apply this principle to determine if an oath is broken when performed after the subject's death.

Core Idea
Actions requiring a living subject do not break an oath when performed after death.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition clarifies that oaths involving actions like hitting, clothing, or speaking are implicitly conditioned on the person being alive. The reasoning is that these actions intrinsically require a living subject to fulfill their intended purpose—such as inflicting pain or conveying understanding. Understanding this principle helps avoid mistakenly thinking an oath is violated when the act is done posthumously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions listed
List the specific actions mentioned in the passage: hitting (ḍarb), clothing (kiswa), entering (dukhūl), kissing (taqbīl), killing (qatl), and speech (kalām).
These are all actions that have meaning in the context of a living person.
2
Understand the implicit condition
Each action is conditioned on the subject's life. The passage states that any action specific to the state of life (actions that cause pain, joy, understanding, etc.) is restricted to the life of the person.
This condition is not explicit in the oath but derived from the nature of the action.
3
State the ruling for post-mortem performance
If the swearer performs any of these actions after the person's death, he does not break his oath. Similarly, if the oath was tied to a divorce or manumission, those do not occur.
The ruling applies even if the action is physically possible on a dead body (e.g., hitting).
4
Analyze the reasoning for each action
For each action, the commentary provides a rationale: hitting requires infliction of pain (impossible on a dead body), clothing involves ownership transfer (dead cannot own), entering requires interactive visitation (dead are not visited), kissing requires pleasure/compassion (absent in death), killing is like hitting, speech requires understanding (dead do not understand).
The common theme is that the intended purpose (maqsad) of the action is unattainable with a dead subject.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Given a new scenario (e.g., swearing not to feed someone), determine if the action requires a living subject. Feeding requires eating, so after death the oath is not broken.
Use the reasoning template: identify the purpose, check if possible with a dead person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب، والكسوة، والكلام، والدخول عليه بالحياة [١]قوله بالحياة ; تفصيله على ما في «الهداية وشروحها وتنوير الأبصار» وشروحه وغيرها : إنّ كل فعل يختص بحالة الحياة ، وهو كل فعل يلد ويؤلم ويغمّ ويسر ونحو ذلك يتقيّد الحلف به بحالة الحياة، فلو حلف لا يضربُ فلاناً، أو لا يكسوه ثوباً، أو لا يدخلُ عليه أو يقبله أو لا يقتله أو لا يكلّمه تقيّد الحلفُ بهذه الأفعال بحياة ذلك الرجل. حتى لو ضربه ، أو قبله، أو قتله، أو دخل عليه، أو كساه ، أو كلمه بعد موته لا يحنث، ولو علق بها طلاقاً أو عتقاً لم يحنث، ولم يقع ما علق عليه إن فعلها بعد موته. أما الضرب ; فلانه اسم لفعل مؤلم يتصل بالبدن أو استعمال آلة التأديب في محل يقبله ، والإيلام والتأديب لا يتحقق في الميت. وأما الكسوة ; فلأنَّ التمليك معتبر في مفهومها ، ولهذا لو قال : كسوتك هذا الثوب، يكون هبة والميت ليس أهلاً للتمليك. وأما الدخولُ عليه ; فلأنّ المراد به زيارته أو خدمته حتى لا يقال : دخل على دابة أو حائط، والميتُ لا يزار، بل يُزارُ قبره. وأما التقبيل ; فلانه تراد به اللذة أو الشفقة وكل ذلك مفقود في الميت. وأما القتل فكالضرب بل أولى منه. وأما الكلام فلأن المقصود منه الإفهام، وهو مفقود في الميت، هذا خلاصة ما ذكروه في هذا المقام.
And hitting, clothing, speech, and entering upon him are conditioned on life. [1] His statement: conditioned on life; its detail according to al-Hidayah and its commentaries and Tanwir al-Absar and its commentaries and others: indeed every action that is specific to the state of life, and it is every action that causes pain, harm, grief, joy and the like, the oath about it is restricted to the state of life. So if he swore not to hit so-and-so, or not to clothe him with a garment, or not to enter upon him, or not to kiss him, or not to kill him, or not to speak to him, the oath is restricted by the life of that man. So even if he hits him, kisses him, kills him, enters upon him, clothes him, or speaks to him after his death, he does not break his oath. And if he suspended divorce or manumission on it, he does not break, and what was suspended does not occur if he does it after his death. As for hitting: because it is a name for a painful action connected to the body or the use of a tool of discipline in a place that accepts it, and inflicting pain and discipline are not realized in the dead. As for clothing: because ownership (tamlik) is considered in its meaning, and therefore if he said: 'I clothed you with this garment,' it is a gift, and the dead is not eligible for ownership. As for entering upon him: because what is meant is visiting or serving him, so it is not said: 'He entered upon an animal or a wall,' and the dead is not visited, rather his grave is visited. As for kissing: because it seeks pleasure or compassion, and all of that is absent in the dead. As for killing: it is like hitting, even more so. As for speech: because the purpose is understanding, and that is absent in the dead. This is the summary of what they mentioned in this topic.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about oaths involving actions that inherently require the subject to be alive. The base text states that actions like hitting, clothing, speaking, and entering upon someone are conditioned on the person's life. The commentary details that any action specific to the living state—actions that cause pain, harm, grief, joy, etc.—when sworn about, are restricted to the person's life. For example, if someone swears not to hit a particular person, or not to clothe him, or not to speak to him, and then does so after that person dies, he does not break the oath. Similarly, if he suspends a divorce or manumission on such an action (e.g., 'if I hit Zaid, my wife is divorced'), and he performs the action after Zaid's death, the divorce does not occur. The reasoning for each action is provided: hitting is about inflicting pain, which is impossible on a dead body; clothing involves transferring ownership (tamlik), and a dead person cannot own property; entering upon someone means visiting or serving, but one does not visit a dead person, only his grave; kissing is for pleasure or compassion, absent in death; killing is like hitting, even more so; speech aims at understanding, which the dead lack. This summary is taken from various Hanafi sources including al-Hidayah and Tanwir al-Absar.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-ḍarb): A painful action involving striking the body or using an instrument of discipline on a suitable place.
الكسوة (al-kiswa): Clothing someone, which conceptually includes transferring ownership of the garment.
الدخول عليه (al-dukhūl 'alayhi): Entering upon someone with the intention of visiting or serving them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'I will not speak to Zaid,' and then speaks to Zaid's corpse after his death, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because speaking is speaking.
B) No, because speech requires understanding which the dead lack.
C) Yes, because the oath was not conditioned explicitly on life.
D) No, only if he speaks before burial.

Key Takeaway
An oath on an action that inherently requires a living subject is not broken by performing it after the subject's death.

Acts in Oaths Conditioned on Life: Hanafi Rulings
Explaining that acts in oaths are conditioned on life

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which acts in an oath are conditioned on the person being alive and explain the Hanafi reasoning for each act.

Core Idea
For an oath on a person-specific act to be broken, the act must be done while the person is alive; the purpose of the act is never realised on a corpse.

You may have sworn not to hit someone or speak to them. But what if you did so after their death? Does the oath break? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer depends on the nature of the act. This lesson explores the rule that certain acts are intrinsically tied to life, so performing them on a dead person does not violate the oath. We will examine the reasoning behind each act from the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts listed
Read the passage and list the six acts mentioned: hitting, clothing, entering, kissing, killing, speaking.
2
Understand the general rule
The oath concerning these acts is tied to the person's life. If the act is done after death, the oath is not broken.
This applies even if the oath is absolute in wording.
3
Analyse the reasoning for each act
For each act, the commentator gives a specific legal reason: hitting implies pain (impossible on a corpse), clothing implies ownership (dead cannot own), entering implies visiting (dead are not visited), kissing implies pleasure/compassion (absent in dead), killing is like hitting, and speaking implies making understand (impossible).
The reasoning is based on the linguistic purpose of the act.
4
Apply to suspended oaths
If an oath is suspended (e.g., 'if I hit you, you are divorced'), doing the act after death does not trigger the suspended effect.
Same principle: the act is not considered a violation.
5
Summarise the Hanafi position
This is the relied-upon Hanafi view from authoritative texts like Al-Hidayah and Tanwir al-Absar.
It is consistent across the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بالحياة ; تفصيله على ما في «الهداية وشروحها وتنوير الأبصار» وشروحه وغيرها : إنّ كل فعل يختص بحالة الحياة ، وهو كل فعل يلد ويؤلم ويغمّ ويسر ونحو ذلك يتقيّد الحلف به بحالة الحياة، فلو حلف لا يضربُ فلاناً، أو لا يكسوه ثوباً، أو لا يدخلُ عليه أو يقبله أو لا يقتله أو لا يكلّمه تقيّد الحلفُ بهذه الأفعال بحياة ذلك الرجل. حتى لو ضربه ، أو قبله، أو قتله، أو دخل عليه، أو كساه ، أو كلمه بعد موته لا يحنث، ولو علق بها طلاقاً أو عتقاً لم يحنث، ولم يقع ما علق عليه إن فعلها بعد موته. أما الضرب ; فلانه اسم لفعل مؤلم يتصل بالبدن أو استعمال آلة التأديب في محل يقبله ، والإيلام والتأديب لا يتحقق في الميت. وأما الكسوة ; فلأنَّ التمليك معتبر في مفهومها ، ولهذا لو قال : كسوتك هذا الثوب، يكون هبة والميت ليس أهلاً للتمليك. وأما الدخولُ عليه ; فلأنّ المراد به زيارته أو خدمته حتى لا يقال : دخل على دابة أو حائط، والميتُ لا يزار، بل يُزارُ قبره. وأما التقبيل ; فلانه تراد به اللذة أو الشفقة وكل ذلك مفقود في الميت. وأما القتل فكالضرب بل أولى منه. وأما الكلام فلأن المقصود منه الإفهام، وهو مفقود في الميت، هذا خلاصة ما ذكروه في هذا المقام.
Statement (of the author) 'by life'; its elaboration is as found in Al-Hidayah and its commentaries and Tanwir al-Absar and its commentaries and others: Every act that is specific to the state of life—which is every act that causes pain, grief, pleasure, and the like—the oath concerning it is restricted to the state of life. So if one swears he will not hit so-and-so, or clothe him with a garment, or enter upon him, or kiss him, or kill him, or speak to him, the oath concerning these acts is tied to that man's life. So if he hits him, or kisses him, or kills him, or enters upon him, or clothes him, or speaks to him after his death, he does not break his oath. And if he suspends divorce or manumission upon it, he does not break his oath, and that which was suspended does not occur if he does it after his death. As for hitting: it is a name for a painful act that connects to the body or the use of an instrument of discipline in a place that accepts it, and causing pain and discipline are not realized in the dead. As for clothing: because ownership (tamlik) is considered in its concept, therefore if he says 'I clothed you with this garment', it is a gift (hiba), and the dead is not fit for ownership. As for entering upon him: because what is meant is visiting him or serving him, so it is not said 'entered upon an animal or a wall', and the dead is not visited; rather his grave is visited. As for kissing: because what is sought by it is pleasure or compassion, and all that is absent in the dead. As for killing: it is like hitting, even more so. As for speaking: because the purpose is making understand (ifham), and that is absent in the dead. This is the gist of what they have mentioned in this discussion.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling explanation. The commentator elaborates on the phrase 'بالحياة' (by life) from the matn. He explains that certain acts—such as hitting, clothing, entering, kissing, killing, and speaking—are inherently tied to living persons. The oath concerning these acts is only violated if the act is performed while the person is alive. The reasoning for each act is based on its linguistic meaning and purpose: hitting implies causing pain (impossible for a corpse), clothing implies transfer of ownership (a corpse cannot own), entering implies visiting (a corpse is not visited; rather his grave is visited), kissing implies pleasure or compassion (absent in death), killing is like hitting, and speaking implies making understand (impossible for the dead). The passage also states that if an oath is suspended on such an act (e.g., 'If I hit you, you are divorced'), doing it after death does not trigger the suspended effect. This is the standard Hanafi position as found in the authoritative texts.

Key Terms
حالة الحياة (halat al-hayat): The state of being alive; a condition restricting the violation of certain oaths to acts done while the person is alive.
الضرب (al-darb): Hitting; a painful act or use of a disciplinary tool on a body part that accepts it. In an oath context, it must be done on a living body to constitute a violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained, if a person swears he will not speak to Zaid and then speaks to Zaid's corpse after his death, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because speaking is a physical act that can be done to anyone.
B) No, because the purpose of speech (making understand) is absent in the dead.
C) Yes, because the oath is absolute and not conditional on life.
D) No, because speaking to a dead person is not considered 'speech' in legal terms.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the violation of an oath involving person-specific acts depends on the purpose of the act, which can only be realised while the person is alive.

Hanafi Defense on the Dead Not Hearing: Responding to Four Objections
Addressing four objections to the Hanafi position that the dead do not hear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that the dead do not hear by identifying the four key objections and analyzing the Hanafi responses based on Quranic and hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The dead do not hear; Quran 27:80 is definitive; opposing hadith are either rejected or reinterpreted.

The Hanafi school's position that the dead do not hear or understand has attracted criticism from hadith-based scholars. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya systematically addresses four objections, each drawing on different bodies of evidence. Understanding these defenses is crucial for grasping how Hanafi usul prioritizes Qur'an over ahad hadith in certain contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and primary proof
Identify the core claim: the dead do not hear, understand, or perceive. Locate the primary Quranic evidence (27:80) and note its authority.
Quran 27:80 is used as a definitive proof (qat'i al-thubut) but with zanni al-dalala in application.
2
List the four objections
From the passage, extract the four objections: (1) ownership acquisition by the dead, (2) pain felt by the dead, (3) visitation and intercession, (4) hearing and responding to greetings.
Each objection is based on a different type of evidence (netting, hadith, visitation hadith, greeting hadith).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each objection
For each objection, explain how the Hanafi commentator answers: response 1 distinguishes ownership from transfer, response 2 implies pain is irrelevant, response 3 reinterprets visitation as visiting the grave, response 4 cites Aisha's rejection of the hadith.
The responses vary in strength; some rely on reinterpretation, others on rejection of hadith.
4
Evaluate the strength of the responses
Consider whether the Hanafi responses fully satisfy the objections. Note that the commentator acknowledges the majority of Companions did not accept Aisha's rejection, leaving some tension.
The critical refinement shows that the Hanafi position is maintained despite acknowledged objections.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference in fiqh: under Hanafi law, oaths involving acts directed at the dead are not broken; under opposing views, they might be.
This illustrates how a seemingly theoretical dispute has concrete legal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هاهنا نسب إلى أئمتنا الأعلام أنهم ينكرون سماع الأموات وفهمهم وإدراكهم، وقد صرح به جمع من أصحاب الفتاوى من أصحابنا وأيدوه بقوله : إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْتَى (١) ، وأجابوا عن حديث: «ما أنتم باسمع منه (۲) بأنه ردّته عائشة رضي الله عنها. وفي المقام أبحاث : الأول : فيما ذكروه في «بحث الكسوة»، وهو أنهم ذكروا أنه لو نصب رجل شبكة فتعلق بها صيد بعد موته لملكه ، وهذا يدل على حصول الملك للميت. وأجيب عنه : بأنه مستند إلى وقتِ الحياة ونسب الشبكة، وأيضاً هو ملك لا تمليك. الثاني : إن ما ذكروه من أنَّ الإيلام لا يتحقق في الميت مخالف للأحاديث الدالة على أنَّ الميت يتأذى بما يتأذى منه الحي، كما ذكره السيوطي في كتابه «شرح الصدور». الثالث : إن قولهم في «باب الدخول»: إنّ زيارة الميت زيارة لقبره لا زيارة للمقبور يخالف قوله : «من جاءني زائراً لا تعمله حاجة إلا زيارتي كان حقاً علي أن أكون له شفيعاً يوم القيامة» (۳) ، وأقوالها الدالة على أنَّ الميت يستأنس بزائره ، ويجيب سلامه، ويعرفُ مَن كان بينه وبينه ،معرفة، وهي كثيرة في كتب الحديث مروّية. الرابع : إن قولهم في «بحثِ الكلام» يخالف الأحاديث الصحيحة الدالة على أنَّ الميت يسمعُ سلامَ مَن يسلّم عليه ، ويجيب السلام، ويفهم كلام الأحياء، وهي مروية في «الصحيحين» وغيرهما. وأما ردّ عائشة رضي الله عنها بعض تلك الأحاديث فلم يعتد به جمهور الصحابة له ومن بعدهم.
And from here it is attributed to our great imams that they deny the hearing, understanding, and perception of the dead. A group of fatwa authors among our companions have stated this and supported it with His saying: 'Indeed you cannot make the dead hear' (Quran 27:80). And they responded to the hadith 'You are not more hearing than them' by saying that Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, rejected it. In this discussion there are investigations: First: Regarding what they mentioned in the discussion of 'clothing'—that they mentioned if a man set up a net and game got caught in it after his death, he would own it. This indicates acquisition of ownership for the dead. The answer: It is based on the time of life and the attribution of the net; also it is ownership without transfer (tamlik). Second: That what they mentioned—that causing pain is not realized in the dead—contradicts the hadiths indicating that the dead is harmed by what harms the living, as mentioned by al-Suyuti in his book 'Sharh al-Sudur'. Third: That their statement in the chapter of 'entering'—that visiting the dead is visiting his grave, not visiting the buried person—contradicts the hadith: 'Whoever comes to me as a visitor with no need except my visit, it is incumbent upon me to be his intercessor on the Day of Judgment', and statements indicating that the deceased is comforted by his visitor, responds to his greeting, and knows those who were between him and him, with knowledge. These are many in hadith books, narrated. Fourth: That their statement in the discussion of 'speaking' contradicts authentic hadiths indicating that the dead hears the greeting of the one who greets him, responds to the greeting, and understands the speech of the living. These are narrated in the two Sahihs and others. As for Aisha's rejection of some of these hadiths, the majority of the Companions and those after them did not pay heed to it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the previous shuruh. After stating that the dead cannot hear or understand, the commentator addresses four objections (abhaath) that have been raised against the Hanafi position. The first objection concerns the acquisition of ownership by a dead person through a net; the answer is that such ownership is based on the living person's action and is ownership without transfer (tamlik), so it does not contradict the rule. The second objection cites hadiths that the dead feel pain; the commentator does not provide a direct answer here but implies that the pain concept is not relevant to the acts in oaths. The third objection involves hadiths about visiting the dead; the answer is that visiting the grave is not the same as visiting the person directly. The fourth objection cites authentic hadiths that the dead hear and respond to greetings; the Hanafi response is that Aisha rejected those hadiths, but the commentator acknowledges that the majority of Companions did not accept her rejection. Overall, the Hanafi position is maintained, though the commentator does not fully resolve all objections.

Key Terms
سماع الأموات (sama' al-amwat): The ability of dead persons to hear; the Hanafi position denies this capability in the context of oath violations.
الإيلام (al-ilam): Causing pain; the Hanafi position states that pain cannot be caused to a dead person in the context of acts like hitting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A verse from the Quran
B) Authentic hadiths indicating the dead hear greetings and respond
C) The consensus of all scholars
D) The Hanafi principle of istihsan

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that the dead do not hear is primarily based on Quran 27:80, and despite multiple hadith-based objections, the Hanafi school maintains its stance through rejection or reinterpretation of those hadiths.

Do the Dead Hear? Custom and Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh
Defense of Hanafi school against denial charge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that an oath to hit or speak to someone is not violated after death, using the principle of custom (ʿurf).

Core Idea
Hanafis do not deny the dead hear; they interpret oaths by custom, not literal possibility.

It is sometimes claimed that the Hanafi school denies that the dead can hear, feel, or perceive. This passage vigorously refutes that claim, showing that the school's actual position relies on custom (ʿurf) to interpret oaths, not on denying authentic hadith. We will work through the four main objections and the Hanafi response to understand this nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the accusation
The passage begins by noting that some attribute to Hanafi imams the denial that the dead can hear, understand, or feel. Identify the basis for this attribution: some fatwa-givers cite Quran 27:80 and dismiss a hadith as rejected by Aisha.
This sets up the dispute: are Hanafis denying hadith or applying custom?
2
Examine the four objections
The author lists four arguments that challenge the supposed Hanafi denial: ownership after death, pain felt by the dead, visits benefiting the deceased, and the dead hearing greetings. These objections rely on hadith and athar.
These objections are not Hanafi positions but criticisms against the alleged denial.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the verse
The verse 'You cannot make the dead hear' is reinterpreted: it negates 'making hear' (إسماع) not 'hearing' (سماع), and refers to spiritually dead disbelievers, not literal corpses. The hadith affirming hearing are accepted.
This shows the Hanafi response is exegetical, not dismissive.
4
Grasp the role of custom (ʿurf)
The author explains that oaths are built on custom. When someone swears 'I will not speak to you', custom understands that to refer to speech during life. Similarly for hitting or entering. Even if the dead can hear, the oath's scope is limited by custom.
This is the key legal principle: custom (ʿurf) overrides literal possibility in oath interpretation.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
The Hanafi imams are innocent of denying the dead's abilities. The earlier fatwa statements were scholarly assumptions overridden by authentic hadith. The fiqh ruling stands on custom, not on denial.
Thus the school maintains both the authenticity of the hadith and the validity of its oath rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه

ومن هاهنا نسب إلى أئمتنا الأعلام أنهم ينكرون سماع الأموات وفهمهم وإدراكهم، وقد صرح به جمع من أصحاب الفتاوى من أصحابنا وأيدوه بقوله : إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْتَى (١) ، وأجابوا عن حديث: «ما أنتم باسمع منه (۲) بأنه ردّته عائشة رضي الله عنها. وفي المقام أبحاث :
الأول : فيما ذكروه في «بحث الكسوة»، وهو أنهم ذكروا أنه لو نصب رجل شبكة فتعلق بها صيد بعد موته لملكه ، وهذا يدل على حصول الملك للميت. وأجيب عنه : بأنه مستند إلى وقتِ الحياة ونسب الشبكة، وأيضاً هو ملك لا تمليك.
الثاني : إن ما ذكروه من أنَّ الإيلام لا يتحقق في الميت مخالف للأحاديث الدالة على أنَّ الميت يتأذى بما يتأذى منه الحي، كما ذكره السيوطي في كتابه «شرح الصدور». الثالث : إن قولهم في «باب الدخول»: إنّ زيارة الميت زيارة لقبره لا زيارة للمقبور يخالف قوله : «من جاءني زائراً لا تعمله حاجة إلا زيارتي كان حقاً علي أن أكون له شفيعاً يوم القيامة» (۳) ، وأقوالها الدالة على أنَّ الميت يستأنس بزائره ، ويجيب سلامه، ويعرفُ مَن كان بينه وبينه ،معرفة، وهي كثيرة في كتب الحديث مروّية. الرابع : إن قولهم في «بحثِ الكلام» يخالف الأحاديث الصحيحة الدالة على أنَّ الميت يسمعُ سلامَ مَن يسلّم عليه ، ويجيب السلام، ويفهم كلام الأحياء، وهي مروية في «الصحيحين» وغيرهما.
وأما ردّ عائشة رضي الله عنها بعض تلك الأحاديث فلم يعتد به جمهور الصحابة له ومن بعدهم.

وأما قوله عل : إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْنَ ) (١) ، ففيه نفي الإسماع لا السماع، على أنَّ الصحيح أن المراد بالموتى هنالك موتى القلوب وهم الكفار، لا الأموات العرفية، وإن شئت تفصيل هذا البحث فارجع إلى رسالتي : تذكرة الراشد برد تبصرة الناقد»، ولولا خوف التطويل لأوردت هاهنا قدراً من التفصيل وليرجع من شاء التوضيح والتنقيح إلى شرحي الكبير. وبالجملة : لم يدل دليل قوي على نفي سماع الميت وإدراكه وفهمه وتألّمه، لا من الكتاب ولا من السنة ، بل السنن الصحيحة الصريحة دالة على ثبوتها له. والحق في هذا المقام أنَّ هذا كله من تقريرات المشايخ وتوجيهاتهم وتكلّفاتهم، ولا عبرة بها حين مخالفتها للأحاديث الصحيحة وآثار الصحابة الصريحة . وأما أئمتنا فهم بريئون عن إنكار هذه الأمور، وإنما حكموا في الحلف بالضرب والكلام والدخول عليه ونحوها بعدم الحنث عند وجودِ هذه الأشياء بالميت ؛ لكون الأيمان مبنية على العرف، والعرفُ قاض على أنَّ هذه الأمور يراد بها ارتباطها ما دام الحياة لا بعد الموت ، فالكلام بالميت وإن كان كلاماً حقيقة، ويوجد فيه الإسماع والإفهام، لكن العرف يحكم بأنّ المراد في قوله : لا أكلمك هو الكلام حالة حياته ، وكذا الإيلام وإن كان يتحقق في الميت، لكن العرف قاض على أن المراد في قوله : لأضربك هو ضربه حياً لا ضربه ميتاً، وبالجملة : فالوجه في تقيّد هذه الأيمان هو حكم العرف لا ما ذكروه.
The Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections

And from here it is attributed to our eminent imams that they deny the hearing, understanding, and perception of the dead. A group of fatwa-givers among our companions stated this and supported it with His saying: 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' (1), and they responded to the hadith 'You are not more capable of hearing than them' (2) by saying that Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) rejected it. In this regard, there are investigations:
First: Concerning what they mentioned in 'the discussion of clothing', that they said if a man set up a net and game got caught in it after his death, he would own it. This indicates ownership for the deceased. It was answered: that it is based on the time of life and attribution of the net, and also it is ownership not transfer of ownership.
Second: That what they mentioned, that pain is not realized in the dead, contradicts the hadith indicating that the dead are hurt by what the living are hurt by, as mentioned by Suyuti in his book 'Sharh al-Sudur'.
Third: That their saying in 'the chapter of entering': that visiting the dead is visiting his grave not visiting the buried person, contradicts his saying: 'Whoever comes to me as a visitor, no need except my visit, it is my right that I be an intercessor for him on the Day of Judgment' (3), and the sayings indicating that the dead finds comfort in his visitor, responds to his greeting, and knows those with whom he had acquaintance, and these are numerous in hadith books.
Fourth: That their saying in 'the discussion of speech' contradicts the authentic hadith indicating that the dead hears the greeting of whoever greets him, responds to the greeting, and understands the speech of the living, and these are narrated in the 'Sahihain' and others.
As for Aisha's rejection of some of those hadith, the majority of the Companions and those after them did not consider it.

As for His saying: 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' (1), it negates making hear, not hearing, and the correct view is that the dead referred to there are the dead of heart, i.e., the disbelievers, not the conventional dead. If you want detail of this research, refer to my treatise: 'Tadhkirat al-Rashid bi Radd Tabṣirat al-Nāqid'. Were it not for fear of prolongation, I would have given some detail here. Whoever wants clarification and scrutiny should refer to my large commentary. In summary: No strong evidence has indicated the negation of the dead's hearing, perception, understanding, and feeling pain, neither from the Book nor from the Sunnah. Rather, authentic and explicit sunan indicate its establishment for him. The truth in this regard is that all of this is from the determinations, explanations, and assumptions of the mashayikh, and no consideration is given to them when they contradict authentic hadith and explicit athar of the Companions. As for our imams, they are innocent of denying these matters. They only ruled in oaths concerning hitting, speaking, entering upon him, and the like, that there is no violation when these things occur with the dead, because oaths are built on custom, and custom judges that these matters are intended in connection with life, not after death. So speech with the dead, although it is real speech and involves making hear and understanding, custom judges that the intended meaning in his saying 'I will not speak to you' is speech during his life. Similarly, pain, although it is realized in the dead, custom judges that the intended meaning in his saying 'I will hit you' is hitting him alive, not hitting him dead. In summary: The reason for the restriction of these oaths is the ruling of custom, not what they mentioned.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute addressing the claim that Hanafi imams deny the dead can hear, understand, or feel pain. The author first reports the basis of this attribution: some Hanafi fatwa-givers cite the Quranic verse 'You cannot make the dead hear' (27:80) and dismiss a contrary hadith as rejected by Aisha. Then four objections are raised: (1) a case of a net set by a deceased person owning game caught after death indicates property for the dead; (2) hadith show the dead feel pain; (3) visiting the dead benefits the deceased, who hears and knows the visitor; (4) the dead hear greetings and respond. The author notes Aisha's rejection was not accepted by the majority of Companions. Then the Hanafi position is clarified: the verse means 'you cannot make hear' (إسماع) not 'hearing' (سماع), and refers to spiritually dead disbelievers. The author states there is no strong evidence against the dead's hearing/feeling; authentic hadith affirm it. The earlier fatwa statements are merely scholars' attempts to explain oaths. The imams themselves do not deny these realities. Instead, oaths are interpreted by custom (عرف): when someone swears not to speak to another, custom indicates that refers to speech during life, not after death. Similarly for hitting, entering, etc. Thus the restriction of such oaths to life is based on custom, not on denying the dead's abilities. The passage thus defends the Hanafi school from the charge of denying the dead's perception, while maintaining the fiqh rulings on oaths.

Key Terms
السماع (al-samā'): The ability to hear. The text distinguishes between إسماع (making hear) and سماع (hearing).
العرف (al-ʿurf): Customary usage. Oaths are interpreted according to common custom (ʿurf), not literal or technical possibilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Hanafis believe the dead cannot hear speech.
B) The oath becomes invalid upon death.
C) Custom (ʿurf) dictates that such oaths refer to speech during life.
D) The Quran clearly states the dead cannot hear.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh does not deny the dead's ability to hear or feel; it interprets oaths by custom, which restricts them to actions during life.

Ruling on Oaths Involving Washing and Time Terms
Commentary on the rule that swearing to wash is not restricted to life, and defining 'near' and 'far' in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an oath to wash someone is not restricted to life and state the default interpretation of 'near' in oaths.

Core Idea
Swearing to wash applies to both living and dead; 'near' in oaths means less than a month by default.

This lesson clarifies two common points in Hanafi oath law. First, while swearing to hit someone is limited to that person's lifetime, swearing to wash them extends beyond death because washing the dead is a common practice. Second, when an oath uses the term 'near' without specifying a duration, Hanafi fiqh interprets it as less than one month, unless the swearer intended otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for hitting
The commentary begins by stating a known rule: an oath to hit someone is restricted to that person's life. If they die before the hitting occurs, the oath is broken.
This is the default for actions that are only meaningful when performed on a living person.
2
Contrast with the ruling for washing
The commentator says that an oath to wash someone is not restricted to life. This is because washing is an action that is commonly performed on the dead as well (ghusl al-mayyit).
Custom (urf) is the basis for this distinction.
3
Expand to other shared actions
The commentator lists similar actions: carrying, touching, dressing. All of these are done for the dead during burial preparations, so oaths involving them are also not life-restricted.
The principle is: any action that a dead person 'participates in' with the living shares this ruling.
4
Define 'near' in oaths
The second part addresses when an oath uses the term 'near' (e.g., 'I will pay your debt within a near time'). The default interpretation is 'less than a month,' based on custom.
The same applies to 'far'—interpreted as more than a month by default.
5
Note the role of intention
If the swearer intends a specific duration for 'near' or 'far', that intention supersedes the default. The passage says 'if he intends a specific period, it is according to that.'
Intention is given priority when it exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لا الغسل) : أي إن حلف ليضر بن زيداً يُقيَّدَ بحال حياته، ولو حلف لأغسلنَّ زيداً لا يتقيَّدُ بحال حياته. والفريب"
(۳) بما دونَ الشَّهْر ) : أي يقيدُ القريب بما دونَ الشَّهْر

[1]قوله : لا الغسل ؛ أي لا يتقيَّدُ الحلف بالغُسل بحالة الحياة، وكذا كل فعل شارك فيه الميت الحي كالحمل واللمس، وإلباس الثوب، ونحو ذلك. [٢] قوله : والقريب...الخ ؛ يعني إذا حلف لأقضين دينه إلى قريب، ونحو ذلك كقوله : لا أكلمه إلى قريب، فإن نوى مدة معينة في القريب، وكذا في البعيد فعلى ما
His saying: 'Not washing': i.e., if he swears to hit Zaid, it is restricted to his life; but if he swears to wash Zaid, it is not restricted to his life. And 'the near (relative)': 
(3) 'less than a month': i.e., the near (relative) is restricted to less than a month.

[1] His saying: 'not washing': i.e., the oath concerning washing is not restricted to the state of life. Similarly, every action in which the dead shares with the living, like carrying, touching, putting on a garment, and the like. [2] His saying: 'and the near...etc.': i.e., if he swears to pay his debt by a near time, and the like, like his saying 'I will not speak to him for a near time', then if he intends a specific duration for the near time, similarly for the far time, then it is according to...
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a ruling statement from the base text (متن). It discusses two types of oaths: one involving the verb 'to wash' (غسل) and another involving the term 'near' (قريب). First, the commentator clarifies that swearing to hit someone (الضرب) is restricted to when the person is alive; but swearing to wash someone (الغسل) is not restricted to life—it applies whether the person is alive or dead. This is because washing the deceased is a common practice (غسل الميت), so the custom does not limit it to life. The same applies to any action that a dead person shares with a living person, such as carrying, touching, or dressing. Second, the term 'near' (قريب) in an oath is defined as less than a month, unless the person specifies a different duration. Similarly, 'far' (بعيد) is defined relative to custom. If the person intends a specific period, that is followed. The passage thus provides rules for interpreting common time terms in oaths.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): Washing of a person, whether alive or dead.
القريب (al-qarīb): A short, unspecified time period; default interpretation is less than a month in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
An oath to wash someone remains valid after death; 'near' in oaths defaults to less than a month unless a specific intention exists.

Oaths on the Deceased: The Role of Custom (ʿUrf)
Ruling on oaths concerning actions on the deceased and the role of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that oaths are interpreted by custom to determine whether an action on a deceased violates the oath.

Core Idea
Custom restricts oaths to actions on the living, even though the deceased can perceive.

This passage clarifies a subtle point: the Hanafi school does not deny that the dead can hear or feel; rather, they rule that oaths of hitting, speaking, etc. are not violated when done to the dead because custom interprets such oaths as applying to the living. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying oaths in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of oaths
Distinguish oaths that are restricted to the living (e.g., 'I will hurt Zayd') from those that are not (e.g., 'I will wash Zayd') based on the passage's initial example.
The restriction is due to custom, not a lack of perception in the dead.
2
Refute the apparent proof against the dead's perception
The Quranic verse (27:80) negates 'making hear' not 'hearing', and 'dead' refers to disbelievers, not literal corpses. Authentic hadiths affirm that the dead can hear and perceive.
This refutes those who deny the dead's perception.
3
Recognize the Hanafi position on perception
The Hanafi imams do not deny that the dead can hear, feel, etc. Such denial is from later scholars and contradicts authentic hadith.
4
Apply the custom principle
Oaths are based on custom (ʿurf). Custom dictates that when someone says 'I will hit you', it means hitting while alive. Thus, hitting the dead does not violate the oath.
The ruling is based on custom, not on a denial of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الغسل) : أي إن حلف ليضر بن زيداً يُقيَّدَ بحال حياته، ولو حلف لأغسلنَّ زيداً لا يتقيَّدُ بحال حياته.

وأما قوله عل : إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْنَ ) (١) ، ففيه نفي الإسماع لا السماع، على أنَّ الصحيح أن المراد بالموتى هنالك موتى القلوب وهم الكفار، لا الأموات العرفية، وإن شئت تفصيل هذا البحث فارجع إلى رسالتي : تذكرة الراشد برد تبصرة الناقد»، ولولا خوف التطويل لأوردت هاهنا قدراً من التفصيل وليرجع من شاء التوضيح والتنقيح إلى شرحي الكبير. وبالجملة : لم يدل دليل قوي على نفي سماع الميت وإدراكه وفهمه وتألّمه، لا من الكتاب ولا من السنة ، بل السنن الصحيحة الصريحة دالة على ثبوتها له. والحق في هذا المقام أنَّ هذا كله من تقريرات المشايخ وتوجيهاتهم وتكلّفاتهم، ولا عبرة بها حين مخالفتها للأحاديث الصحيحة وآثار الصحابة الصريحة . وأما أئمتنا فهم بريئون عن إنكار هذه الأمور، وإنما حكموا في الحلف بالضرب والكلام والدخول عليه ونحوها بعدم الحنث عند وجودِ هذه الأشياء بالميت ؛ لكون الأيمان مبنية على العرف، والعرفُ قاض على أنَّ هذه الأمور يراد بها ارتباطها ما دام الحياة لا بعد الموت ، فالكلام بالميت وإن كان كلاماً حقيقة، ويوجد فيه الإسماع والإفهام، لكن العرف يحكم بأنّ المراد في قوله : لا أكلمك هو الكلام حالة حياته ، وكذا الإيلام وإن كان يتحقق في الميت، لكن العرف قاض على أن المراد في قوله : لأضربك هو ضربه حياً لا ضربه ميتاً، وبالجملة: فالوجه في تقيّد هذه الأيمان هو حكم العرف لا ما ذكروه.
(No washing): That is, if he swears to hurt Zayd, it is restricted to his lifetime, but if he swears to wash Zayd, it is not restricted to his lifetime. As for His statement, may He be exalted: 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' (Quran 27:80), in it is a negation of making hear, not hearing, and the correct view is that the dead there means those dead of heart, that is, disbelievers, not the dead in the usual sense. If you want detail on this research, refer to my treatise 'Tadhkirat al-Rashid bi Radd Tabṣirat al-Naqid'. Were it not for fear of length, I would have given some detail here. Let whoever desires clarification and refinement refer to my large commentary. In summary: No strong evidence has been presented to negate the dead's hearing, perception, understanding, and pain, neither from the Book nor from the Sunnah; rather, the sound, explicit Sunan indicate its affirmation for him. The truth in this context is that all this is from the determinations of the mashayikh, their interpretations, and their affectations, and they are not to be considered when they contradict authentic hadiths and explicit athar of the Companions. As for our imams, they are innocent of denying these matters. They only ruled in oaths of hitting, speaking, entering upon him, and the like, that there is no violation (hanth) when these things occur with the dead, because oaths are based on custom, and custom dictates that these matters are intended to be connected as long as life remains, not after death. Speaking to the dead, although it is speech in reality and involves making hear and understanding, custom rules that the intent in his statement 'I will not speak to you' is speech during his lifetime. Likewise, causing pain, although it occurs with the dead, custom dictates that the intent in his statement 'I will hit you' is hitting him alive, not hitting him dead. In summary, the basis for restricting these oaths is the ruling of custom, not what they mentioned.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on oaths involving actions directed at a deceased person. The commentator begins by stating that if one swears to harm someone, the oath is restricted to the person's lifetime; but if one swears to wash the deceased, it is not restricted—washing can be done after death. The commentator then engages in a lengthy critique of those who deny that the deceased can perceive or understand. He argues that authentic hadiths affirm that the dead can hear, and that the Quranic verse about not making the dead hear refers to disbelievers, not literal corpses. He says that the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) do not deny these realities; rather, they ruled that oaths of hitting or speaking are not violated when done to a dead person because custom interprets such oaths as applying to the living. The basis is 'urf (custom), not any denial of the dead's perception. Thus, the ruling is based on the conventional understanding of such oaths.

Key Terms
العرف (al-ʿurf): Custom: the prevailing practice of people that determines the interpretation of oaths
الحنث (al-ḥanṯ): Violation of an oath: when the action sworn against occurs, the oath is broken

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does hitting a dead person not violate an oath to hit that person?
A) Because the dead cannot feel pain.
B) Because authentic hadiths deny the dead can hear.
C) Because custom interprets the oath as referring only to hitting while alive.
D) Because the oath becomes invalid after death.

Key Takeaway
Oaths are interpreted by custom; thus actions on the dead may not violate oaths even though the dead can perceive.

Interpreting 'Qareeb' and 'Ba'eed' in Oaths
Ruling on the default meanings of 'near' and 'far' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi interpretation of 'qareeb' and 'ba'eed' in oaths and apply the principle that custom governs the meaning.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'near' and 'far' in oaths are defined by custom, not a fixed period.

When someone swears to do something 'soon' or 'later' without specifying, how does the law determine the timeframe? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that relies on customary usage rather than a rigid number of days. You'll learn the default periods and the distinction between judicial and moral accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms
Determine which temporal terms ('qareeb', 'ba'eed', 'aajil', 'aajil') appear in the oath.
The same ruling applies to synonyms like 'immediate' and 'postponed'.
2
Determine the default meaning by custom
Without explicit intention, the law relies on general custom: less than a month is 'near', a month or more is 'far'.
Custom is assessed locally, but the Hanafi texts give this as the default.
3
Apply to the oath
If the action is not done within the custom-defined period, the oath is broken.
4
Distinguish judicial from moral
The oath-taker's actual intention is accepted only in conscience (diyānatan), not in court if it would ease the obligation.
In court, the default applies unless evidence shows a different custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقريب بما دون الشهر في ليقضين دينَهُ إلى قريب، والشهر بعيد.

نوى، ولا يُصدق قضاءً فيما فيه تخفيف عليه بل ديانة فقط ، وإن لم ينو شيئاً فالقريبُ يحمل على ما دون الشهر، والبعيد على الشهر فما فوقه إلى الموت، ولفظ العاجل والسريع كالقريب، والآجل كالبعيد، وهذا كله مبني على العرف، فإنّ ما دون الشهر قريب عرفاً، والشهر وما فوقه بعيد عرفاً.
And 'qareeb' (near) is less than a month in the oath 'I will pay his debt soon', and a month is 'ba'eed' (far). He intends, and he is not believed judicially in what is easier for him, but only in his conscience. If he does not intend anything, then 'qareeb' is taken as less than a month, and 'ba'eed' as a month or more up to death. The words 'immediate' and 'quick' are like 'qareeb', and 'postponed' is like 'ba'eed'. All this is based on custom, for less than a month is conventionally near, and a month or more is conventionally far.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage explains the default interpretation of temporal terms in oaths. If someone swears to do something 'soon' (qareeb) or 'later' (ba'eed), without specifying a precise time, the law relies on custom. In the Hanafi school, 'qareeb' is customarily understood as less than a month, and 'ba'eed' as a month or more, up to the end of life. The person's actual intention is considered only in his conscience (dināna), not in court if it would ease the oath. This ruling is based on general custom, not on a fixed textual definition.

Key Terms
القريب (al-qarīb): Near: customarily less than a month in oath contexts
البعيد (al-baʿīd): Far: a month or more in oath contexts
ديانة (diyānatan): In conscience: the oath-taker's intention is accepted before Allah but not in court

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Within one day
B) Within one week
C) Within less than a month
D) Within one year

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default periods for 'near' and 'far' in oaths are defined by custom, not by a fixed number, and the oath-taker's intention is only accepted in conscience.

Defining 'Idaam' in Oaths: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defining 'idaam' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on what constitutes 'idaam' in an oath and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Idaam is what is eaten as a subsidiary (tabaʿan), not alone — this criterion distinguishes dips from standalone foods like meat.

When someone swears not to eat 'idaam' (condiment), what exactly have they excluded? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: the majority view limits idaam to things used for dipping, while Imam Muhammad includes anything normally eaten with bread. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining oath violations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
A man swears not to eat idaam. What counts as idaam? This is a legal definition problem.
The oath is on the word itself, not on specific items.
2
State the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf)
They define idaam as anything used for dipping (isbagh), e.g., salt, shawālīs, vinegar.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
State Muhammad’s view
He defines idaam as anything normally eaten with bread, e.g., meat, eggs.
There is also a transmission from Abu Yusuf agreeing with Muhammad.
4
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The majority use the criterion of subsidiary (tabaʿiyya) and mixing (imtizāj); Muhammad uses compatibility (mu'ādama).
Each derives from a different understanding of the root word.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: meat is idaam in Muhammad's view but not in the majority view, unless intended.
This affects whether the oath is broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وما اصطبغ على صيغة المجهول من الاصطباغ ، وهو ا افتعال من الصبغ ، قال في «الهداية»: «لو حلف لا يَأْتَدِم ، فكلُّ شيء اصطبغ به إدام، والشواليس بإدام، والملح إدام، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف
وقال محمد : كلّ ما يؤكل مع الخبز غالباً فهو إدام ، وهو روايـة عــن أبـي يوسف له ؛ لأنَّ الإدام من المؤادمة، وهي الموافقة، وكل ما يؤكل مع الخبز موافق له ، كاللحم والبيض ونحوه. ولهما : إنّ الإدام ما يؤكل تبعاً، والتبعية في الاختلاط حقيقة ؛ ليكون قائماً به، وفي أن لا يؤكل على الانفراد حكماً، وتمام الموافقة في الامتزاج أيضاً، والخل وغيره من المائعات لا يؤكل وحدها، بل يشرب، والملح لا يؤكل بانفراده عادة، ولأنه يذوب، فيكون تبعاً بخلاف اللحم وما يضاهيه ؛ لأنه يؤكل وحده ، إلا أن ينويه لما فيه من التشديد، والعنب والبطيخ ليس بإدام ، هو الصحيح.
His statement: 'And whatever is dipped in [to make] idaam' — on the passive form of 'isbagh' (dipping), which is iftiʿāl from ṣubgh. He said in Al-Hidayah: 'If he swears not to eat idaam, then everything that is dipped in is idaam, and shawālīs is idaam, and salt is idaam. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Everything that is normally eaten with bread is idaam, and this is a transmission from Abu Yusuf from him, because idaam is from mu'ādama, which is compatibility, and everything eaten with bread is compatible with it, like meat, eggs, and the like. According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): Idaam is what is eaten as a subsidiary, and subsidiary in mixing is real, so that it stands by it, and in not being eaten alone by legal estimation. Complete compatibility is also in mingling. Vinegar and other liquids are not eaten alone but drunk, and salt is not eaten alone customarily, and because it dissolves, so it becomes subsidiary. Unlike meat and similar, because it is eaten alone, unless he intends it due to strictness. Grapes and watermelon are not idaam; that is the correct view.'
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of 'idaam' (condiment) in oaths. The base text from Al-Hidayah states a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that anything used for dipping (like shawālīs and salt) is idaam, while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that any food usually eaten with bread (like meat, eggs) is idaam. There is also a transmission that Abu Yusuf adopted Muhammad's view. The reasoning for the majority is that idaam is something eaten as a subsidiary (tabaʿan), not alone. Liquids like vinegar and salt are not eaten alone, so they qualify; meat is eaten alone, so it is not idaam unless specifically intended. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: only things used for dipping are idaam.

Key Terms
الإدام (al-idām): Condiment: something eaten with bread as a dip or accompaniment, typically liquid or semi-liquid
الصبغ (aṣ-ṣubgh): Dipping: the act of dipping bread into a liquid or semi-liquid for flavor
التبعية (at-tabaʿiyya): Subsidiariness: the characteristic of being consumed as an accompaniment, not as a main item

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), which of the following is NOT considered 'idaam' in an oath?
A) Salt
B) Shawālīs (a dip)
C) Meat
D) Vinegar

Key Takeaway
Idaam is defined by the Hanafi school as anything used for dipping (isbagh), not just any food eaten with bread.

8 min
Oaths on Specific Date Stages: Description Change Prevents Violation
Ruling with rational proof on description-specific oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when an oath is made on a specific description (e.g., busr), consuming a different description (rutab, tamr) does not violate the oath, and analogize to similar cases such as laban and shiraz.

Core Idea
When an oath is tied to a specific description, changing that description prevents violation.

This lesson centres on a key principle in Hanafi oath jurisprudence: the description upon which an oath is made becomes the exclusive subject of the vow. If a person swears not to eat a particular stage of date (e.g., busr – unripe dates) and then eats a different stage (e.g., rutab – ripe fresh dates), the oath is not broken. This ruling is extended analogically to other similar cases, such as swearing not to drink milk and then consuming shiraz (a thickened milk product). Understanding this principle requires careful attention to how the description 'ties' the oath to a specific object.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling
Identify the act (swearing not to eat busr) and the outcome (eating rutab does not break the oath). Note that the same applies to swearing on rutab and eating tamr, or swearing on laban and eating shiraz.
The ruling is non-intuitive: the oath seems on 'dates' but is actually on a specific stage.
2
Extract the legal reason (illah)
The commentary explains: when an oath is made on something described by a specific attribute, that attribute becomes the cause (ba'ith) of the oath, taking the place of the generic noun. Changing the description means the consumed item is not the subject of the oath.
This is a rational (aqli) proof based on custom and strict interpretation of oaths.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
The text explicitly analogises: an oath on laban (milk) is not broken by shiraz (thickened milk); an oath on rutab is not broken by tamr. Show how the same illah (description-specificity) applies.
Analogy is explicit: 'قس عليه' – measure it on that.
4
Distinguish from opposing views
Some jurists consider the essence (e.g., 'date') rather than the stage, so any form of date would violate. The Hanafi school insists on the literal description, making the oath restrictive.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (mukhtalaf fih).
5
Conclude with practical application
In practice, a Hanafi mufti must ask the swearer what exactly they intended by the description. If the description was central, the oath is not violated by a changed description. This protects the oath-taker from inadvertent violation.
The principle is rooted in tashdid (strictness) on the oath's wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا يحنث في لا يأكل من هذا البُسر ) فأكل رطبة ، أو من هذا الرطب أو اللبن فأكل تمراً أو شيرازاً، أو بُسْراً فأكل رطباً) : أي لا يحنث في لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً، واعلم أنه لا فرق بين قولنا : لا يأكل من هذا البشر فأكله رطباً ، وبين قولنا : لا يأكل بُسْراً فأكل رطباً [١] قوله : من هذا البسر ؛ - بضم الباء الموحدة وسكون السين المهملة آخره راء جمع بسرة، وهي اسم ثمر النخلة في المرتبة الرابعة من مراتبه الست، يقال لها بالفارسية : غور خرما، وأولها طلع، ثمّ خلال - بالفتح – ، ثم بلح – بفتح الباء الموحدة واللام آخره حاء مهملة - ، ثم بُسر، ثم رطب، ثم تمر [٢] قوله : فأكل رطبة ؛ الرطب بضم الراء المهملة ، وفتح الطاء المهملة، آخره باء موحدة، بالفارسية : خرماى تروواكتمز بفتحتين خرمای خشك. [٣] قوله : وشيرازاً؛ أي فأكل شيرازاً - وهو بكسر الشين المعجمة، بعدها ياء مثناة تحتية ساكنة، بعدها راء مهملة، ثم ألف، ثمّ زاي معجمة - : اللبن المستخرج ماؤه ، بحيث يصير كالفالوذج الغليظ ، وهذا متعلق بحلفه بعدم أكل اللبن. [٤] قوله : أنه لا فرق ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه لا فرق بين حلفه لا يأكل من هذا البشر فأكل رطباً، وبين حلفه لا يأكل رطباً فأكل بسراً في عدم الحنث. والوجه في ذلك : أنَّ اليمين إذا انعقدت على غير موصوف بوصف، يصير ذلك الوصف باعثاً على اليمين، ينزل منزلة اسم الجنس، فذلك لا يحصل للحنث بعد تغيير الأوصاف، وقس عليه الحلف بعدم أكل اللبن فأكل شيرازاً، وعدم أكل الرطب فأكل تمرا.
('And he does not break the oath in 'I will not eat from this busr' and he ate rutabah, or from this rutab or laban (milk) and he ate tamr or shiraz, or busr and he ate rutab'): That is, he does not break the oath if he swore not to eat busr and ate rutab. Know that there is no difference between our saying: 'I will not eat from this busr' and he ate rutab, and our saying: 'I will not eat busr' and he ate rutab. ... The gist of it: there is no difference between his oath 'I will not eat from this busr' and he ate rutab, and his oath 'I will not eat rutab' and he ate busr in not breaking the oath. The reason for that: when the oath is made on something described by a description, that description becomes the cause of the oath, taking the place of the generic noun, so violation does not occur after changing the descriptions. Analogize to it the oath of not eating milk and he ate shiraz, and not eating rutab and he ate tamr.)
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage rules on oaths involving different stages of dates. If a person swears not to eat a specific type of date (e.g., busr – unripe dates) and then eats a different type (rutab – ripe fresh dates), he does not break his oath. The same applies if he swears not to eat rutab and eats tamr (dried dates), or swears not to drink milk (laban) and drinks shiraz (a thickened milk product). The key principle is that when an oath is made on something described with a specific attribute (here the stage of ripeness or state of the food), that description is treated as the specific subject of the oath. Changing the description means the thing consumed is not the same as the subject of the oath. The commentary explains that there is no difference between swearing 'from this busr' and 'busr' generally – both restrict the oath to that specific stage. The reason is that the description becomes the cause (ba'ith) of the oath, and changing the description prevents violation. This is based on custom and the strict interpretation of oaths (tashdid). The text also clarifies the six stages of date development: tal', khalal, balah, busr, rutab, tamr, and the Persian equivalent for each.

Key Terms
بسر (busr): Unripe dates, the fourth stage of date development.
رطب (rutab): Ripe fresh dates, the fifth stage.
تمر (tamr): Dried dates, the sixth stage.
شيراز (shiraz): Thickened milk product (like kashk or yogurt) made by extracting water from laban.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears: 'By Allah, I will not eat busr.' He then eats tamr (dried dates). Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because tamr is also a type of date.
B) No, because busr and tamr are different stages with different descriptions.
C) Yes, because the essence of dates is the same.
D) No, because any oath on food is nullified by custom.

Key Takeaway
The description upon which an oath is made becomes the legal subject; changing that description prevents violation of the oath.
