Al-Muqtada Has No Generality
Explaining the principle that al-muqtada has no generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that al-muqtada has no generality and state the consequence for niat al-takhsis.

Core Idea
Al-muqtada is a meaning, not a word, so it lacks generality and cannot be specified by intention.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies a key Hanafi usul principle that directly affects fiqh rulings on oaths and speech acts. Understanding why al-muqtada lacks generality prevents common errors in applying takhsis and strengthens your ability to analyze legal speech acts.

Guided Path
1
Define al-muqtada
Identify al-muqtada as the implied meaning necessary for the validity of the utterance (to prevent falsehood or legal invalidity). Recognize that it is a meaning (ma'na), not a verbal expression.
Example: In 'free your slave from me,' the implied meaning is 'from my liability' – necessary for the command to be valid.
2
Distinguish from hadhf al-maf'ul
Understand that omission of the object for conciseness (like in 'I do not eat') is not al-muqtada. The object is a necessary part of the verb's existence but not required for the speech's correctness.
If it were al-muqtada, every verb would imply a muqtada because every verb requires a place and time, but that is not the case.
3
Explain why no generality
Generality ('umum) is an attribute of lafz (words). Since al-muqtada is a meaning, it cannot have generality. Necessity is fulfilled by a single instance, so there is no indication for all individuals.
Quote from al-Talwih: 'al-umum min 'awarid al-lafz, wa al-muqtada ma'na la lafz.'
4
Apply the rule to takhsis
Since there is no generality, niat al-takhsis (intention to specify) is invalid. The speaker's intention cannot restrict the muqtada to a subset. This applies both in qada (court) and diyanah (religious conscience).
Example: If one swears 'I will not drink' intending only not to drink alcohol, the oath is not restricted.
5
Contrast with nakira in negation
A nakira (indefinite noun) in the context of negation (e.g., 'لا أكل أكلاً') is general and can be specified by intention. This is because the nakira is a lafz, not a muqtada.
The masdar 'aklan' is general because it is a nakira in the negative context, whereas the implied object is not a lafz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقتضى لا عموم له ، فلا يصح فيه نية التخصيص".
۹۳
المشروب، هكذا ذكروا ، والتحقيق على ما في «الفتح»: «إنّ هذا ليس من المقتضى ؛ لأنه يُقدَّر لتصحيح المنطوق بأن يكون الكلام كذباً ظاهراً لرفع الخطأ والنسيان، أو غير شرعاً : كأعتق عبدك عني ، وقوله : لا آكل خال عن ذلك.
صحيح
نعم المفعول ؛ أي المأكول من ضرورياتِ وجود الأكل، ومثله ليس من المقتضى، بـل مـن حذف المفعول اقتصاراً، وإلا لزم أن يكون كل كلام مقتضى ؛ إذ لا بد أن يستدعي مكاناً وزماناً ، وحيث كان هذا المصدر ضرورياً للفعل لا يصح تخصيصه، وإن عم ؛ لوقوعه في سياق النفي، فإنّ من ضرورة ثبوت الفعل المنفي ثبوت المصدر العام بدون ثبوت التصرف فيه، فإنّ عمومه ضرورة تحقق الفعل في النفي، فلا يقبل

.(٢٦٣ - ٢٦٤ :

"And al-muqtada has no generality, so niat al-takhsis is not valid in it."
93
...the drink, thus they mentioned, and the verification according to what is in al-Fath: "This is not from al-muqtada because it is estimated to correct the uttered speech so that the speech is apparently false to remove error and forgetfulness, or it is not legally valid: like 'free your slave from me', and his saying 'I do not eat' is free from that.
Correct
Rather the object; i.e., the eaten thing is from the necessities of the existence of eating, and the like is not from al-muqtada, but rather from the omission of the object for conciseness, otherwise every speech would be muqtada; since it must require a place and time, and when this masdar is necessary for the act, its specification is not valid, even if it is general; because it occurs in the context of negation, for from the necessity of establishing the negated act is the establishment of the general masdar without the possibility of disposing in it, for its generality is a necessity of the realization of the act in negation, so it does not accept specification." (1) His saying: "And al-muqtada has no generality" – he said in al-Talwih: "It is on the form of the passive participle; i.e., the necessary prior [element] that the speech necessitated to correct it, if under it there are individuals, it is not obligatory to affirm all of them because necessity is removed by affirming one, so there is no indication for affirming what is beyond it, and because generality is an attribute of the lafz, and al-muqtada is a meaning not a lafz." Ended. (2) His saying: "So niat al-takhsis is not valid in it" – because takhsis is a branch of generality, so where there is no generality, there is no takhsis, and if its intention is not valid, he is not believed neither in qada nor in diyanah. The commentator said in al-Tanqih: "If it is said: 'aklan' is estimated, and it is a masdar established in the language, so it becomes like his saying: 'I do not eat an eating.' We say: The masdar established in the language is that which indicates the essence not the individuals, unlike his saying 'I do not eat an eating', for 'aklan' is a nakira in the context of negation and it is general, so it may be specified by intention. If it is said: If it is not general, then he should not be guilty of sin by every eating. We say: He is only guilty of sin because it is included under the essence of eating, not because the lafz indicates all individuals."
(1) Ended from Fath al-Qadir (5:135) with abbreviation and alteration.
(2) From al-Talwih (1:263-264).
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with talil explanation from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It establishes a key usul principle: al-muqtada (the implied meaning necessary for the validity of the utterance) has no generality. The author explains that since al-muqtada is a meaning (ma'na) not a lafz (verbal expression), it does not possess the attribute of 'umum (generality). Therefore, niat al-takhsis (intention of specification) is not valid in it. The commentator elaborates by distinguishing between genuine al-muqtada and mere omission of the object (hadhf al-maf'ul) for conciseness. For example, in 'I do not eat,' the implied object (the eaten thing) is a necessary aspect of the act, but it is not al-muqtada; rather, it is omitted for brevity. If it were al-muqtada, then every speech would be muqtada because every verb requires a place and time. The principle is further clarified by contrasting it with the general nakira in the context of negation, which does have generality and can be specified. The discussion from al-Talwih and Fath al-Qadir reinforces that al-muqtada is only that which is necessitated to correct the speech (to prevent it from being false or invalid), and since necessity is fulfilled by a single instance, there is no indication for all individuals. Thus, the Hanafi school maintains that in oaths, if one utters a phrase that necessitates an implied meaning, the intention cannot restrict that meaning to a specific instance, and the oath is not believed in court or religiously regarding such specification.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): The implied meaning that is necessary to correct the speech, i.e., to prevent it from being false or legally invalid.
نية التخصيص (niyyat al-takhsis): The intention to restrict a general term to a specific subset.
حذف المفعول (hadhf al-maf'ul): The omission of the object for conciseness, which is not considered al-muqtada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-muqtada have no generality according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because it is a verbal expression with limited scope.
B) Because it is a meaning not a lafz, and generality is an attribute of lafz.
C) Because it is always specific by nature.
D) Because it is derived from the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Al-muqtada is a meaning, not a lafz, so it lacks generality and cannot be specified by intention in Hanafi fiqh.

Niyyat al-Takhsis in Oaths: Dianah vs Qada
Ruling on intention in general oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why niat al-takhsis in a general oath is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qadan), and identify the exception for specification by custom ('urf).

Core Idea
Intention to specify a general oath is believed religiously, but the judge only sees the apparent word.

When someone swears using a general word like 'cloth' but secretly intends a specific cloth, does his intention count? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the realms of religious conscience (dianah) and court judgment (qada). In this lesson, we explore how the intention of specification works only in uttered general expressions and why the mufti accepts it while the judge does not, except when custom defines the specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage: someone says 'I will not wear a garment' but intends a specific garment. Note that the lafz (word) is general.
The text gives examples: thawb, ta'am, sharab.
2
Understand the principle of niat al-takhsis
The intention to specify a general term is valid only in the uttered general expression, not in implied meanings. It is accepted in dianah because the mufti considers the person's intention.
The principle: 'إنما تعمل في الملفوظ العام لا في غير الملفوظ'
3
Distinguish dianah and qada
Dianah: the person is believed based on his claim of intention. Qada: the judge does not accept it because it contradicts the apparent lafz.
The text: 'صدق ديانة لا قضاء'
4
Recognize the exception for 'urf
Specification by custom ('urf) is accepted even in qada, because custom defines the meaning of the general word in that context.
The text: 'تخصيص العام بالعرف يصدق فيه في باب الأيمان قضاء أيضاً'
5
Apply to a new example
Take an oath: 'By Allah, I will not eat food' intending only spicy food. In dianah, he is fine if he eats non-spicy; in qada, the judge rules he broke the oath if he eats any food.
The mufti and qadi roles are different.

Source Text

For his saying: 'thawban, ta'aman, sharaban' is a nakira in the context of a condition, so it is general like a nakira under negation, because the oath in an affirmative condition is upon its negation, and when it became general, the intention of specification became valid in it.
And the principle is that niat al-takhsis operates only in the general uttered expression not in what is not uttered.
[2] His saying: 'He is believed in dianah not in qada'; so the mufti gives him [the benefit] but the judge does not, because the mufti only gives a fatwa according to what the mustafti intends, and the judge only judges by the apparent, so he does not accept what contradicts the apparent, especially in what involves leniency due to suspicion. [3] His saying: 'fa-niyyat al-takhsis'; as for specifying the general by 'urf, he is believed in it in the chapter of oaths even in qada, as previously discussed in the preceding issues.

(1) i.e., if he said: I will not wear a garment and intended a specific garment....
(2) From al-Tanqih (1:265).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling statement. It covers two main issues: (1) When someone utters a general term in an oath (e.g., 'I will not wear a garment' intending a specific garment), the intention is valid only religiously (dianah) but not in court (qada), because the lafz is general and going against the apparent is not accepted by the judge. (2) The condition of tasawwur al-birr (possibility of fulfillment) is a condition for the validity of the oath, according to Abu Hanifa and the school, contrary to Abu Yusuf who does not require it. The commentary explains that the intention of specification works only in general uttered expressions (malfooz 'amm). The example of cohabitation (musakanah) is given to illustrate that if someone swears not to cohabit with someone and intends a specific type (complete cohabitation), the intention is valid because the term includes types. The commentator distinguishes between dianah and qada: the mufti accepts the intention, but the judge does not because he judges by the apparent. An exception is made for specification by 'urf (custom), which is accepted even in court. Finally, tasawwur al-birr means the oath must be possible to fulfill; if it is impossible (e.g., swearing to do something contradictory), the oath is not valid from the start. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that the oath is valid even if impossible, and the oath will be broken immediately.

Key Terms
دين (diyanatan): In religious matters; believed based on his claim of intention, but not enforceable by a judge.
قضاء (qadan): In court; the judge decides based on apparent evidence, not on hidden intentions.
نية التخصيص (niyyat al-takhsis): The intention to specify a general term to a particular instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, niat al-takhsis is accepted in dianah but not in qada, except when:
A) it is for divorce.
B) it is specified by 'urf.
C) it is for oaths by Allah.
D) it is for general words only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the intention to specify a general oath is believed religiously but not in court, unless custom defines the specification.

Tasawwur al-Birr: The Condition of Possibility in Oaths
Ruling on the condition of possibility in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tasawwur al-birr as a condition for oath validity, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf, and apply the ruling to cases of impossible oaths.

Core Idea
An oath must be possible to fulfill; swearing to do the impossible makes the oath invalid.

What happens if someone swears to do something impossible, like 'I will fly'? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, requires that the oath be possible (tasawwur al-birr) from the start. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that the oath is valid even if impossible. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Define tasawwur al-birr
Tasawwur al-birr means the possibility (imkan) of fulfilling the oath, not mere mental conception. The oath must be possible from the beginning and continuously.
The text: 'المراد بالتصور الإمكان لا مجرد التصور الذهني'
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa: Tasawwur al-birr is a condition for the validity of the oath. If the action is impossible, the oath is not contracted (invalid).
The text: 'وتصور البر شرط صحة الحلف'
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf: It is not a condition; the oath is valid even if the action is impossible. The oath will be broken immediately, requiring kaffarah.
The text: 'خلافاً لأبي يوسف'
4
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If someone swears to do something impossible, under Abu Hanifa no oath is contracted (no sin, no kaffarah). Under Abu Yusuf, the oath is valid and immediately broken, so kaffarah is wajib.
Example: swear to fly. Hanafi: invalid. Abu Yusuf: valid, kaffarah.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the school is that tasawwur al-birr is a condition, following Abu Hanifa.
The text indicates this is the madhhab position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling statement. It covers two main issues: (1) When someone utters a general term in an oath (e.g., 'I will not wear a garment' intending a specific garment), the intention is valid only religiously (dianah) but not in court (qada), because the lafz is general and going against the apparent is not accepted by the judge. (2) The condition of tasawwur al-birr (possibility of fulfillment) is a condition for the validity of the oath, according to Abu Hanifa and the school, contrary to Abu Yusuf who does not require it. The commentary explains that the intention of specification works only in general uttered expressions (malfooz 'amm). The example of cohabitation (musakanah) is given to illustrate that if someone swears not to cohabit with someone and intends a specific type (complete cohabitation), the intention is valid because the term includes types. The commentator distinguishes between dianah and qada: the mufti accepts the intention, but the judge does not because he judges by the apparent. An exception is made for specification by 'urf (custom), which is accepted even in court. Finally, tasawwur al-birr means the oath must be possible to fulfill; if it is impossible (e.g., swearing to do something contradictory), the oath is not valid from the start. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that the oath is valid even if impossible, and the oath will be broken immediately.

Key Terms
تصور البر (tasawwur al-birr): The possibility or feasibility of fulfilling the oath; the oath must be capable of being fulfilled.
نية التخصيص (niyyat al-takhsis): The intention to specify a general term to a particular instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), if someone swears an oath that is impossible from the start, the oath is:
A) valid but broken immediately.
B) invalid (not contracted).
C) valid but requires kaffarah later.
D) suspended until possible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that an oath be possible to fulfill; otherwise, it is invalid and no kaffarah is required.

Specifying a Particular Item in a General Oath
Ruling on specifying a specific item from a general term in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the religious and judicial acceptance of an intention to specify a general term in oaths, and apply the Hanafi rule that such intention is accepted diyānan not qaḍā’an.

Core Idea
In oaths, the intention to specify a general term is believed religiously but not judicially.

This lesson focuses on a key Hanafi distinction: the difference between what is accepted by a mufti (based on intention) and what is accepted by a judge (based on apparent wording). The passage examines a person who swears using a general term but intends a specific item. This introduces the crucial fiqh principle of niyyat al-takhṣīṣ and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the hukm: He is believed diyānan (religiously) but not qaḍā’an (judicially).
2
Analyze the reason
Because the wording is general (ʿāmm), and specifying it is contrary to the apparent (khilāf al-ẓāhir).
The indefinite noun in a conditional clause (e.g., 'a garment') becomes general.
3
Understand the distinction
The mufti accepts the intention because he considers the person's inner state; the judge only looks at outward evidence.
Hence the distinction between diyānah and qaḍā.
4
Recognize the exception
If the specification is by custom (ʿurf), then it is accepted judicially as well.
Custom can make a general term specific in the eyes of the court.
5
Apply the principle
Given a scenario, determine whether a person is believed religiously, judicially, or both.
Consider if the specification is by intention alone or by common custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ ضَمَّ ثَوْبًا، أَوْ طَعَامًا، أَوْ شَرَابًا دَيْنًا: أَيْ صُدِّقَ دِيَانَةً لَا قَضَاءً؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ عَامٌّ، فَنِيَّةُ التَّخْصِيصِ خِلَافُ الظَّاهِرِ، فَلَا يُصَدَّقُ فِي الْقَضَاءِ. قَوْلُهُ: وَلَوْ ضَمَّ; أَيْ لَوْ قَالَ: إِنْ لَبِسْتُ ثَوْبًا وَنَوَى ثَوْبًا مُعَيَّنًا، أَوْ قَالَ: إِنْ أَكَلْتُ طَعَامًا وَنَوَى طَعَامًا مُعَيَّنًا، أَوْ قَالَ: إِنْ شَرِبْتُ وَنَوَى شَرَابًا مُعَيَّنًا صُدِّقَ دِيَانَةً؛ لِأَنَّهُ ذَكَرَ اللَّفْظَ الْعَامَّ، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَهُ: ثَوْبًا وَطَعَامًا وَشَرَابًا نَكِرَةٌ فِي سِيَاقِ الشَّرْطِ، فَتَعُمُّ كَالنَّكِرَةِ تَحْتَ النَّفْيِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَلِفَ فِي الشَّرْطِ الْمُثْبَتِ يَكُونُ عَلَى نَفْيِهِ، وَلَمَّا صَارَ عَامًّا صَحَّتْ فِيهِ نِيَّةُ التَّخْصِيصِ. وَالْأَصْلُ أَنَّ نِيَّةَ التَّخْصِيصِ إِنَّمَا تَعْمَلُ فِي الْمَلْفُوظِ الْعَامِّ لَا فِي غَيْرِ الْمَلْفُوظِ. قَوْلُهُ: صُدِّقَ دِيَانَةً لَا قَضَاءً; فَيُسَلِّمُهُ الْمُفْتِي وَلَا يُسَلِّمُهُ الْقَاضِي؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُفْتِي إِنَّمَا يُفْتِي بِحَسَبِ مَا يَنْوِيهِ الْمُسْتَفْتِي، وَالْقَاضِي إِنَّمَا يَحْكُمُ بِالظَّاهِرِ، فَلَا يُسَلِّمُ مَا هُوَ خِلَافُ الظَّاهِرِ لَا سِيَّمَا فِيمَا فِيهِ تَخْفِيفٌ لِمَكَانِ التُّهْمَةِ. قَوْلُهُ: فَنِيَّةُ التَّخْصِيصِ; وَأَمَّا تَخْصِيصُ الْعَامِّ بِالْعُرْفِ فَيُصَدَّقُ فِيهِ فِي بَابِ الْأَيْمَانِ قَضَاءً أَيْضًا عَلَى مَا مَرَّ بَحْثُهُ فِي الْمَسَائِلِ السَّابِقَةِ.
And if he specifies a garment, or food, or drink, [it is accepted] religiously: meaning he is believed religiously not judicially; because the wording is general, so the intention of specification is contrary to the apparent, so he is not believed judicially. His statement: 'And if he specifies' means if he said: 'If I wear a garment' and intended a specific garment, or said: 'If I eat food' and intended specific food, or said: 'If I drink' and intended a specific drink, he is believed religiously; because he mentioned the general wording, for his saying 'garment' and 'food' and 'drink' are indefinite nouns in the context of a condition, so they become general like an indefinite noun under negation; because the oath in a positive condition is upon its negation, and since it became general, the intention of specification is valid in it. The principle is that the intention of specification only works in the general wording, not in what is not mentioned. His statement: 'Believed religiously not judicially' means the mufti accepts it but the judge does not; because the mufti gives fatwa according to what the mustafti intends, while the judge rules by the apparent, so he does not accept what is contrary to the apparent, especially where there is leniency due to suspicion. His statement: 'The intention of specification' – as for specifying the general by custom, he is believed in that in the chapter of oaths judicially as well, as discussed previously.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary on specifying a particular item from a general term in oaths. The original matn states that if one says 'If I wear a garment' and intends a specific garment, or similarly for food or drink, he is believed religiously (diyānah) but not judicially (qaḍā). The reason given is that the wording is general (عام), and intending to specify it is contrary to the apparent meaning (خلاف الظاهر). The commentary expands: The indefinite noun in a conditional clause is general, so the intention to specify is valid religiously. The mufti accepts it because he considers the person's intention, but the judge rules only by the outward evidence. There is an exception: if the specification is by custom (عرف), then it is accepted judicially as well, as discussed in previous rulings. This highlights the Hanafi distinction between the internal (diyānah) and external (qaḍā) aspects of fiqh.

Key Terms
دِيَانَةً (diyānatan): Religiously; according to one's inner intention and conscience, accepted by the mufti but not by the judge.
قَضَاءً (qaḍā’an): Judicially; according to the apparent evidence, as ruled by a judge in court.
نِيَّةُ التَّخْصِيصِ (niyyat al-takhṣīṣ): The intention to specify a particular referent from a general word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when someone swears 'If I wear a garment' intending a specific garment, he is believed:
A) Both religiously and judicially
B) Religiously but not judicially
C) Judicially but not religiously
D) Neither religiously nor judicially

Key Takeaway
Intention to specify a general term in oaths is accepted religiously but not judicially, unless supported by custom.

Condition of Possibility for the Validity of an Oath
Intra-Hanafi dispute on condition of possibility for oath validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the condition of possibility in oaths and apply the ruling to scenarios involving impossible actions.

Core Idea
An oath to do the impossible is invalid — no expiation is due, contrary to Abu Yusuf.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute centered on a fundamental condition for the validity of an oath: the possibility of fulfilling what was sworn. The majority position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that if the sworn act is impossible from the outset, the oath is invalid and no expiation is required upon violation. Abu Yusuf dissents, arguing that such an oath can still be valid because the possibility of expiation provides a basis for it. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze the structure of oaths and the role of intentionality in legal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
Read the opening line: 'And the possibility of fulfilling the oath is a condition for the validity of the oath, contrary to Abu Yusuf.' Recognize that the condition of possibility (imkān al-birr) is the core issue.
The term 'possibility' here means feasibility in reality, not mere mental conception.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, an oath to do something impossible is invalid and does not create an obligation of expiation upon violation. They require that the sworn act be conceivable in reality at the time of the oath.
This position is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that even if the act is impossible, the oath can be valid because it can lead to expiation, which is a form of fulfillment (birr) in the future. Breaking such an oath requires kaffarah.
His reasoning is that the oath is like a conditional link; the consequence (kaffarah) is possible even if the condition is impossible.
4
Understand the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: if someone swears to drink water from an empty cup, the majority say no expiation; Abu Yusuf says expiation is due. This affects fatwa in cases of impossible oaths.
The tarjih (preferred view) is that of the majority, as confirmed by al-Zayla'i and al-Binayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصور البر شرط صحة الحلف خلافاً لأبي يوسف. فإن قيل: لا يساكن فلاناً ونوى في بيت واحد تصح نيته، والبيت ثابت. قلنا: إنما تصح نيته؛ لأن المساكنة نوعان: قاصرة، وهي أن يكون في دار واحدة، وكاملة وهي هذه، فنوى الكامل. انتهى. قوله: وتصوّر؛ مبتدأ خبره قوله شرط صحة الحلف، والمراد بالتصوّر الإمكان لا مجرد التصور الذهني الذي يوجد في المستحيلات أيضاً، وحاصله: إن الشرط في صحة الحلف ابتداءً وبقاء سواء كان حلفاً بالله أو بالطلاق والعتاق. فمن حلف لأشربنَّ ماء هذا الكوز اليوم، ولا ماء فيه، أو كان فصب في يومه لا يحنث. اعلم أن إمكان البر شرط صحة الحلف عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد سواء كان بالله تعالى، أو بالطلاق، أو بالعتاق، وعند أبي يوسف ليس بشرط. فإن حلف: والله لأشربنَّ الماء الذي في هذا الكوز اليوم، ولا ماء فيه، أو حلف إن لم أشرب الماء الذي في هذا الكوز اليوم فامرأته طالق، ولا ماء فيه، لا يحنث عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف يحنث. وإن حلف وكان فيه ماء فأريق في اليوم ونحوه مما يمكن البر، فإن لم يكن ممكناً لم ينعقد اليمين يميناً، فلا تجب الكفارة بالحنث فيه. قوله: اليوم؛ هذا بطريق التمثيل والاتفاق، والمراد كل وقت معين من يوم أو جمعة أو شهر. قوله: ولا ماء فيه؛ أي في الواقع سواء علم عدمه وقت الحلف أو لم يعلم. وذكر الاسبيجابي: إن عدم الحنث إذا لم يعلم عدم الماء، فأما إذا علم تقع يمينه على ما يخلقه الله فيه، وقد تحقق العدم فيحنث. وصحح الزيلعي في شرح الكنز عدم الحنث مطلقاً؛ لأن ما يحدث في الكوز غير المحلوف عليه؛ لأن المحلوف عليه هو الماء المظروف في الكوز وقت الحلف، دون الحادث بعد. قوله: يحنث؛ لأن البر وإن لم يكن ممكناً لكن أمكن القول بانعقاده موجباً للبر على وجه يظهر في حق الخلف، وهو الكفارة. ولهما: إنه لا بد من تصور الأصل لينعقد في حق الخلف، فإذا لم يتصور الأصل لا ينعقد في حق الخلف. كذا في البناية.
And the possibility of fulfilling the oath is a condition for the validity of the oath, contrary to Abu Yusuf. If it is said: He does not reside with so-and-so and intended in one house, his intention is valid, and the house is established. We say: His intention is valid only because co-residence is of two types: limited, which is being in one house, and complete, which is this, so he intended the complete. End. His statement: 'And the possibility'; it is a subject whose predicate is 'condition for the validity of the oath', and by 'possibility' is meant feasibility, not mere mental conception which also exists in impossibilities. Its summary: The condition for the validity of the oath initially and subsequently, whether it is an oath by Allah or by divorce or manumission. So whoever swears 'I will drink the water in this cup today' and there is no water in it, or there was water and it was spilled during the day, he does not break his oath. Know that the feasibility of fulfillment is a condition for the validity of the oath according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, whether it is by Allah or by divorce or manumission, and according to Abu Yusuf it is not a condition. If he swears 'By Allah, I will drink the water in this cup today' and there is no water in it, or swears 'If I do not drink the water in this cup today, my wife is divorced' and there is no water in it, he does not break his oath according to the two, but according to Abu Yusuf he breaks his oath. If he swears and there is water in it, and it was spilled during the day, and similar cases where fulfillment is possible, but if it is not possible, the oath does not take effect as an oath, so expiation is not obligatory upon breaking it. His statement: 'Today' is by way of example and agreement, and the intended meaning is any specific time, whether a day, a week, or a month. His statement: 'And there is no water in it' means in reality, whether he knew of its absence at the time of swearing or not. Al-Isbijabi mentioned that the absence of breaking the oath is when he did not know of the absence of water; as for when he knew, his oath applies to what Allah creates in it, and if absence is realized, he breaks his oath. Al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz corrected the absolute absence of breaking the oath because what comes into existence in the cup is not the subject of the oath; the subject of the oath is the water contained in the cup at the time of the oath, not what comes later. His statement: 'He breaks his oath' because although fulfillment is not possible, it is possible to say that the oath is concluded to cause fulfillment in a manner that appears in the future, which is expiation. And for the two: it is necessary to conceive the original in order to conclude it for the future. If the original is not conceivable, it is not concluded for the future. As in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of an oath that is impossible to fulfill. The matn states that the possibility of fulfilling the oath (إمكان البر) is a condition for its validity, contrary to Abu Yusuf. The commentary explains that 'possibility' here means feasibility (الإمكان) not mere mental conception. Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold that an oath to do something impossible is invalid ab initio and does not incur expiation (kaffarah) if violated. Abu Yusuf argues that such an oath is valid and breaking it requires expiation. The example given is swearing to drink water from a cup that is empty at the time of swearing: according to the majority, no violation occurs; according to Abu Yusuf, it does. Al-Isbijabi adds a nuance: if the person knew the cup was empty, his oath applies to water Allah may create – but since it doesn't, he breaks it. Al-Zayla'i rejects this, stating the oath refers to the water present at the time, not later. The final commentary gives Abu Yusuf's argument: the oath can be conceived as leading to expiation (the future consequence). The response of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that without a possible original act, there is no basis for the future obligation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as stated in al-Binayah.

Key Terms
إِمْكَانُ الْبِرِّ (imkān al-birr): The feasibility of fulfilling the oath; the possibility of doing what was sworn.
الحَنَثُ (al-ḥanath): Violation of an oath by failing to fulfill it.
الكَفَّارَةُ (al-kaffārah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath, typically by feeding or clothing poor persons or freeing a slave.
تَصَوُّرُ الْأَصْلِ (taṣawwur al-aṣl): The conception of the original act; the principle that the base act must be conceivable for the oath to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling on an oath to drink water from an empty cup?
A) The oath is valid and expiation is required upon violation
B) The oath is invalid and no expiation is due
C) The oath is valid but no expiation is required
D) The oath is invalid but expiation is still required

Key Takeaway
The possibility of fulfilling the sworn act is a condition for the validity of an oath; otherwise, the oath is invalid and no expiation is required.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Is Imkan al-Birr a Condition for Oath Validity?
Intra-Hanafi dispute: condition of possibility in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions on imkan al-birr as a condition for oath validity, explain the reasoning of each side, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
If an oath is impossible from the start, the majority of Hanafi imams consider it void – no expiation required.

What happens if someone swears an oath that is literally impossible to fulfill, like drinking water from an empty cup? The Hanafi school has a well-known internal disagreement. In this lesson, we will examine the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Muhammad, and Imam Abu Yusuf. We will explore the reasoning behind each view and see which view is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
First, recognize the scenario: a person swears to perform an act that is impossible at the moment of the oath. The dispute centers on whether such an oath is validly contracted or not.
The act is swearing an oath, e.g., 'By Allah, I will drink the water in this empty cup.'
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two') hold that imkan al-birr (possibility of fulfillment) is a condition. Therefore, the oath is not valid (batil). Abu Yusuf holds that imkan al-birr is not a condition; the oath is valid and expiation is due upon breaking it.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the proofs
The two argue that an oath must have a conceivable object; if the object is impossible, the oath is void ab initio. Abu Yusuf argues that even if fulfillment is impossible, the substitute (kaffarah) can still be established, so the oath contracts.
The key is whether the original (the fulfillment) must be conceptually possible for the substitute to be valid.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to the two, no expiation is due. According to Abu Yusuf, expiation is wajib if the oath is broken (i.e., if he does not drink from the empty cup).
This difference affects the obligation of kaffarah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The text indicates this by saying 'cinda huma' (according to the two) and by presenting their argument with clear approval.
Al-Zayla'i and al-Babarti support this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن حلف لأشربن ماء هذا الكوز اليوم ولا ماء فيه أو كان فصب في يومه لا يحنث. اعلم أن إمكان البر شرط صحة الحلف عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله سواء كان بالله تعالى أو بالطلاق أو بالعتاق وعند أبي يوسف رحمه الله ليس بشرط فإن حلف والله لأشربن الماء الذي في هذا الكوز اليوم ولا ماء فيه أو حلف إن لم أشرب الماء الذي في هذا الكوز اليوم فامرأته طالق ولا ماء فيه لا يحنث عندهما وعند أبي يوسف رحمه الله يحنث وإن حلف وكان فيه ماء فأريق في اليوم ونحوهما أن يمكن البر فإن لم يكن ممكناً لم ينعقد اليمين يميناً فلا تجب الكفارة بالحنث فيه. قوله اليوم هذا بطريق التمثيل والاتفاق والمراد كل وقت معين من يوم أو جمعة أو شهر. قوله ولا ماء فيه أي في الواقع سواء علم عدمه وقت الحلف أو لم يعلم وذكر الاسبيجابي إن عدم الحنث إذا لم يعلم عدم الماء فأما إذا علم تقع يمينه على ما يخلقه الله فيه وقد تحقق العدم فيحنث. وصحح الزيلعي في شرح الكنز عدم الحنث مطلقاً لأن ما يحدث في الكوز غير المحلوف عليه لأن المحلوف عليه هو الماء المظروف في الكوز وقت الحلف دون الحادث بعد. قوله يحنث لأن البر وإن لم يكن ممكناً لكن أمكن القول بانعقاده موجباً للبر على وجه يظهر في حق الخلف وهو الكفارة. ولهما إنه لا بد من تصور الأصل لينعقد في حق الخلف فإذا لم يتصور الأصل لا ينعقد في حق الخلف. كذا في البناية.
Whoever swore, 'I will surely drink the water of this pitcher today,' and there is no water in it, or there was water and it was spilled during the day, he does not break his oath. Know that the possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the validity of the oath according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, whether the oath is by Allah Almighty, or by divorce, or by freeing a slave. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, it is not a condition. So if he swore, 'By Allah, I will surely drink the water that is in this pitcher today,' and there is no water in it, or he swore, 'If I do not drink the water that is in this pitcher today, my wife is divorced,' and there is no water in it, he does not break his oath according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, he breaks his oath. And if he swore and there was water in it, and it was spilled during the day, similarly, fulfillment is possible. So if it is not possible, the oath is not established as an oath, so expiation is not required for breaking it. His statement 'today' is by way of example and agreement, and what is meant is every specific time, be it a day, a week, or a month. His statement 'and there is no water in it' means in reality, whether he knew of its absence at the time of the oath or did not know. Al-Isbijabi mentioned that the absence of breaking the oath is when he did not know of the absence of water. As for when he knew, his oath applies to what Allah creates in it, and absence is realized, so he breaks his oath. Al-Zayla'i corrected in Sharh al-Kanz the absence of breaking the oath absolutely, because what comes into existence in the pitcher is not the object of the oath, for the object of the oath is the water contained in the pitcher at the time of the oath, not what comes later. His statement 'he breaks his oath' because, even though fulfillment was not possible, it is possible to say that it is established as necessitating fulfillment in a way that appears with regard to the substitute (kafarah). According to the two, it is necessary to conceptualize the original for it to be established with regard to the substitute; if the original is not conceptualized, it is not established with regard to the substitute. This is stated in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of an oath that is impossible to fulfill from the outset. The ruling states that if someone swears to drink water from a pitcher that is empty, or if the water is spilled during the day, he does not break his oath. The author (al-Babarti) then explains that the possibility of fulfillment (imkan al-birr) is a condition for the validity of the oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. This applies whether the oath is by Allah, by divorce, or by freeing a slave. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that imkan al-birr is not a condition. The practical consequence is that if the oath is impossible from the start, the two imams hold that no oath is contracted, so no expiation is due upon breaking it. Abu Yusuf holds that the oath is contracted and expiation becomes obligatory. The text then addresses the case where water was present but was spilled later. The two imams distinguish: if the water was there initially but later spilled, fulfillment was possible at the time of the oath, so the oath is valid and breaking it requires expiation. Abu Yusuf holds similarly in this case. The commentary provides nuances: Al-Isbijabi distinguished between knowing or not knowing the absence of water at the time of the oath, but Al-Zayla'i corrected that the ruling is absolute non-breaking because the water that might later appear is not the subject of the oath. The core reasoning of the two imams is that an oath must have a conceivable object to be valid; if the object is impossible from the start, the oath is void. Abu Yusuf argues that even if fulfillment is impossible, the oath can still be established to necessitate expiation.

Key Terms
إمكان البر (imkān al-birr): The possibility of fulfilling the oath literally, as required by the wording.
حنث (ḥinth): Breaking the oath by failing to fulfill it, either by commission or omission.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation required for breaking a valid oath, such as feeding or clothing ten poor people or freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, if someone swears 'By Allah, I will drink the water in this cup' when the cup is empty, what is the ruling?
A) The oath is valid and expiation is due if he does not drink.
B) The oath is not contracted and no expiation is due.
C) The oath is valid but he must try to find water.
D) The oath is invalid and he must seek forgiveness.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi requires imkan al-birr at the time of swearing; impossible oaths are not contracted, and no expiation is due upon breach.

Absolute vs Time-Bound Oaths: The Spilled Water Dispute
Distinction between mu'aqqat and mutlaq oaths when water is spilled

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute (muṭlaq) and time-bound (mu'aqqat) oaths in Hanafi fiqh and state the ruling of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) versus Abu Yusuf when the subject of the oath is lost.

Core Idea
In time-bound oaths, fulfillment is due at the last part of the time; if the object is lost before, the oath becomes void, not broken.

This lesson unpacks an important intra-Hanafi difference about the effect of losing the subject of an oath. The central question: does the possibility of fulfillment (imkān al-birr) condition the continuation of the oath? Abu Yusuf differentiates between absolute and time-bound oaths differently than the two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad). We will trace the reasoning and see how it affects the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
There are two cases: (1) when there is no water in the pitcher at the time of the oath, and (2) when water is present but later spilled. Both arise in absolute and time-bound oaths.
The passage discusses the ruling for each.
2
Distinguish absolute vs time-bound
An absolute oath (muṭlaq) has no time restriction – fulfillment is immediate. A time-bound oath (mu'aqqat) specifies a time, e.g., 'today', and fulfillment is required at the last part of that time.
This distinction is key to the dispute.
3
State the position of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
In an absolute oath, if the water is spilled, the oath is broken immediately (ḥanth) because the possibility of fulfillment is lost. In a time-bound oath, the oath is not broken until the end of the time; if at that moment fulfillment is impossible, the oath becomes void (bāṭil) and no expiation is due.
The condition of imkān al-birr applies at the time the act is due.
4
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that in a time-bound oath, the oath is contracted (mun‘aqid) and remains valid; if at the end the person has not fulfilled it, the oath is broken and expiation is wajib. He does not consider the loss of the object as voiding the oath.
He focuses on the grace period: the time-binding gives a chance to fulfill later.
5
Understand the reasoning: imkān al-birr as a condition
The two maintain that possibility of fulfillment is a condition both at the inception and continuation of the oath. In a time-bound oath, the act is due only at the last part; if then the object is gone, there is no possibility, so the oath fails. Abu Yusuf does not require this condition for continuation.
This difference in usul leads to different outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أطلق فكذا في الأول دون الثاني. أي إن لم يقل اليوم لا يحنث فيما لم يكن في الكوز ماءً عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف وإن كان فصب يحنث إجماعاً. وذلك لأنه إذا لم يكن في الكوز ماءً فالحكم على ما ذكر. يعني تنعقد اليمين عند أبي يوسف ويظهر ذلك في وجوب الكفارة لعدم إمكان البر وعندهما لا تنعقد اليمين لعدم إمكان البر فلا تجيء الكفارة بالحنث. وذلك لأن إمكان البر شرط لابتداء اليمين وبقائها أيضاً. وإذ لا إمكان فلا بقاء. فإن قلت البر متصور في صورة الإراقة لأن الإعادة ممكنة. قلت إن البر إنما يجب في هذه الصورة في آخر جزء من أجزاء اليوم بحيث لا يسع فيه غيره فلا يمكن إعادة الماء في الكوز وشربه في ذلك الزمان. كذا في العناية. قوله وإن أطلق أي لم يقيّد حلفه باليوم وقال لأشربن الماء الذي في هذا الكوز أو قال إن لم أشرب الماء الذي في هذا الكوز فامرأته طالق. قوله فكذا في الأول أي في الوجه الأول وهو ما إذا لم يكن في الكوز ماء فيحنث عند أبي يوسف ولا يحنث عندهما بخلاف الثاني أي ما إذا كان فيه ماء فأريق فإنه يحنث عندهما أيضاً. قال في الهداية فأبو يوسف فرق بين المطلق والمؤقت ووجه الفرق إن التأقيت للتوسعة فلا يجب الفعل إلا في آخر الوقت فلا يحنث قبله وفي المطلق يجب البر كما فرغ وقد عجز فيحنث في الحال. وهما قد فرقا بينهما ووجه الفرق إن في المطلق يجب البر كما فرغ فإذا فات البر بفوات ما عقد عليه اليمين يحنث في يمينه كما إذا مات الحالف والماء باق أما في المؤقت يجب البر في الجزء الأخير من الوقت وعند ذلك لم تبق محلية البر لعدم التصور فلا يجب البر فيه وتبطل اليمين كما إذا عقده ابتداءً في هذه الحالة. انتهى.
And if he made the oath absolute, then it is as in the first case but not the second. That is, if he did not say 'today', he does not break his oath when there is no water in the pitcher according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. But if there was water and it was spilled, he breaks his oath by consensus. This is because if there is no water in the pitcher, the ruling is as mentioned. That is, the oath is contracted according to Abu Yusuf, and this appears in the obligation of expiation due to the impossibility of fulfillment; according to the two, the oath is not contracted due to the impossibility of fulfillment, so no expiation comes for breaking it. This is because the possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the initiation of the oath and also for its continuation. Since there is no possibility, there is no continuation. If you say: Fulfillment is conceivable in the case of spilling because returning (the water) is possible. I say: Fulfillment is only required in this case at the last part of the day such that nothing else can accommodate it, so it is impossible to return the water to the pitcher and drink it at that time. This is stated in al-'Inayah. His statement 'and if he made it absolute' means he did not restrict his oath to 'today' and said, 'I will surely drink the water in this pitcher,' or said, 'If I do not drink the water in this pitcher, my wife is divorced.' His statement 'so it is as in the first' means in the first scenario, which is when there is no water in the pitcher – he breaks his oath according to Abu Yusuf but not according to the two – contrary to the second scenario, which is when there is water and it was spilled, for he breaks his oath according to both as well. It is stated in al-Hidayah: 'Abu Yusuf differentiated between the absolute and the time-bound. The reason for the difference is that time-binding provides grace, so the act is only required at the end of the time, so he does not break it before that. In the absolute, fulfillment is required immediately, and he is unable, so he breaks it immediately. The two also differentiated between them. The reason for the difference is that in the absolute, fulfillment is required immediately; so if fulfillment is lost by the loss of the subject of the oath, he breaks his oath, just as if the one swearing dies and the water remains. As for the time-bound, fulfillment is required at the last part of the time, and at that point the locus of fulfillment no longer exists due to lack of conceptualization, so fulfillment is not required therein and the oath becomes void, just as if he had contracted it initially in that state.' End quote.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage elaborates the distinction between absolute (mutlaq) and time-bound (mu'aqqat) oaths in the context of the earlier dispute. The author explains that if the oath is absolute (not specified to a time), the ruling is the same as the first case: if there is no water, Abu Yusuf says the oath is contracted and expiation is due, while the two say it is not. However, in the second case where water was present but spilled, all agree that the oath is broken and expiation is due – because fulfillment was possible initially (the water existed) but was lost by the spilling. The key difference between Abu Yusuf and the two lies in their reasoning for the distinction between absolute and time-bound. Abu Yusuf says: time-binding gives a grace period, so the act is only due at the end of the period; thus, if the water is spilled, one can still hope to fulfill it later (e.g., by obtaining more water) – but if that is impossible at the final moment, then the oath is broken. In contrast, the two say: in absolute oaths, fulfillment is required immediately; if the subject of the oath is lost (like water spilled), the oath is broken immediately. In time-bound oaths, fulfillment is required at the final part of the time; but if at that final moment fulfillment is impossible (e.g., no water can be obtained in time), then the oath is void because the condition of possibility is not met. This shows the consistent application of the condition of imkan al-birr by the two: the possibility must exist at the time when fulfillment is due. Abu Yusuf's view, though different, is also recorded.

Key Terms
مطلق (muṭlaq): An oath not restricted to a specific time; fulfillment is required immediately.
مؤقت (mu'aqqat): An oath restricted to a specific time period, such as 'today'; fulfillment is required within that time, and the obligated act is due at the last part of the period.
إمكان البر (imkān al-birr): The possibility of fulfilling the oath; according to the two, this is a condition for both the initiation and continuation of the oath.
حنث (ḥanth): Breaking a valid oath, which incurs expiation (kaffārah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (the two), if someone swears 'By Allah, I will drink this water today' and the water is spilled an hour later, when does the oath break or become void?
A) The oath is broken immediately upon spilling.
B) The oath is broken at the end of the day if not fulfilled.
C) The oath becomes void at the end of the day because fulfillment is impossible.
D) The oath remains valid and must be fulfilled the next day.

Key Takeaway
In time-bound oaths, the possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the continuance of the oath; if the subject is lost and cannot be recovered within the time, the oath becomes void, not broken.

Impossible Oaths: Validity and Immediate Sin
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths involving impossible acts, explain why the oath is valid despite impossibility, and apply the qiyas on the elderly person's fast to determine the timing of hanth (sin) and kaffarah.

Core Idea
An oath to do something impossible is valid because fulfillment is conceptually conceivable, and inability triggers immediate sin and expiation.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a seemingly absurd scenario: a person swears to ascend to heaven or turn stone to gold. Common sense might dismiss such an oath as void, but Hanafi fiqh rules it valid. The key is that validity depends on the conceptual possibility of fulfilling the oath (tasawwur al-birr), not on actual ability. The passage also presents a crucial difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding when the sin (hanth) occurs, especially for time-bound oaths. The proof is a qiyas (analogy) on the obligation of fasting for an elderly person who cannot fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the base ruling
The source passage explicitly states that an oath to perform an impossible act (e.g., ascending to heaven, turning stone to gold, killing a known dead person) is valid (in'aqadat al-yamin). The swearer becomes sinful (hanith) due to his inability to fulfill the oath.
The ruling applies when the swearer knows the act is impossible at the time of the oath.
2
Locate the dalil (proof) – the qiyas
The commentary in Al-Inayah provides the proof: just as Allah obligates fasting on the elderly who cannot fast, and they must pay fidya (substitute), similarly, the oath is valid because there is a substitute (kaffarah) and the act is conceptually conceivable (tasawwur al-birr).
The asl (original case) is the elderly person's fast; the far' (new case) is the impossible oath; the illa (effective cause) is the existence of a substitute.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
The link: In both cases, the obligation (fasting/fulfilling the oath) is valid despite inability because the obligation has a substitute (fidya/kaffarah). The principle is that Allah's obligation does not depend on ability when there is a substitute. Therefore, the oath is valid, and sin occurs immediately due to inability.
This qiyas is jali (clear) because the underlying cause is explicitly mentioned.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (dispute)
Abu Yusuf agrees the oath is valid but differs on the timing of hanth for time-bound oaths. He says hanth occurs only after the specified time passes, because the obligation to fulfill is not immediate. Abu Hanifa holds that hanth occurs immediately in all cases, as the impossibility is constant.
The matn (base text) presents Abu Hanifa's view without qualification, indicating it is the relied-upon position.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and conclude
The commentator (Al-Inayah) supports Abu Hanifa's view. The practical effect (thamarah al-khilaf): under Abu Hanifa, kaffarah is due immediately after the oath; under Abu Yusuf, it is due only after the time expires for time-bound oaths. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is Abu Hanifa's position.
The passage includes the phrase 'wa hanith li al-ajz' which implies immediate hanth, consistent with Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي ليصعدن السماء، أو ليقلبن هذا الحجر ذهباً، أو ليقتلن فلاناً عالماً بموته انعقد اليمين لتصور البر، وحنث للعجز. وعند أبي يوسف يحنث في الكل، ففي المؤقت بعد مضي الوقت، وفي غير المؤقت يحنث في الحال. قوله: في الكل؛ أي في الصور الأربعة ذكر اليوم أو لا لم يكن الماء فيه أو كان فيه فأريق. قوله: وفي ليصعدن السماء...الخ؛ ومثله: إن لم أمس السماء، بخلاف قوله: إن تركت مس السماء فعبدي حر، فإن الشرط هو الترك، وهو غير موجود في غير المقدور عادة، وعدم المس يتحقق فيه. كذا في «المحيط» وفي «شرح الجامع الكبير»، قال الكرخي: إذا حلف أن يفعل ما لا يقدر عليه كقوله: لأصعدن السماء ففيه إثم. قوله: أو ليقلبن؛ مضارع من التقليب، أي ليجعلن هذا الحجر ذهباً. قوله: عالماً؛ أي حال كون الحالف عالماً أن فلاناً قد مات قبل هذا الكلام. قوله: انعقد اليمين؛ قال في «العناية»: «إنما كان كذلك لأن إيجاب العبد معتبر بإيجاب الله تعالى، وإيجاب الله لا يعتمد التصور دون القدرة فيما له خلف، ألا يرى أن الصوم واجب على الشيخ الفاني ولم يكن له قدرة لإمكان التصور والخلف، فكذلك هاهنا حنث عقيب وجوب البر، فوجبت الكفارة للعجز الثابت عادة كما وجبت الفدية هناك عقيب وجوب الصوم». قوله: وحنث؛ أي في الحال: أي إن كان اليمين مطلقاً، وبعد مضي الوقت إن كانت مقيدة بوقت.
And regarding [an oath] 'he will surely ascend the sky,' or 'he will surely turn this stone into gold,' or 'he will surely kill so-and-so knowing that he is dead,' the oath is valid because fulfillment is conceivable, and he becomes sinful (hanith) due to inability. And according to Abu Yusuf, he becomes sinful in all [cases]; in a time-bound [oath] after the time passes, and in a non-time-bound [oath] he becomes sinful immediately. His statement: 'in all' i.e. in the four images: whether he mentioned the day or not, whether there was no water in it or there was and it was poured out. His statement: 'and regarding ascending the sky...etc.' and its like: 'if I do not touch the sky,' unlike his statement: 'if I leave touching the sky, then my slave is free,' for the condition is leaving (tark), which does not exist in what is not normally within one's power, but not touching is realized in it. Thus in al-Muhit and Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Karkhi said: 'If he swears to do what he is not able to do, like his statement: I will surely ascend the sky, there is sin in it.' His statement: 'or he will surely turn it over' is a present tense from taqlib, i.e. he will surely make this stone gold. His statement: 'knowing' i.e. while the swearer is knowing that so-and-so had died before this speech. His statement: 'the oath is valid' said in al-Inayah: 'It is only thus because the slave’s obligation is considered by Allah’s obligation, and Allah’s obligation does not rely on possibility without ability in what has a substitute. Do you not see that fasting is obligatory on the elderly man who is decrepit and he has no ability due to the possibility of conception and substitute? Thus here, he becomes sinful directly after the obligation of birr, so expiation becomes obligatory for the inability established by custom, just as fidya became obligatory there directly after the obligation of fasting.' His statement: 'and he becomes sinful' i.e. immediately: if the oath is absolute, and after the passing of the time if it is tied to a time.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on oaths that involve acts impossible by nature, such as ascending to heaven or turning a stone into gold. The matn states that the oath is valid (mun'aqid) because fulfillment (birr) is conceptually conceivable (tasawwur al-birr), even if practically impossible. The swearer becomes sinful (hanith) due to his inability to fulfill. Abu Yusuf qualifies this: he becomes sinful in all cases, but for time-bound oaths, the sin occurs after the specified time elapses, while for absolute oaths it occurs immediately. The commentary explains that 'in all' refers to the four scenarios from the previous discussion (with or without mentioning a day, water present or not). It then compares this to an oath like 'I will not touch the sky' versus 'If I leave touching the sky, my slave is free' – the latter involves a condition of leaving (tark), which is possible (not touching normally is true), so the oath is valid. Al-Karkhi confirms that swearing to do the impossible incurs sin. The key proof from al-Inayah uses qiyas: just as fasting is obligatory on an elderly person who cannot actually fast, and expiation (fidya) becomes due, similarly here, since birr is conceivable and has a substitute (kaffarah), the oath is valid and sin occurs, requiring expiation for inability established by custom. The conclusion: hanth occurs immediately for absolute oaths, and after the time for restricted ones.

Key Terms
انعقد اليمين (in'aqada al-yamin): The oath is legally valid and binding, creating a religious obligation.
تصور البر (tasawwur al-birr): Conceptual possibility of fulfillment; the act can be imagined or defined, even if not practically feasible.
خلف (khalaf): Substitute or alternative, here referring to kaffarah as a substitute for the impossible act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Never, because it is impossible.
B) Immediately after the oath.
C) After one day.
D) Only if he tries and fails.

Key Takeaway
An oath to do an impossible act is valid in Hanafi fiqh because fulfillment is conceptually conceivable, and inability triggers immediate sin (hanth) and kaffarah.

Oaths on Customarily Impossible Acts: Validity and Immediate Breach
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths for customarily impossible acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths for customarily impossible acts and explain why the oath is valid despite customary impossibility.

Core Idea
An oath on an inherently possible act is valid even if customarily impossible; breach is immediate and expiation is required.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether an oath to perform a customarily impossible act (like ascending to the sky) is valid. The majority Hanafi view holds that the oath is concluded because the act is inherently possible, and the swearer breaks his oath immediately due to his incapacity. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling, the dissenting views of Abu Yusuf and Zufar, and the practical consequence of expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three example oaths
List the three oaths mentioned: ascending to the sky, turning stone to gold, killing a dead person knowing he is dead.
These are all acts customarily impossible.
2
State the majority Hanafi ruling (Abu Hanifa's view)
The oath is valid (mun'aqid) and the swearer becomes a breaker (hanith) immediately, because the act is inherently possible.
Validity is based on inherent possibility, not customary possibility.
3
Understand the distinction: inherent vs customary possibility
Inherent possibility (imkan dhati) means the act is not logically impossible. Customary impossibility (imkan urfi) is simply difficult or unusual. The Hanafi school bases oath validity on the former.
This distinction is key to the legal reasoning.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disputes
Abu Yusuf distinguishes time-bound oaths (breach after time) from absolute oaths (immediate breach). Zufar holds the oath invalid because customary impossibility is like absolute impossibility.
Compare their proofs and the Hanafi response.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, expiation (kaffarah) is obligatory upon breach. Under Zufar, no expiation is required because the oath is void.
This is the practical difference in ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي ليصعدنَّ السَّماء، أو ليقلبن هذا الحجر ذهباً ، أو ليقتلن فلاناً عالماً بموته انعقد اليمين لتصور البر، وحنث للعجز وعند أبي يوسف الله يحنث في الكلِّ ، ففي المؤقِّتِ بعد مضي الوقت، وفي غير المؤقت يحنث في الحال. ( وفي ليصعدن السَّماء " ، أو ليقلبن" هذا الحجر ذهباً ، أو ليقتلن فلاناً عالماً بموته انعقد اليمين لتصوّر البرّ، وحنث للعجز آخر، فلزم الوجوب عقب الحلف موسعاً بشرط عدم الفوات، فإذا فات المحل ظهر أنَّ الوجوب كان مضيقاً من أوّل أوقات الإمكان، ونظيره ما قرّروه في القول بوجوب الحج موسعاً [1] قوله : في الكلّ ؛ أي في الصور الأربعة ذكرُ اليوم أو لا لم يكن الماء فيه أو كان فيه فأريق. [2]قوله : مستحيلاً عادة ؛ فإنّ صعود السماء وتقليب الحجر ذهباً وقتل الميت مستحيل عادة، فكان كالمستحيل حقيقة، فلا تنعقد اليمين، كما لم تنعقد في مسألة الكوز. والجواب عنه : إن مبنى انعقاد اليمين على إمكان المحلوف عليه ذاتاً، لا على إمكانه عادة. [3] قوله : ممكنة ؛ أي في ذاتها غير ممتنعة في ذاتها ؛ فإنّ الملائكة تصعد السماء في كل يوم، وقد صعد عليه سيدنا عيسى الكلية، وسيدنا إدريس العلة، ونبينا ليلة المعراج، كما ذكر الله الله في كتابه، ولولا إمكانه في نفسه لم يوجد في مخلوق. وتقليب الحجر ذهباً ممكن بتحويل الله ، وأهل صنعة الكيميا يفعلون ذلك ، ولا دليل على عدم إمكانه. مرة. وكذا قتل الميت ممكناً بأن يخلق الله له فيه الحياة بعد موته، وقد وقع ذلك غير [4] قوله : في الحال ؛ المراد به ما قبل الموت، فيدخل فيه المطلق والمقيد، وقد ذكرنا الفرق بينهما. [5] قوله : للعجز عادة ; علّة للحنث في الحال، والحاصل : إنّه تنعقد اليمين لإمكان المحلوف عليه في ذاته ويحنث للعجز العادي، بخلاف مسألة الكوز، فإنّ
And [regarding] 'I will certainly ascend to the sky,' or 'I will certainly turn this stone into gold,' or 'I will certainly kill so-and-so knowing he is dead,' the oath is concluded because fulfillment is conceivable, and he becomes a breaker due to incapacity. And according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, he becomes a breaker in all cases: in the time-bound case after the time passes, and in the non-time-bound case he becomes a breaker immediately. (And [regarding] 'I will certainly ascend to the sky,' or 'I will certainly turn this stone into gold,' or 'I will certainly kill so-and-so knowing he is dead,' the oath is concluded because fulfillment is conceivable, and he becomes a breaker due to incapacity. Another [point]: the obligation follows immediately upon the oath broadly [with time] on condition of non-loss, so when the subject is lost it becomes apparent that the obligation was narrow from the first possible time, and its analogy is what they established regarding the obligation of Hajj being broad. [1] His statement: 'in all' means in the four cases, whether he mentioned a day or not, whether there was water or it was spilled. [2] His statement: 'And regarding ascending to the sky...etc.' and similar to it: 'If I do not touch the sky,' contrary to his statement: 'If I leave touching the sky, then my slave is free,' because the condition is leaving, which does not exist in what is not normally possible, but non-touching is realized therein. Thus in al-Muhit and in Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Karkhi said: If he swears to do what he is unable to do, like saying 'I will certainly ascend to the sky,' there is no sin. [3] His statement: 'or I will certainly turn' is present tense from taqlib, meaning 'I will certainly make this stone gold.' [4] His statement: 'knowing' means the swearer knowing that so-and-so died before this speech. [5] His statement: 'the oath is concluded' – he said in al-Inayah: 'That is so because the obligation of a servant is considered by the obligation of Allah, and the obligation of Allah does not depend on conceivability without ability in what has a substitute. Do you not see that fasting is obligatory on the decrepit old man even though he has no ability, due to the possibility of conceivability and substitute. So here too, he becomes a breaker after the obligation of fulfillment, so expiation becomes obligatory due to incapacity established by custom, just as the ransom became obligatory there after the obligation of fasting.' [6] His statement: 'and he becomes a breaker' means immediately if the oath is absolute, and after the time passes if it was time-bound. Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections. And if he did not know, [the oath is not concluded], and in it is the disagreement of Zufar, for according to him the oath is not concluded because fulfillment is customarily impossible. We say: These matters are inherently possible, and this suffices for the conclusion of the oath, and he becomes a breaker immediately without waiting until the time of death due to customary incapacity. [On killing] so-and-so. [4] [1] His statement: 'And if he did not know' means if he did not know of the death of so-and-so before his oath, so he said 'I will certainly kill him' while he had died before, the oath is not concluded and expiation is not obligatory. [2] His statement: 'customarily impossible' – for ascending the sky, turning stone into gold, and killing the dead are customarily impossible, so it is like truly impossible, so the oath is not concluded, as it is not concluded in the issue of the pitcher. The answer to it: The basis of the conclusion of the oath is on the inherent possibility of the sworn matter, not on its customary possibility. [3] His statement: 'possible' means inherently not impossible in itself; for the angels ascend the sky every day, and our master Isa (peace be upon him) ascended, and our master Idris (peace be upon him), and our Prophet (peace be upon him) on the night of the Mi'raj, as Allah mentioned in His Book. Were it not inherently possible, it would not exist in creation. And turning stone into gold is possible by Allah's transformation, and the people of alchemy do that, and there is no proof of its impossibility. Similarly, killing the dead is possible by Allah creating life in him after his death, and that has occurred more than once. [4] His statement: 'immediately' means before death, so it includes the absolute and the time-bound, and we have mentioned the difference between them. [5] His statement: 'due to customary incapacity' is the cause for immediate breaking. The conclusion: The oath is concluded due to the inherent possibility of the sworn matter, and he becomes a breaker due to customary incapacity, contrary to the issue of the pitcher, because...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with oaths (al-ayman) on acts that are customarily impossible, such as ascending to the sky, turning a stone into gold, or killing a dead person. The author first presents the base ruling: the oath is valid (mun'aqid) because the sworn act is conceivable (tasawwur al-birr), and the swearer becomes a breaker (hanith) immediately due to incapacity (al-ajz). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that in time-bound oaths, breach occurs only after the time passes; in non-time-bound oaths, breach is immediate. The text then expands on this with commentary. It cites al-Karkhi who says that if someone swears to do what he cannot do, there is no sin, meaning the oath is not sinful but still valid in terms of conclusion. The key issue is the disagreement with Zufar, who holds that such oaths are not concluded because the act is customarily impossible. The Hanafi response, articulated in the passage, distinguishes between inherent possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and customary possibility. Since these acts are possible in themselves—angels ascend, prophets ascended, alchemists turn substances into gold, and resurrection is possible—the oath is valid. The swearer breaks the oath due to customary incapacity, which triggers expiation (kaffarah). The commentary also compares this to the case of the old man who must pay fidyah for not fasting, establishing that obligation can exist without ability when a substitute (khalaf) is available. The passage concludes by reinforcing that the basis of oath validity is inherent possibility, not customary possibility.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): The oath sworn by the speaker to do or not do something.
البرّ (al-birr): Fulfilling the sworn act; the action that the swearer promises to do.
الحنث (al-hanth): The breach of an oath by failing to perform the sworn act or by doing the forbidden act.
الانعقاد (al-in'iqad): The legal validity and binding nature of the oath, making it subject to rulings of expiation upon breach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the conclusion of an oath on a customarily impossible act?
A) Customary possibility
B) Inherent possibility
C) The swearer's intention
D) The presence of a substitute

Key Takeaway
An oath on an inherently possible act is valid even if customarily impossible, and the swearer becomes a breaker immediately, requiring expiation.

Oath to Kill a Deceased Person: Essential vs. Customary Possibility
Expands the ruling on oaths when the subject is dead

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential possibility (imkan dhati) and customary possibility (imkan adi) in Hanafi oath jurisprudence, and apply this distinction to determine when an oath takes effect and when immediate breaking occurs.

Core Idea
An oath is valid based on essential possibility, even if customarily impossible; but immediate breaking is ruled due to customary inability.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on a ruling from al-Wiqaya regarding oaths on acts that seem impossible. The author discusses the dispute with Zufar within the Hanafi school. The key is understanding that oath validity depends on the act's possibility in essence, not its customary feasibility. This distinction has practical consequences for expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the ruling when the swearer knew of the death versus when he did not know. The text states that if he did not know, the oath does not take effect; if he knew, it does take effect but he breaks immediately.
Knowledge changes the intention: knowing implies he intends killing after resurrection (possible in essence), while ignorance implies he intends conventional killing (impossible even in essence).
2
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that since killing a dead person is customarily impossible (like ascending the sky or turning a stone into gold), the oath should not take effect at all, similar to the 'jug case' where the sworn matter is impossible in essence.
Zufar equates customary impossibility with essential impossibility for oath validity.
3
Grasp the Hanafi response
The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) reply that oath validity is based on essential possibility (imkan dhati), not customary possibility. The act is possible in essence because Allah can resurrect the dead; therefore the oath is valid.
Examples: angels ascend, prophets ascended, alchemy can turn stone to gold – these show essential possibility.
4
Apply the distinction to the 'jug case' analogy
The 'jug case' (mas'alat al-kuz) involves an oath to drink water from a specific jug; if the jug is empty and even if Allah creates water, it is not the same water, so the oath never becomes fulfillable. Here, killing after resurrection is a different act than killing a dead corpse, so the analogy is rejected.
In the jug case, the sworn matter is impossible in essence (the specific water cannot exist), whereas here the sworn matter (killing after life) is possible in essence.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Because the oath is valid but fulfillment is customarily impossible, the swearer breaks the oath immediately (before death) and must pay expiation (kaffarah). Zufar's view would require no expiation.
Immediate breaking applies whether the oath is absolute or restricted (e.g., 'by the end of the year').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
