[4] [1] قوله: وإن لم يعلم؛ أي وإن لم يعلم موت فلان قبل حلفه، فقال: لأقتلن وقد كان مات قبله لا ينعقد اليمين، ولا تجب الكفارة. [2]قوله: مستحيلاً عادة؛ فإن صعود السماء وتقليب الحجر ذهباً وقتل الميت مستحيل عادة، فكان كالمستحيل حقيقة، فلا تنعقد اليمين، كما لم تنعقد في مسألة الكوز. والجواب عنه: إن مبنى انعقاد اليمين على إمكان المحلوف عليه ذاتاً، لا على إمكانه عادة. [3] قوله: ممكنة؛ أي في ذاتها غير ممتنعة في ذاتها؛ فإن الملائكة تصعد السماء في كل يوم، وقد صعد عليه سيدنا عيسى الكلية، وسيدنا إدريس العلة، ونبينا ليلة المعراج، كما ذكر الله في كتابه، ولولا إمكانه في نفسه لم يوجد في مخلوق. وتقليب الحجر ذهباً ممكن بتحويل الله، وأهل صنعة الكيميا يفعلون ذلك، ولا دليل على عدم إمكانه. وكذا قتل الميت ممكناً بأن يخلق الله له فيه الحياة بعد موته، وقد وقع ذلك غير مرة. [4] قوله: في الحال؛ المراد به ما قبل الموت، فيدخل فيه المطلق والمقيد، وقد ذكرنا الفرق بينهما. [0] قوله: للعجز عادة؛ علّة للحنث في الحال، والحاصل: إنه تنعقد اليمين لإمكان المحلوف عليه في ذاته ويحنث للعجز العادي، بخلاف مسألة الكوز، فإن المحلوف عليه هناك غير ممكن في ذاته، فلا تنعقد اليمين في بعض الصور، وتنعقد ويحنث في آخر جزء من حياته في بعض الصور على ما مر تفصيله. [1]قوله: لأنه حينئذ؛ أي حينما علم بموته، ومع ذلك حلف بقتله يكون حلفه على قتله بعد الحياة الثانية وهو ممكن ذاتاً، مستحيل عادة، فتنعقد اليمين للإمكان الذاتي، وتجب الكفارة للعجز العادي. [2] قوله: فالمراد القتل المتعارف؛ وهو إنما يكون في الحياة المتعارفة لا في الحياة الحادثة بعد الموت. [3] قوله: فصار كمسألة الكوز؛ تشبيه في عدم الحنث؛ لعدم التصوّر لا في التفصيل بين العالم وبين غيره؛ لأن الأصح عدم التفصيل هنالك، فإن حنت العالم هاهنا لكون البر متصوّراً على ما ذكرنا، أما في الكوز فلو خلق الله له فيه الماء لا يكون عين الماء الذي انعقد عليه اليمين، فلا يتصوّر البر أصلاً. كذا في «شرح الجامع الكبير».
And if he did not know, then no [oath] [1], and there is a disagreement with Zufar: according to him, the oath does not take effect because fulfillment is customarily impossible. We said: These matters are possible in their essence, and this suffices for the oath to take effect, and he breaks it immediately without waiting until the time of death due to customary inability. [4] [1] His saying: 'And if he did not know' means: if he did not know the death of so-and-so before his oath, and he said, 'I will kill him' while he had already died before, the oath does not take effect, and expiation is not obligatory. [2] His saying: 'customarily impossible' – for ascending the sky, turning a stone into gold, and killing a dead person are customarily impossible, so they are like truly impossible, so the oath does not take effect, as it did not take effect in the case of the jug. The answer to it: The basis of the oath taking effect is the possibility of the sworn matter in its essence, not its possibility by custom. [3] His saying: 'possible' means possible in its essence, not impossible in itself; for the angels ascend the sky every day, and our master Jesus ascended, and our master Idris, and our Prophet on the night of the Mi'raj, as Allah mentioned in His book; and if it were not possible in itself, it would not exist in creation. Turning a stone into gold is possible by Allah's transformation, and the people of alchemy do that, and there is no proof of its impossibility. Similarly, killing a dead person is possible by Allah creating life in him after his death, and that has occurred more than once. [4] His saying: 'immediately' means before death, so it includes the absolute and the restricted, and we have mentioned the difference between them. [0] His saying: 'due to customary inability' is the cause for breaking the oath immediately; and the conclusion: the oath takes effect due to the possibility of the sworn matter in its essence, and he breaks due to customary inability, contrary to the case of the jug, for the sworn matter there is not possible in its essence, so the oath does not take effect in some cases, and takes effect and he breaks in the last part of his life in some cases, as detailed before. [1] His saying: 'because then' means when he knew of his death and yet swore to kill him, his oath is to kill him after the second life, which is possible in essence, customarily impossible; so the oath takes effect due to essential possibility, and expiation becomes obligatory due to customary inability. [2] His saying: 'the intended conventional killing' – that is only in conventional life, not in life after death. [3] His saying: 'so it became like the case of the jug' – a comparison in not breaking the oath due to lack of conceptualization, not in the distinction between the one who knows and others; for the correct view is that there is no distinction there; for the breaking of the one who knows here is because fulfillment is conceptualized as we mentioned; as for the jug, even if Allah created water for him, it would not be the same water that the oath was sworn upon, so fulfillment is not conceptualized at all. Thus in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir'.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from al-Wiqaya regarding oaths on impossible acts. The matn states: 'If he did not know [the person's death], then no [oath takes effect].' The commentary explains the ruling and the dispute with Zufar. Zufar held that the oath does not take effect at all because the act (killing a dead person) is customarily impossible. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and others) is that the oath does take effect because the act is possible in essence (Allah can resurrect the dead). The key distinction is between essential possibility (imkan dhati) and customary possibility (imkan adi). The oath is valid based on essential possibility. However, since it is customarily impossible, the oath-taker breaks it immediately (without waiting until death) due to customary inability. If the oath-taker knew the person was dead, he intends killing after resurrection (possible in essence), so the oath takes effect and he breaks immediately. If he did not know, he intends conventional killing (impossible even in essence for a dead body), so no oath takes effect. The commentary also compares to the 'jug case' (mas'alat al-kuz) where the sworn act is impossible in essence.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah, binding the swearer to do or not do something.
البر (al-birr): Fulfillment of the oath by doing the sworn act.
الحنث (al-hinth): Violation of the oath by failing to fulfill it.
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkan al-dhati): Possibility of the act in its essence, not prevented by logical or physical impossibility in itself.
العجز العادي (al-ajz al-adi): Inability to perform the act by normal means, though possible by divine intervention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on an oath to kill a dead person if the swearer did not know of the death?
A) The oath does not take effect, and no expiation is required.
B) The oath takes effect, and the swearer breaks immediately, requiring expiation.
C) The oath takes effect, but the swearer waits until death to see if fulfillment becomes possible.
D) The oath is invalid because the act is customarily impossible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, validity rests on essential possibility, not customary feasibility; an oath on a customarily impossible act is valid but immediately broken, requiring expiation.

Actions Equivalent to Striking in Oaths: Hair Pulling, Strangling, Biting
Ruling that painful actions are like striking in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which actions break an oath not to strike and evaluate the weaker opinion regarding playful context.

Core Idea
Any painful act breaks an oath not to strike, even if not typical hitting.

In Hanafi fiqh, an oath not to strike (darb) is not limited to slapping. This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that hair pulling, strangling, and biting count as striking because they cause pain. However, a weaker opinion considers playful interaction exempt. Understanding this ruling is crucial for determining when an oath is broken and kaffarah becomes due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath condition
The person swore not to strike a woman. The key term is 'strike' (darb).
2
Define what constitutes striking
According to the default Hanafi position, darb is defined by causing pain (ilam), not by a specific motion.
The text states: 'because it is a name for a painful act, and pain has been caused.'
3
Apply the default ruling
Pulling hair, strangling, and biting cause pain, therefore they are considered striking and break the oath.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
A weaker opinion (qil) says if done playfully (mula'aba), it is not called striking but jesting, so the oath is not broken.
The source mentions: 'it is called jesting, not striking.'
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The stronger, relied-upon view is that any intentional infliction of pain counts, regardless of manner. Thus, the oath is broken.
Al-Hidayah presents this as the default ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومد شعرها، وخنقها، وعضها، كضربها [4] قوله: ومد شعرها؛ هذا اختصار لقول صاحب «الهداية»: «ومن حنث لا يضرب امرأة فمد شعرها أو خنقها أو عضها حنث؛ لأنه اسم لفعل مؤلم، وقد تحقق الإيلام، وقيل: لا يحنث في حال الملاعبة؛ لأنه يسمى ممازحة لا ضرباً». (1) انتهى من «الهداية» (5 : 196).
And pulling her hair, strangling her, and biting her are like hitting her. [4] His saying: 'and pulling her hair' – this is an abbreviation of the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'Whoever swears not to strike a woman, then pulls her hair, or strangles her, or bites her, he breaks the oath; because it is a name for a painful act, and pain has been caused. And it is said: He does not break the oath in the case of playful interaction; because it is called jesting, not striking.' (1) End from al-Hidayah (5:196).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on what constitutes 'striking' (darb) in an oath not to strike a woman. The matn states: 'Pulling her hair, strangling her, and biting her are like hitting her.' The commentary from al-Hidayah explains that these acts are considered hitting because the name 'striking' applies to any painful act (fi'l mu'lim). The key element is inflicting pain (ilam). However, there is a weaker opinion (qil) that if done playfully (mula'aba), it is not called striking but jesting, so the oath is not broken. The relied-upon opinion is that any intentional infliction of pain counts, regardless of the manner.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-darb): Any act causing pain, including hitting, pulling hair, strangling, biting.
الإيلام (al-ilam): Causing physical pain.
الملاعبة (al-mula'aba): Playful interaction, jesting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an oath not to strike a woman, which of the following actions breaks the oath according to the default Hanafi ruling?
A) Only hitting with the hand.
B) Any act causing pain, such as pulling hair or strangling.
C) Only actions done in anger, not playfully.
D) Only actions that leave a mark.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'striking' in oaths as any intentional act that causes pain, including hair pulling, strangling, and biting.

Oath on Wearing Clothes Spun by Wife: Ownership Condition
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership condition for vow on wife's spinning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid vow according to the Sahibayn and explain how custom ('urf) affects Abu Hanifa's position in this intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn require ownership at the time of oath; Abu Hanifa accepts future ownership based on custom.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a vow (nadhr) to donate a garment to Mecca if ever worn. The weaver's oath is framed around his wife's spinning. The two leading disciples of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ from their teacher on the condition of ownership at the time of the oath. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A weaver says to his wife: 'If I wear anything from your spinning, it is a hadiy (to be sent to Mecca).' Then he acquires cotton after the oath, she spins it, he wears it.
2
Identify the point of disagreement
Abu Hanifa holds the vow is valid even if cotton was acquired after the oath. The two hold that ownership must exist at the time of the oath for the vow to be valid.
The two are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Custom ('urf) dictates that a wife normally spins from her husband's cotton, so the oath is implicitly 'from my cotton or cotton I will own.'
Thus the vow is interpreted according to common usage.
4
Follow the Sahibayn's reasoning
A vow is only valid on property that is owned at the time of oath or attached to a cause of ownership. The wife's spinning is not a cause of ownership for the husband.
Wearing and spinning do not transfer ownership.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the husband buys cotton after the oath and wears the garment, according to Abu Hanifa he must donate it to Mecca; according to the two, he does not have to.
This is the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل اليمين في لبس الثياب وغير ذلك] وقطن ملكه (۲۱) بعد إن لَبِسْتُ من غزلك فهدي، فغزلَتْهُ ونُسِجَ ولَبِسَ هدي) قطن : مبتدأ، وهدي خبره ، ومعنى الهدي ما يُهْدَى إلى مكة للتصدق ، وعندهما إن كان القطنُ ملكَهُ يوم الحلف، فغزلَتْهُ ونُسِجَ ولَبِسَ يجب أن يُهْدَى إلى مكة، وإن لم يكن القطن ملكه يوم الحلف لا.
[1] قوله : ملكه ؛ الجملة صفة لقوله : «قطن»، وبـه جـاز وقوع النكرة مبتدأ، وقوله : «بعد» متعلق بـ«ملكه»، وحاصله : إنّه قال حائك لزوجته : إن لبست من غزلك ؛ أي مغزولك فهو هدي ، فملك قطناً بعد الحلف بالشراء أو غيره ، فغزلت زوجته ذلك القطن ونسج الحالفُ ذلك الغزل ولبس يجب عليه أن يهديه إلى مكة. قال في «الفتح») : معنى الهدي هاهنا ما يتصدق به بمكة ؛ لأنه اسم لما يهدي إليها ، فإن كان نذر هدي شاةٍ أو بدنة فإنّما يخرجه عن العهدة ذبحه في الحرم والتصدق به هناك، فلا يجزئه إهداء قيمته ، وإن نذر ثوباً جاز التصدّق في مكة بعينه أو بقيمته، ولو نذر إهداء ما لم ينقل كاهداء دار ونحوهما، فهو نذر بقيمتها.
[٢] قوله : ملکه ؛ - بكسر الميم - مصدر بمعنى المملوك، أو هو صيغة ماض. [۳] قوله : لا ؛ وذلك لأنَّ النذر إنما يصح في الملك أو مضافاً إلى سبب الملك ولم يوجد ؛ لأن اللُّبْس وغزل المرأة ليسا من أسباب ملكه ، فلا يصح اليمين في حق القطن المشترى بعد الحلف ؛ ولأنّ غزلَ المرأة عادة يكون من قطن الزوج، والمعتاد هو المراد، فصار كأنه قال : من قطني أو من قطن سأملكه ، والغزل من قطن الزوج، الزوج لما غزلته. كذا في «العناية»(۳).
[Section: Oath regarding wearing clothes and other matters] 'And cotton he owned afterwards saying: If I wear from your spinning then it is a hadiy, then she spun it and it was woven and he wore it, it is a hadiy.' Cotton is the subject, and hadiy is its predicate. The meaning of hadiy is what is sent to Mecca for charity. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if the cotton was his property on the day of the oath, then she spun it and it was woven and he wore it, he must send it to Mecca; and if the cotton was not his property on the day of the oath, then no.
[1] His saying 'he owned': the clause is an adjective for his saying 'cotton', and by it the indefinite subject is allowed. His saying 'after' is connected to 'he owned'. The summary: He, a weaver, said to his wife: If I wear from your spinning, i.e., your spun thread, then it is a hadiy. Then he owned cotton after the oath by purchase or other means. Then his wife spun that cotton and the swearer wove that yarn and wore it; he must send it to Mecca. He said in al-Fath: The meaning of hadiy here is what is given in charity in Mecca, because it is a name for what is sent there. If one vowed a sheep or camel as hadiy, he discharges it by slaughtering in the Haram and giving charity there, so giving its value does not suffice. If he vowed a garment, it is permissible to give charity in Mecca with the garment itself or its value. If he vowed to send something not movable, like a house or similar, then it is a vow of its value.
[2] His saying 'milku': with kasra on meem, a verbal noun meaning owned property, or it is a past tense form.
[3] His saying 'no': because a vow is only valid over property in one's ownership or connected to a cause of ownership, and this is absent. Because wearing and the wife's spinning are not among the causes of his ownership, so the oath is not valid regarding cotton purchased after the oath. And because the wife's spinning is usually from the husband's cotton, and what is customary is what is intended. So it is as if he said: from my cotton or from cotton I will own. And the spinning is from the husband's cotton, the husband for what she spun. Thus in al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath by a weaver concerning clothes made from his wife's spinning. The matn presents the ruling: if a weaver says to his wife, 'If I wear anything made from your spinning, it is a hadiy (to be sent to Mecca),' then later acquires cotton, she spins it, he weaves and wears it, the resulting garment becomes a hadiy that must be sent to Mecca. The commentary explains that this ruling is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) condition that the cotton must have been in the husband's ownership at the time of the oath; otherwise, the oath does not apply. Their reasoning is that a vow is only valid on owned property or property whose acquisition is a cause of ownership. Since the wife's spinning and his wearing are not causes of ownership, the vow cannot apply to cotton acquired after the oath. Furthermore, custom ('urf) is considered—a wife typically spins from her husband's cotton, so the intended meaning is his own cotton. Thus, Abu Hanifa treats the oath as implicitly referring to cotton he will own. The commentary from al-Fath elaborates on the definition of hadiy and the rulings on different types of vows, such as animals vs. garments, and the permissibility of giving value for non-movable items.

Key Terms
هدي (hadiy): Something sent to Mecca as a sacrifice or charity; here it refers to a garment vowed to be given in Mecca.
ملك (milk): Ownership; possessed property. In this passage, it refers to the cotton owned by the oath-maker at the time of the oath.
يمين (yamin): Oath; a solemn declaration that triggers a legal consequence. Here synonymous with nadhr (vow).
عندهما (indahuma): According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the condition for the validity of the weaver's oath according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?
A) The cotton must be owned at the time of the oath.
B) The cotton must be owned after the oath.
C) The oath is always valid regardless of ownership.
D) The wife must own the cotton.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi vow's validity may depend on ownership at the time of oath according to the majority, but Abu Hanifa permits reliance on custom to allow future acquisition.

Jewelry in Oaths: Gold, Silver, and Pearls
Fragmented verse with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what constitutes jewelry for oath purposes and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on unset pearl necklaces.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted by custom: gold rings are jewelry, silver rings are not, and unset pearl necklaces are disputed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the ruling on oaths regarding jewelry. It establishes a general principle based on custom and Shariah, and then presents a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding an unset pearl necklace. Understanding this dispute sheds light on how custom and textual evidence interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule on rings
Read the first sentence: gold ring is jewelry, silver ring is not. Note the exception if the silver ring is made in women's style.
The exception comes from al-Zayla'i.
2
Understand the justification
Why gold but not silver? Because gold rings are prohibited for men and customarily considered jewelry; silver rings are permissible for men and usually not considered jewelry by custom.
This combines shariah and urf.
3
Identify the disputed case
Find the phrase 'and according to the two: an unset pearl necklace is jewelry'. This is the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn.
Unset means not set with gold or silver.
4
Analyze the dispute
Abu Hanifa relies on urf (not customary unless set); Sahibayn rely on reality and the Quranic verse. The fatwa follows them, noting possible change in custom over time.
The tarjih is 'and by that the fatwa is given'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخاتم ذهب حلي لا خاتم فضة، وعندهما : عقد لؤلؤ لم يرصع حلي ، وبه يُفْتَى.
[١] قوله : حَلْي ؛ - بفتح الحاء المهملة وسكون اللام – بالفارسية : زيور، وحاصله : أنه إذا حلف لا يلبس حلياً ، فلبس خاتم فضة لم يحنث، وإن لبس خاتم ذهب حنث، ووجه الفرق أنّ خاتم ذهب حليّ لا خاتم فضة عرفاً وشرعاً ؛ ولذا أبيح استعمال خاتم الفضة للرجال، ولم يبح استعمال خاتم الذهب. كذا في «البناية».
[٢] قوله : لا خاتم فضة؛ إلا إذا كان مصوغا على هيئة خواتم النساء، فحينئذ يحنث. كذا ذكره الزيلعي.
[٣] قوله : وعندهما ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يلبس حلياً، فلبس عقد در غير مرصع لم يحنث عنده ؛ لأنه لا يتحلّى به عرفاً إلا مرصعاً، ومبنى الأيمان على العرف. وعندهما يحنث لأنه حلي حقيقة، حتى سمي به في قوله لا في وصف أهل الجنة : يُحَلَّوْنَ فِيهَا مِنْ أَسَاوِرَ مِن ذَهَبٍ وَلُوْلُوا ) (4) ، وقيل : هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان. كذا في «الهداية».
[٤] قوله : لم يرصع ؛ بصيغة المجهول من الترصيع بمعنى التركيب، احترازاً عما إذا كان مرصعاً بالذهب والفضة، فلأنه يحنث بلبسه اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه حلي عرفاً.
And a gold ring is jewelry, not a silver ring, and according to the two: an unset pearl necklace is jewelry, and by that the fatwa is given.
[1] His saying 'hily': with fatha on ha and sukun on lam, in Persian: zeyvar. The summary: If one swears not to wear jewelry, then wears a silver ring, he does not break the oath; if he wears a gold ring, he breaks it. The reason for the difference is that a gold ring is jewelry by custom and law, not a silver ring; thus the use of a silver ring is permitted for men, while the use of a gold ring is not. Thus in al-Binaya.
[2] His saying 'not a silver ring': except if it is made in the style of women's rings, then he breaks the oath. Thus mentioned by al-Zayla'i.
[3] His saying 'and according to the two...': meaning if one swears not to wear jewelry, then wears an unset string of pearls, he does not break the oath according to him (Abu Hanifa), because it is not adorned with by custom unless set; and oaths are based on custom. And according to the two, he breaks it because it is jewelry in reality, even named as such in His saying describing the people of Paradise: 'They will be adorned therein with bracelets of gold and pearls' (Quran 22:23). And it is said this is a difference of era and time. Thus in al-Hidaya.
[4] His saying 'unset': in the passive voice from tarsee' meaning setting, as a precaution against when it is set with gold and silver, for then he breaks the oath by wearing it by agreement, because it is jewelry by custom.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths regarding jewelry. The matn establishes a basic rule: if someone swears not to wear jewelry, wearing a gold ring constitutes a violation, but wearing a silver ring does not. The reason is that a gold ring is considered jewelry both by custom (urf) and by Islamic law, since silver rings are permissible for men while gold rings are not. An exception is when a silver ring is made in the style of women's rings, in which case it does count as jewelry. Then the passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a pearl necklace that is not set with precious metals. According to Abu Hanifa, such a necklace is not considered jewelry because it is not customarily adorned with unless set. He bases this on the principle that oaths are interpreted according to custom. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is jewelry because the term 'hily' in reality includes pearls, as evidenced by the Quranic verse describing the people of Paradise wearing bracelets of gold and pearls. They hold that even an unset pearl necklace is jewelry. The text notes that some attribute this difference to a change in custom over time. The fatwa (operative ruling) follows the view of the two. Finally, the passage clarifies that if the necklace is set with gold or silver, there is agreement that it is jewelry.

Key Terms
حلي (hily): Jewelry; adornment. In this passage, it determines what counts for oath purposes.
عرف (urf): Custom; common practice. Used as the basis for interpreting oaths.
لم يرصع (lam yurassa'): Not set (with gold or silver); an unset pearl necklace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi), if a man swears not to wear jewelry and wears a silver ring made in the style of women's rings, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because it is considered jewelry.
B) No, because silver rings are not jewelry.
C) It depends on the era.
D) Only according to the two.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths about jewelry are interpreted by custom: gold rings are jewelry, silver rings are not (unless styled like women's), and unset pearl necklaces are considered jewelry according to the mufta bihi.

Oath on Sleeping on a Bed or Sitting on the Ground: The Role of Custom (Urf) in Dependent Items
Rulings on what counts as breaking an oath regarding bedding and seating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tabi' (dependency) and custom (urf) to determine whether an oath is broken when an intermediate item is used.

Core Idea
When custom considers an item part of the main object, its use breaks the oath; independent items do not.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rulings on oaths (yamīn) regarding specific beds or the ground. The key challenge is determining whether using an item that sits on the sworn object counts as using the object itself. The Hanafi school relies on custom (urf) and the principle of dependency (tabaʿiyya) to draw clear lines. You will learn how a thin rug (qiram) on a bed is considered part of the bed, while a separate bed on top is not; and how clothing is part of the person, while a mat on the ground is an independent barrier.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sworn object and the prohibited action
Determine exactly what the oath prohibits: e.g., sleeping on this particular bed, sitting on the ground, etc.
The oath wording is crucial.
2
Check the intermediary
Is there an object placed between the person and the sworn object? What is its nature?
3
Apply the principle of tabi' (dependency)
If the intermediary is customarily considered a dependent part of the sworn object or of the person's body, then using it counts as using the main object. If it is independent, it does not.
4
Consider analogous cases
Review parallels in the text, such as sleeping on a rug (qiram) on a bed vs. a separate bed, or sitting on a mat on the ground vs. clothing intervening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حلف لا ينام على هذا الفراش
فنام على قرام فوقه حنث، لا من جعل فوقه فراشاً آخر، أو حلف لا يجلس على الأرض، فجلس على بساط ، أو حصير فوقه، ولو حال بينه وبينها لباسه حنث ،

[0] قوله : لا ينام ; وكذا لا يعقد ، ولا يضطجع، ونحو ذلك.
[٦] قوله : على هذا الفراش ; بكسر الفاء - ما يفرش على الأرض وغيرها
[1] قوله : على قرام ; - بكسر القاف - بالفارسية بردة باريك وبرؤه منقش.
[2] قوله : لا الفراش الآخر; فإنّ الشيء لا يتبع مثله ، فالنائم على فراش لا يعدّ عرفاً نائماً على فراش آخر ، والنائم على قرام فوق الفراش يعد نائماً عليه عرفاً.
[3] قوله : أو حلف...الخ ; يعني إذا حلف لا يجلس على الأرض لا يحنث بالجلوس على بساط أو حصير فوق الأرض ; لأنه لا يعد جلوساً على الأرض عرفاً، ومثله : إذا جلس على خشب أو جلد وغير ذلك مما يحول بينه وبين الأرض، ولو حلف لا يمشي على الأرض فمشى عليها بنعل أو خف حنث، وإن مشى على بساط الأرض لا يحنث. كذا في «البحر».
[4] قوله : ولباسه تبع ; فلا يعد مثل ذلك حائلاً بين الجالس وبين الأرض.
[0] قوله : كمن حلف ... الخ ; يعني يحنث بالجلوس على البساط المبسوط على السرير في حلفه بقوله : لا يجلس على هذا السرير ; لأنَّ الجلوس على البساط جلوس على السرير عرفاً، بخلاف ما لو حلف لا ينام على ألواح هذا السرير أو ألواح هذه السفينة ففرش عليها فراشاً، فإنّه لا يحنث ; لأنه لم ينم على الألواح ، كما لا يحنث على سرير آخر فوق ذلك السرير.
And whoever swears not to sleep on this bed, and sleeps on a rug (qiram) over it, he breaks his oath; not he who places another bed over it. Or if he swears not to sit on the ground, and sits on a mat or a straw mat over it; and if his clothing intervenes between him and it, he breaks his oath. [0] He said: 'not to sleep' - also not to bend, not to lie down, etc. [6] He said: 'on this bed' - with kasra on the fa - what is spread on the ground and other things. [1] He said: 'on a rug' - with kasr on the qaf - in Persian: a thin embroidered cloth. [2] He said: 'not the other bed' - for a thing is not followed by its like; one who sleeps on a bed is not customarily considered sleeping on another bed, while one who sleeps on a rug over a bed is customarily considered sleeping on it. [3] He said: 'or if he swears...' meaning if he swears not to sit on the ground, he does not break his oath by sitting on a mat or straw mat over the ground, because it is not considered sitting on the ground customarily. Similarly, if he sits on wood, leather, or anything else that separates him from the ground. If he swears not to walk on the ground and walks on it with sandals or slippers, he breaks his oath; but if he walks on a mat on the ground, he does not break his oath. Thus in al-Bahr. [4] He said: 'and his clothing is dependent' - so such is not considered an obstacle between the sitter and the ground. [0] He said: 'like one who swears...' meaning he breaks his oath by sitting on a mat spread over a bed when he swears not to sit on this bed, because sitting on the mat is customarily sitting on the bed. Contrary to if he swears not to sleep on the planks of this bed or the planks of this ship, and he spreads a bed over them, he does not break his oath, because he did not sleep on the planks, just as he does not break his oath by another bed over that bed.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on oaths related to sleeping on a specific bed or sitting on the ground. The commentary explains that the determination of whether an oath is broken is based on custom (urf), not literal meanings. For example, if one swears not to sleep on a particular bed and sleeps on a rug (qiram) that is placed on top of that bed, custom considers that as sleeping on the bed, so the oath is broken. However, if one places a completely different bed on top of the original bed, that is not considered as sleeping on the original bed customarily. Similarly, if one swears not to sit on the ground, sitting on a mat or straw mat that is on the ground does not break the oath because the mat is an intermediary that customarily prevents direct contact. However, if one's own clothing (like trousers) is between them and the ground, that does not prevent the oath from being broken because clothing is considered part of the person. Finally, if one swears not to sit on a specific bed and sits on a mat that is spread on that bed, the oath is broken because customarily sitting on the mat is considered sitting on the bed. However, if the oath is about the planks of a bed, then placing a mattress on the planks does not break the oath because one is not directly on the planks.

Key Terms
قرام (qiram): A thin, decorative rug or cloth spread over a bed, considered part of the bed customarily.
تابع (tabi'): A dependent item that is customarily considered part of the main item, such as clothing on the body or a rug on a bed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears not to sit on the ground and sits on a thick leather cushion placed directly on the ground, do they break the oath?
A) Yes, because the cushion is a dependent item like clothing.
B) No, because leather is a separate barrier similar to wood or a mat.
C) Yes, because any seating on the ground is covered by the oath.
D) No, because leather is not considered a barrier.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) determines whether an intermediate item is dependent (tabi') and thus counts as using the sworn object, or is independent and thus does not break the oath.

Oaths on Sitting: The Role of Custom (Urf) in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on rulings for oaths involving sitting surfaces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of custom (urf) to determine when an oath regarding sitting or sleeping on a surface is broken, and explain the distinction between subordinate and independent intervening layers.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) determines the legal relationship between a person and a surface in oaths: subordinate items are treated as part of the surface, independent items are not.

This lesson explores detailed Hanafi rulings on oaths involving sitting or sleeping on surfaces like the ground or a bed. The key is understanding how custom (urf) defines the relationship between the person and the surface, and whether an intervening item (rug, mat, qiram, another bed) is considered part of the surface or an independent layer. These rulings arise from the principle that in oaths, the customary understanding prevails over literal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath Scenario
Determine what the person swore regarding (e.g., not to sit on the ground, not to sit on a specific bed, not to sleep on a bed). Also note the action taken (e.g., sitting on a rug, sleeping on a qiram, placing another mattress).
The specific wording of the oath matters—e.g., 'ground' vs 'this bed'.
2
Classify the Intervening Item
Assess whether the item (rug, mat, qiram, another mattress) is customarily considered part of the surface (subordinate) or a separate entity. This depends on urf: e.g., a qiram on a bed is subordinate to the bed; a separate mattress placed on top is independent.
The Hanafi school uses the criterion: 'a thing does not follow its like'—two mattresses are independent, but a qiram is subordinate.
3
Apply the Ruling Based on Custom
If the item is subordinate, the action is considered as if directly on the surface (oath broken). If independent, the action is not on the surface (oath not broken). Also consider the role of clothing: if clothing touches the ground, it is subordinate, so the person is sitting on the ground.
This is based on the principle that custom (urf) governs the interpretation of actions in oaths.
4
Conclude Whether the Oath is Broken
State clearly whether hanath (breaking the oath) occurs, requiring kaffarah (expiation). If the oath is not broken, no expiation is needed.
The rulings here are from Zahir al-Riwayah and are the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

So he slept on a qiram (thin rug) above it, he breaks his oath. Not if he placed another mattress above it. Or if he swore not to sit on the ground, and he sat on a rug or a mat above it, and if his clothing came between him and it, he breaks his oath.

[2] Like one who swore not to sit on this bed, and he sat on a rug above it, so he slept on a qiram above it, he breaks his oath. Not if he placed another mattress above it, because the qiram is a subordinate to the mattress, not another mattress. Or if he swore not to sit on the ground, and he sat on a rug or a mat above it, where he does not break his oath because he did not sit on the ground. And if his clothing came between him and it, he breaks his oath because he sat on the ground and his clothing is subordinate to him. Like one who swore not to sit on this bed, and he sat on a rug above it for sitting, and similarly on this rug or this mat.

[1] His statement: 'on a qiram' - with kasra on the qaf - in Persian means a thin embroidered cloth.
[2] His statement: 'not another mattress' - because a thing does not follow its like. So the sleeper on a mattress is not customarily considered sleeping on another mattress, and the sleeper on a qiram above a mattress is customarily considered sleeping on it.
[3] His statement: 'Or if he swore...' etc.; meaning if he swore not to sit on the ground, he does not break his oath by sitting on a rug or mat above the ground because it is not considered sitting on the ground customarily. Similarly, if he sits on wood or leather or other things that intervene between him and the ground. And if he swore not to walk on the ground and he walked on it with sandals or khuffs, he breaks his oath, but if he walked on a rug on the ground, he does not break his oath. Thus in 'al-Bahr'.
[4] His statement: 'and his clothing is subordinate' - so such a thing is not considered an intervening barrier between the sitter and the ground.
[0] His statement: 'Like one who swore...' etc.; meaning he breaks his oath by sitting on a rug spread on the bed in his oath saying 'I will not sit on this bed' because sitting on the rug is sitting on the bed customarily, contrary to if he swore not to sleep on the planks of this bed or the planks of this ship and then spread a mattress on them, then he does not break his oath because he did not sleep on the planks, just as he does not break his oath on another bed above that bed.

104
'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah
Contrary to his sitting on another bed above it, for sitting on another bed is not sitting on that bed. And he does not do it (the oath of abandonment) extends forever, and doing it (the oath of action) is done once. Because sitting on this bed is not customary without placing a rug on it, so sitting on the rug is sitting on the bed. (Contrary to his sitting on another bed above it, for the act of sitting on another bed is not sitting on that bed. And he does not do it extends forever, and doing it is done once.)

[11] His statement: 'this bed...' etc.; and if he said 'bed' absolutely without specifying, he breaks his oath by sitting on a bed above another bed as well.
[2] His statement: 'extends forever'; it is said in detailing the situation: the oath regarding doing or not doing an action - like speaking, eating, traveling, etc., and its absence - is either limited by a time like a day or month, or absolute. If it is the second, it is mentioned in the matn; if it is regarding abandonment, he abandons it forever; if it is regarding action, he fulfills it by doing it once in any manner, whether forgetfully or deliberately, willingly or under compulsion, or by deputization; because the action includes its source (masdar) like the inclusion of the whole in the part, and it is indefinite due to no need for definition, and the indefinite in the context of negation is general, thus necessitating general prohibition; and in affirmation it is specific. So if he does it in the case of negation, he breaks his oath; if he does it in the case of affirmation once, he fulfills his oath. He only breaks his oath by the occurrence of despair (i.e., impossibility) of it, which is by the death of the swearer or the loss of the subject of the action. And if it is the first (time-bound), it is not mentioned in the matn, so he does not break his oath before the lapse of time, even if despair occurs by his death or loss of the subject; because time prevents dissolution; for if the subject were lost before the lapse of time, there would be no benefit in the temporal restriction. Thus in 'Kashf al-Wiqayah'.
(3) His statement: 'forever' - with two fatha - meaning perpetual future, so whenever he does it he breaks his oath, and if he intended a day or two days or the like, he is not believed religiously either. Thus in 'al-Dhakhirah'.
(1) Meaning if he says: "By Allah, I will not do such and such," it is necessary that he not do it forever, because it is in effect an indefinite in the context of negation; and doing it is done once because it is an indefinite in the context of affirmation. See 'Durar al-Hukkam' (2:54).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on oaths related to sitting or lying on surfaces. The author explains that when a person swears not to sit on the ground, and sits on a rug or mat placed on the ground, the oath is not broken because customarily one is not considered to be sitting directly on the ground. However, if one's clothing touches the ground (like a loose garment), it is considered sitting on the ground because the clothing is subordinate to the person. Similarly, if one swears not to sit on a specific bed and sits on a rug placed on that bed, the oath is broken because customarily sitting on the rug is equivalent to sitting on the bed. But if one places another bed on top of that bed, sitting on the upper bed does not break the oath because it is a separate bed. The commentary also discusses the principle that an oath to refrain from an action extends forever (the indefinite in negation gives generality), while an oath to perform an action is fulfilled by doing it once (indefinite in affirmation gives specificity). The proofs are based on custom (urf) and the linguistic principles of general and particular. The Hanafi position is that these rulings are based on customary understanding, not literal interpretation alone.

Key Terms
قرام (qiram): A thin, embroidered cloth or rug spread over a mattress or bed.
حنث (hanath): The act of breaking one's oath; incurring sin and requiring expiation.
عرف (urf): Customary practice that determines the common understanding of actions in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not sit on the ground' and then sits on a rug placed on the ground, according to the Hanafi school, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he is sitting on the ground.
B) No, because customarily sitting on a rug is not considered sitting directly on the ground.
C) Yes, only if the rug is very thin.
D) No, because rugs are considered part of the ground.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) overrides literal interpretation in Hanafi oath rulings; the key is whether the intervening item is customarily considered part of the surface or a separate entity.

Oath Duration: Perpetual Abstention vs. One-Time Fulfillment
Explanation of linguistic principle behind oath duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of an oath of abstention versus an oath to perform, and identify the usul principle that supports this distinction.

Core Idea
An oath to abstain is perpetual; an oath to perform is fulfilled once — indefinite negation generalises, affirmation particularises.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on oaths: when someone swears not to do something, the obligation is permanent unless a time limit is specified. Conversely, swearing to do something is satisfied by a single instance. The proof hinges on a fundamental usul principle: the indefinite noun in negation indicates generality (عموم), while in affirmation it indicates particularity (خصوص). We will explore the ruling, its proof, and a refinement offered by later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
There are two scenarios: swearing not to do something (ترك) and swearing to do something (فعل). For untimed oaths: abstention is forever; action is once.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the usul principle: النكرة في سياق النفي تعم وفي الإثبات تخص. This is an aqli proof derived from Arabic linguistics.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The indefinite verbal noun (e.g., 'speaking') in negation becomes general, covering all times; in affirmation, it is particular, satisfied by one occurrence.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi refinement
Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq critiques the formulation that indefinites in negation are general over times; instead, the generality applies to individuals of the action, but since negation is absolute, it covers all times. The ruling is unchanged.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
If the swearer does the act once (in abstention oath) or fails to do it at all (in action oath after one time), the oath is broken. For timed oaths, the restriction applies only within the specified period.

[11] His statement: this bed etc.; and if he specified the bed generally and did not particularize, he breaks the oath by sitting on a bed above another bed as well. [2] His statement: applies forever; it was said in the detailing of the situation: indeed the oath on doing something or leaving it, such as speaking, eating, traveling and the like and their non-existence, is either limited by a time like a day or a month, or absolute. If it is the second, it is mentioned in the text: and if it is on leaving, he leaves it forever; and if it is on doing, he fulfills by doing it once in any manner, whether forgetfully or intentionally, willingly or coerced, or by deputization; because the action includes its verbal noun like the inclusion of the whole in the part, and it is indefinite due to no need for definition, and the indefinite in the context of negation is general, so it necessitates generality of abstention; and in affirmation it is particularized, so if he does it in the form of negation he breaks the oath; and if he does it in the form of affirmation once, he fulfills; and he only breaks the oath by the occurrence of despair of it, which is by the death of the swearer or by the loss of the locus of the action. And if it is the first, it is not mentioned in the text, so he does not break the oath before the passing of the time, even if despair occurs by his death or loss of the locus; because the time prevents dissolution; since if dissolution occurred before passing of the time, the restriction would have no benefit. Thus in 'Kashf al-Wiqayah'. (3) His statement: forever, with two fathas, meaning perpetual continuation, so if he does it at any time he breaks the oath, even if he intended a day or two days or the like, he is not believed religiously either. Thus in 'al-Dhakhirah'. (1) That is, if he says: By Allah I will not do such-and-such, he must not do it forever; because it is effectively an indefinite in the context of negation; and his doing it applies once because it is an indefinite in the context of affirmation. See: 'Durar al-Hukkam' (2:54). Know that his statement: 'he does not do this' in custom is the negation of his statement 'he does it', and his statement 'he does it' applies to once, so his statement 'he does not do it' is forever. [1] His statement: applies to once; because the action requires an indefinite verbal noun, so his statement 'I will not speak to Zaid' for example means no speech occurs from him with him; and the indefinite in affirmation is particularized, so he fulfills by doing it once; and since the indefinite is general in the context of negation, his statement 'he does not do it' becomes applying forever. Thus in 'Ghayat al-Bayan'. And it is objected to that: indeed the generality of the indefinite under negation is only with respect to its individuals, not with respect to times; so it is better to say: 'he does not do it' is a negation of that action absolutely, without restriction to anything and not anything else, so abstention is general due to the necessity of the generality of negation. Thus in 'al-Bahr'.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the legal effect of oaths: when someone swears not to do something, the obligation is perpetual; when they swear to do something, it is fulfilled by doing it once. The text begins with an example: if one swears not to sit on a particular bed but sits on another bed resting on it, that is not considered sitting on the bed in question—unless a mat is placed on the bed, in which case sitting on the mat counts as sitting on the bed. The core of the ruling is then stated: 'He does not do it applies forever, and he does it applies once.' The commentary elaborates by distinguishing between timed and untimed oaths. For an untimed oath to refrain, the swearer must refrain forever; for an untimed oath to do something, doing it once suffices, regardless of intent, compulsion, or deputization. The proof is based on Arabic grammar: the indefinite noun in the context of negation indicates generality (so abstention is permanent), while the indefinite noun in the context of affirmation indicates particularity (so one instance fulfills). The text also notes that if the oath is time-bound, it only applies within that period; moreover, if the swearer dies or the action becomes impossible before the time ends, the oath is not broken until the time expires. A later clarification explains that 'forever' means perpetual continuation; if the swearer intended a limited time, that is not accepted even religiously. The passage concludes with a quote from 'Ghayat al-Bayan' reiterating the linguistic principle, followed by a critique from 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' which points out that the generality of the indefinite in negation applies to individuals of the action type, not to times; a more precise formulation is that the negation is absolute, covering all times naturally. This critique does not change the ruling but refines the proof. The ruling itself is settled: an oath to abstain is perpetual (unless specified otherwise), and an oath to perform is fulfilled by one occurrence.

Key Terms
اَلنَّكِرَة فِي سِيَاق النَّفْي (al-nakirah fī siyāq al-nafy): An indefinite noun that appears within a negative construction, which according to Hanafi usul indicates generality (ʿumūm) of all individuals and times.
اَلنَّكِرَة فِي سِيَاق الْإِثْبَات (al-nakirah fī siyāq al-ithbāt): An indefinite noun in an affirmative statement, which Hanafi usul holds indicates particularity (khuṣūṣ) and is fulfilled by one instance.
أَبَد (abad): Perpetual future duration; in oaths it means forever, with no time limit, and if the swearer intends a limited time, that is not accepted.
مَرَّة (marrah): One time; the oath to do something is fulfilled by doing it once, regardless of circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the legal effect of an oath such as 'By Allah, I will not speak to Zaid' without any time limit?
A) The swearer must refrain from speaking to Zaid forever.
B) The swearer must refrain for one day only.
C) The oath is fulfilled if the swearer speaks to Zaid once.
D) The oath is void because it is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The indefinite noun in negation (e.g., لا أفعل) makes an oath of abstention perpetual; in affirmation (e.g., لأفعلن) it makes the oath fulfilled by one occurrence.

Scope of Negative Oaths: One Act vs. Forever
Ruling on the duration of negative oaths based on linguistic principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of negative oaths and explain the linguistic principle (nakirah in negation) that supports it.

Core Idea
A negative oath (e.g., 'I will not do X') binds forever because an indefinite noun in negation applies to all instances.

In everyday language, if someone says 'I will not eat that fruit', do they mean just once or forever? The Hanafi school, based on a deep linguistic principle, rules that such a negative oath applies to all future occurrences unless the speaker explicitly limits it. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and a refinement from later Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a negative oath (e.g., 'لا أفعله'). The ruling: it applies forever (الأبد).
2
Locate the proof
The proof is linguistic: the verb 'يفعل' implies an indefinite verbal noun (مصدر منكر). In positive statements, an indefinite noun specifies one instance; in negation, it becomes general (تعم).
This is from Ghayat al-Bayan.
3
Analyze the objection
An objection is raised: the generality of the indefinite under negation applies to its individuals (different types of the action), not to times. So the better formulation is that the negative oath is an absolute negation of the action, which necessarily implies perpetual prohibition.
From al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
4
Confirm the operative position
Despite the refinement in reasoning, the ruling remains the same: the negative oath binds forever unless a specific duration was intended by the oath-taker.
5
Identify the linguistic rule
The key principle: النكرة في الإثبات تخص وفي النفي تعم (the indefinite in affirmation specifies, in negation generalizes). This is a Hanafi usul principle applied to oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اعلم أن قوله : لا يفعل هذا في العرف سلب لقوله : يفعلُهُ، وقوله: يفعله واقع على مرَّة، فقولُهُ: لا يفعله يكون للأبد.
[1]قوله : واقع على مرّة ؛ لأنَّ الفعل يقتضي مصدراً منكراً، فقوله : لا أكلم زيداً مثلاً معناه لا يصدر منه معه . كلام، والنكرة في الإثبات تخص فيبر بفعله مرّة ، ولَمَّا كانت النكرة تعمّ في موضع النفي صار قوله : لا يفعله واقعاً على الأبد. كذا في «غاية البيان».
ويَرِدُ عليه : إن عموم النكرة تحت النفي إنّما يكون بالنسبة إلى أفرادها لا بالنسبة إلى الأزمان، فالأحسن أن يقال : لا يفعله نفي لذلك الفعل مطلقاً، من دون تقييد بشيء دون شيء، فيعم الامتناع عنه ضرورة عموم النفي. كذا في «البحر»
(1)
(۱) «البحر الرائق» (٤ : ٤٠٠).
Chapter of Oaths: Oaths regarding clothing, Hajj, prayer, and fasting. Know that his statement: 'he does not do this' in custom is a negation of his statement: 'he does it', and his statement: 'he does it' applies to one time, so his statement: 'he does not do it' applies forever. [1] His statement: 'applies to one time' because the verb necessitates an indefinite verbal noun, so his statement: 'I do not speak to Zayd' for example means that no speech occurs from him with him. And the indefinite in affirmation specifies, so it is fulfilled by doing it once. And since the indefinite is general in the context of negation, his statement: 'he does not do it' becomes applicable forever. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And it is objected to him: The generality of the indefinite under negation is only with respect to its individuals, not with respect to times. So better to say: 'He does not do it' is a negation of that action absolutely, without restriction to anything in particular, so it necessitates prohibition of it necessarily due to the generality of the negation. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a hashiya on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addresses the scope of negative oaths. The author states the primary rule: in customary usage, saying 'he does not do this' negates the meaning of 'he does it', and 'he does it' implies a single occurrence. Therefore, a negative oath like 'I will not speak to Zayd' is understood as forever. The commentator explains the logic: the verb implies an indefinite verbal noun; in positive statements, an indefinite noun specifies a single instance, but in negative contexts, the indefinite becomes general, thus encompassing all times. This is cited from Ghayat al-Bayan. Then a critique is presented from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: the generality of the indefinite under negation applies to its individuals (types of action) not to temporal repetitions. A better formulation is that 'he does not do it' is an absolute negation of the action, so prohibition is required for all times. The Hanafi position is that negative oaths are binding for the entire future unless the oath-taker intended a specific time. This ruling is based on linguistic principles (urf in language) and is consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on the general import of indefinite nouns in negation.

Key Terms
النكرة (al-nakirah): An indefinite noun that in positive statements specifies a single instance, but in negative statements applies generally to all instances.
اليمين (al-yameen): An oath, a solemn promise or vow that may be binding to fulfill or expiate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A negative oath in Hanafi fiqh is binding forever because the indefinite noun in negation applies to all future instances of the action.

Vows to Walk to the Haram: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on vow binding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on vows to walk to the Haram and explain the role of custom in resolving the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A vow's binding force depends on whether the phrase is customarily used for undertaking ihram.

Vows (nudhur) are a form of worship that can become obligatory once uttered. However, the Hanafi school splits on whether a vow to walk to the Haram, the Sacred Mosque, or Safa and Marwah creates a binding obligation. Abu Hanifa says no, while the Sahibayn say yes. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the critical role of custom in determining the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow and its phrasing
Read the vow phrases: 'upon me is to walk to the Haram,' 'the Sacred Mosque,' 'Safa and Marwah.' These are the trigger for the dispute.
The vow is to walk, not ride.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that nothing is required for any of these phrases. The vow is not binding because the expression is not customary for undertaking ihram.
Custom ('urf) is key: if people do not typically use such phrases to mean they will perform Hajj/Umrah, the vow is not valid.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that walking to the Haram or the Sacred Mosque requires Hajj/Umrah on foot. However, Safa and Marwah are separate and not binding.
They argue that the Haram includes the Ka'bah, so mentioning it is equivalent to mentioning the Haram itself.
4
Analyze the proofs
For the Sahibayn: the Haram encompasses the Ka'bah and Sacred Mosque, so a vow to walk to them is like a vow to walk to the Haram. For Abu Hanifa: such phrasing is not a customary way to undertake ihram, so the vow is not recognized.
The difference is rooted in whether custom gives these phrases a specific legal meaning.
5
Apply the principle of changing customs
The passage notes that this dispute is a 'difference of era and custom.' This means the ruling may vary if the custom changes over time.
In a society where such phrases are commonly understood as vows for Hajj, the Sahibayn's view might prevail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودم إن ركب، ولا شيء بعلي الخروج، أو الذهاب إلى بيت الله تعالى، أو المشي إلى الحرم، أو المسجد الحرام، أو الصفا والمروة. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عند أبي يوسف ومحمد فيلزمه حج، أو عُمْرة مشياً، أو المسجد الحرام، أو الصفا والمروة. [1] قوله: ودم؛ أي يجب دم إن ركب في جميع الأوقات أو في أكثرها لإدخاله النقص، وقد مر «بحث وجوب الدماء» في «كتاب الحج». [2] قوله: هذا؛ أي عدم وجوب شيء في: علي المشي إلى الحرم، وأما عدم وجوب شيء فيما قبله فاتّفاقي، وكذا في قوله: علي المشي إلى الصفا والمروة. This is according to Abu Hanifa. As for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, Hajj or Umrah walking is required, or [to walk to] the Sacred Mosque or Safa and Marwah. [1] He said: 'And a blood sacrifice' meaning a blood sacrifice is obligatory if he rides at all times or most of them because he introduces deficiency, and the discussion of the obligation of blood sacrifices has passed in the Book of Hajj. [2] He said: 'this' meaning the non-obligation of anything in 'upon me is to walk to the Haram'. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on a person who makes a vow (nadhr) to walk to the Haram, the Sacred Mosque, or Safa and Marwah. The passage first presents the ruling of Abu Hanifa: nothing is obligatory upon the one who says 'upon me is to go out' or 'to go to the House of Allah' or to walk to these places. However, if he says 'upon me is to walk to the Haram' or similar, Abu Hanifa rules that nothing is required, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule that he must perform Hajj or Umrah on foot. The commentary then explains three points: (1) The mention of 'blood sacrifice' in the original text refers to the case where the person rides during the vow, requiring a dam (sacrificial animal) for violating the vow. (2) The phrase 'this' in the commentary indicates that the non-obligation specifically applies to walking to the Haram, while the non-obligation for the previous items (going out, going to the House of Allah) is agreed upon by all. (3) The difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is discussed: the Sahibayn argue that since the Haram includes the Ka'bah and the Sacred Mosque, mentioning them is like mentioning the Haram itself, so the vow to walk to them is equivalent to walking to the Haram. Abu Hanifa counters that undertaking ihram with such a phrase is not customary ('urf), so the vow is not binding. The commentary notes that this difference is considered a difference of era and custom, implying that the ruling may vary with time and place.

Key Terms
المشي إلى الحرم (al-mashy ilā al-ḥaram): A vow to walk to the Haram (the sacred precinct in Mecca).
الاختلاف في العصر والزمان (al-ikhtilāf fī al-‘aṣr wa al-zamān): A difference of ruling based on changing customs over time.

Key Takeaway
The binding force of a vow to walk to the Haram depends on custom: Abu Hanifa rules no obligation, while the Sahibayn rule obligation for the Haram and Sacred Mosque, not for Safa and Marwah.

Freeing a Child Born Alive After Stillbirth: Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on freeing live child after stillbirth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling of freeing a living child after a stillbirth according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the principle of qarinah to similar oath cases.

Core Idea
Context (qarinah) can restrict an oath's condition, so a stillbirth may not dissolve the oath intended for a live birth.

In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted according to their apparent meaning, but context can restrict that meaning. In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: a man tells his slave-girl, 'If you give birth, the child is free.' She delivers a stillborn and then a living child. Does the stillbirth dissolve the oath, freeing only the stillborn (impossible) or does the oath continue to cover the living child? The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hinges on whether the word 'free' provides a contextual indicator (qarinah) that restricts 'birth' to a living child.

Guided Path
1
State the case and the two positions
Clearly describe the scenario: oath to free child if birth occurs. Then state Abu Hanifa's view (living child freed) and the Sahibayn's view (oath dissolved by stillbirth).
2
Identify the root of disagreement
Determine what interpretive principle divides them: whether the condition is absolute birth or birth of a living child, and how the word 'free' acts as a qarinah.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's argument
Follow the reasoning: because a dead child cannot be free, the phrase 'he is free' must refer to a living child, thus restricting the condition. Therefore, the stillbirth does not fulfill the condition, and the oath remains for the second child.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's argument
Understand their view: the oath is unconditional; any birth satisfies it. The stillbirth is a birth, so the oath dissolves; nothing remains to free the second child.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the preferred view
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa the living child is freed, under Sahibayn it is not. The text supports Abu Hanifa, and this is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[3] قوله : وعتق الحي... الخ ; يعني إذا قال: إن ولدت ولداً فذلك الولد حرّ، فولدت ميتاً ثم حيا يعتق الحي، ولا تنحل اليمين بولادة الميت ; لأن قوله : فهو حرّ ; قرينة مقيدة للولادة بولادة الحى ; لعدم إمكان حرية الميت. [4] قوله : انحلت ; لأن اليمين تنحل بوجود الشرط ، وهو هنا مطلق الولادة، وقد وجد في ولادة الميت، فلم يبق لليمين أثر بعد ذلك ليحكم بعتق الولد الثاني. (وفي : ليقضين دينه اليوم، وقضاه زيوفاً، أو نبهرجة، أو مستحقة، أو باعه به قلنا : لم تنحل ; لأنَّ قولَهُ : إن ولدت ; المراد به الحي بقرينة قوله : فهو حرّ ; لأنَّ الميت لا يمكن حريته. وجوابه : أنا لا نُسَلّم أن المراد بالولادة هنا مطلقاً، بل المراد ولادة الحي بقرينة حكم حرية الولد المختص بالحي، فلا تنحل اليمين بولادة الميت بخلاف قوله : إن ولدت فأنت حرّة، فإنه لا قرينة هناك تخص الولادة بولادة الحي. And the freeing of the living child in [the phrase]: 'If you give birth, he is free; if you give birth to a dead child then a living one', this is with Abu Hanifa. But with the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is not freed because the oath is dissolved by the birth of the dead child. [3] His saying: 'And freeing of the living child...' etc.; meaning if he says: 'If you give birth to a child, that child is free', and she gives birth to a dead child then a living one, the living one is free, and the oath is not dissolved by the stillbirth because his saying 'he is free' is a context (qarinah) restricting birth to the birth of a living child, due to the impossibility of freedom for a dead child. [4] His saying: 'dissolved' because the oath is dissolved by the existence of the condition, which here is absolute birth, and it occurred in the stillbirth, so the oath has no effect to rule on the freedom of the second child. (And regarding: 'He shall surely pay his debt today, and he pays it with defective coins, or counterfeit, or claimed by another, or sells it for it' we said: It is not dissolved, because his saying 'If you give birth' means the living child by the context of his saying 'he is free', because a dead child cannot be free. And the answer: We do not concede that the meaning of birth here is absolute; rather, it means the birth of a living child due to the context of the ruling of freedom specific to the living, so the oath is not dissolved by the stillbirth, unlike his saying 'If you give birth, you are free' where there is no context specifying birth to a living child. [1] His saying: 'means the living'; the pronoun 'bi-hi' refers to his saying 'If you give birth', and the added word is omitted, i.e., 'the birth of the living one', or it is said: his pronoun refers to the child understood from his saying 'you gave birth'.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath to free a child born to a slave-girl. If the owner says, 'If you give birth, the child is free,' and she gives birth to a stillborn first and then a living child, what is the ruling? According to Abu Hanifa, the living child is freed because the context of 'he is free' indicates that the sworn condition is the birth of a living child. The oath is not dissolved by the stillbirth because the stillbirth does not fulfill the condition as intended. However, according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the oath is dissolved by the stillbirth because the condition of absolute birth occurred, and nothing remains to effect the freedom of the second child. The text then provides a rebuttal from the Hanafi position: the context of 'free' restricts birth to a living child, so the stillbirth does not dissolve the oath. The discussion contrasts this with a simple divorce/manumission oath (e.g., 'If you give birth, you are free') where no context exists, so stillbirth would fulfill the condition. This highlights the importance of qarinah (context) in interpreting oaths.

Key Terms
قرينة (qarinah): Contextual indicator that specifies the intended meaning of a word, here 'he is free' restricts birth to a living child.
انحلال اليمين (inhilal al-yamin): Dissolution of the oath, meaning it is fulfilled and no longer binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He is freed only if the father intended live birth
D) The oath is invalid

Key Takeaway
The presence of a qarinah can restrict an oath's condition, preventing stillbirth from dissolving the oath.

Fulfilling an Oath with Defective Coins: The Hanafi Ruling
Explaining when paying debt with defective coins fulfills the oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on paying a debt with defective coins and apply the principle of predominant attribute to determine oath fulfillment.

Core Idea
The ruling follows the predominant metal: silver-dominant coins repay the debt; base metals do not.

When a person swears to repay a debt, the method of payment matters. Must he use exactly the same type of coins? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on the actual composition of coins. This lesson examines the passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that clarifies when payment with defective or claimed coins fulfills the oath, and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Payment Methods
List the types of payment mentioned in the text: paying with counterfeit coins (ziyuf), defective coins (nabahraja), claimed coins (mustahaqqa), selling the debt, base metal (suqa), lead, and gift. Each has its own ruling.
Focus on the distinction between silver-dominant and base-dominant coins.
2
Classify Coins by Metal Content
Distinguish coins where silver is predominant (ziyuf, nabahraja) from those where base metal is predominant (suqa, rasas). The classification determines legal effect based on the principle that the ruling follows the majority (al-ghalib).
This classification is analogous to their permissibility in salam and sarf transactions.
3
Apply the Principle of Genus
Coins of the same genus as the debt (silver dirhams) fulfill the oath; coins not of the genus do not. Understand why ziyuf and nabahraja are considered of the same genus despite their adulteration.
The analogy to salam and sarf supports that they are treated as dirhams.
4
Evaluate Special Cases
Examine claimed coins (mustahaqqa): they are real dirhams but subject to another's claim; payment with them is valid if the claimant permits. Also, examine sale of debt: it discharges the debt immediately via set-off (muqassa).
Gift of debt (isqat) does not constitute repayment.
5
Conclude Oath Status
For each payment method, determine whether the swearer is barr (righteous) or hanith (violator), based on whether the debt is truly repaid with something of the same genus.
The ruling is consistent: repayment requires delivering something of the same genus as the debt.

Source Text

وفي : ليقضين دينه اليوم، وقضاه زيوفاً ، أو نبهرجة، أو مستحقة (1) ، أو باعه به (0) وجوابه : أنا لا نُسَلّم أن المراد بالولادة هنا مطلقاً، بل المراد ولادة الحي بقرينة حكم حرية الولد المختص بالحي، فلا تنحل اليمين بولادة الميت بخلاف قوله : إن ولدت فأنت حرّة، فإنه لا قرينة هناك تخص الولادة بولادة الحي. [٢] قوله : في مسائل شتى ; - بفتح الشين المعجمة وتشديد التاء المثناة الفوقية المفتوحة جمع شتيت - أي متفرّقة، وقد جرت عادتهم في آخر الكتاب أو الباب بذكر مسائل متفرّقة متعلقة به، وتسميتهم لها بمسائل متفرقة، أو مسائل شتى. [٣]قوله : ما يرده ; فهي أدون من الزيوف، فإن التجار متسامحون في أخذ ما لا يتسامح فيه بيت المال.
And regarding: 'He will surely pay his debt today, and he paid it with counterfeit coins, or defective coins, or coins claimed by another, or sold it to him (the creditor) for it' - The answer: we do not concede that the intended meaning of birth here is absolute, rather it means the birth of a living child as indicated by the ruling of the child's freedom specific to the living, so the oath is not dissolved by the birth of a dead child, unlike if he said 'If you give birth, you are free' because there is no indication there restricting birth to that of a living child. [1] His saying: 'meant by it the living' - the pronoun in 'it' refers to his saying 'if she gives birth', and the complement is omitted, i.e., the birth of a living child, or it is said: its pronoun refers to the newborn understood from his saying 'she gave birth'. [2] His saying: 'counterfeit' (ziyuf) - with a damma on the zay, plural of zayf like falas and fulus, they are the adulterated ones that traders use, and the treasury rejects them, and the term 'ziyafa' is not Arabic but rather from the usage of jurists, as in 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Fath', and in 'Kulliyat Abi al-Baqa'': 'Al-zayf is the dirham mixed with copper or other such that its quality is lost, so the treasury rejects it, not the traders.' End. [3] His saying: 'or defective' (nabahraja) - with a fatha on the nun and ba, then a sukun on the ba, then a fatha on the ra, then a jim - is a non-Arabic word, originally 'nabahra', which means share: i.e., the share of silver in these dirhams is less; therefore the traders reject them: i.e., the strict ones among them, while the lenient ones accept them. As in 'al-Nahr'. [4] His saying: 'or claimed by another' (mustahaqqa) - on the pattern of the passive participle from istihqaq: i.e., those which a man claimed as his property. [0] His saying: 'or sold it to him' - i.e., for the debt, meaning the debtor who swore sold something for that debt which he swore to repay, and the creditor bought it in exchange for his debt and took possession. Then he said: 'something and its possession makes him righteous, even if it was base metal, or lead, or he gifted it to him, no' - it will come in various issues from the Book of Judgments: indeed al-zayf is what the treasury rejects, and al-nabahraja is what the traders reject, and al-suqa is what has predominant dross. So al-zayf and al-nabahraja are those in which silver is predominant over the adulteration such that they are of the same genus as dirhams, but they are rejected due to adulteration. [1] His saying: 'righteous' (barr) - i.e., he becomes righteous in these scenarios, not a violator. As for the scenario of counterfeit and defective coins: because they are of the same genus as dirhams, since silver is predominant in them over adulteration, and the ruling is for the predominant; that is why if one uses them in salam and sarf transactions, it is permissible. And if they were not of the same genus as dirhams, this would be an exchange (istibdal), and that is not permissible in sarf and salam. This is contrary to base metal and lead, for they are not considered of the same genus as dirhams; that is why using them in sarf and salam transactions is not permissible, so paying with them is not repayment of his debt, and he becomes a violator. As for the scenario of paying with claimed dirhams: because they are real dirhams, and the fact that they are claimed does not prevent repayment, because possession of claimed goods is suspended, such that if the claimant permits it, repayment with them is valid. As for the scenario of sale and similar whereby set-off (muqassa) is achieved: because debts are repaid with their equivalents, and selling the item in exchange for the debt is repayment for him. Mention of possession is incidental in what Muhammad mentioned, because righteousness and repayment of debt occur by mere sale, even if the item perishes before possession, the sale is rescinded and the debt returns. He specified possession so that the debt is established upon the creditor, thus set-off occurs. This is contrary to when the creditor gifts the debt to the debtor, for the oath swearer is not righteous therein, because gift is a remission (isqat), not a set-off, so payment is not found. As in the commentaries of 'al-Jami' al-Kabir'. [2] His saying: 'in various issues' - with fatha on the shin and tashdid on the ta, plural of shatit, i.e., scattered, and their habit is at the end of the book or chapter to mention scattered issues related to it, and they call them scattered issues or various issues. [3] His saying: 'what it rejects' - for it is inferior to counterfeit, because traders are lenient in accepting what the treasury is not lenient in.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, deals with the fulfillment of an oath to repay a debt. The type of passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the conditions under which an oath is considered fulfilled (barr). The author begins by presenting the case: a man swears, 'He will surely pay his debt today,' and then pays with counterfeit coins (ziyuf), defective coins (nabahraja), coins claimed by another (mustahaqqa), or sells something to the creditor in lieu of the debt. The ruling is that in all these cases, the oath is fulfilled—the swearer is barr (righteous) and not hanith (violator). However, if he pays with base metal (suqa) or lead (rasas), or if the creditor gifts the debt to him, the oath is not fulfilled. The proof lies in the principle that the dominant attribute (al-ghalib) determines the ruling. Since the counterfeit and defective coins have silver as the predominant metal, they are considered of the same genus as the debt (which is silver dirhams). This is analogous to their permissibility in salam and sarf transactions. Base metals and lead are not of the same genus, so they do not repay the debt. Coins claimed by another are still real dirhams; repayment is valid contingent on the claimant's permission. Selling the debt to the creditor effects a set-off (muqassa), discharging the debt immediately upon the contract, even before possession. Conversely, gifting the debt is a remission, not a repayment, so the oath is broken. The passage concludes with a definition note: 'al-zayf' is what the treasury rejects (due to quality), while 'al-nabahraja' is what strict traders reject; both have dominant silver but are less pure. 'Al-suqa' is predominantly base metal. This distinction is crucial for the ruling.

Key Terms
زيوف (ziyuf): Plural of zayf: dirhams adulterated with copper or other metals, but with silver still predominant; rejected by the treasury but used by traders.
نبهرجة (nabahraja): Defective dirhams with less silver content; rejected by strict traders but accepted by lenient ones; still considered of the same genus due to predominant silver.
مستحقة (mustahaqqa): Dirhams claimed by another as rightful property; they are real dirhams but possession is contingent on the claimant's permission.
ستوقة (suqa): Base metal (e.g., copper) coins with predominant dross; not considered of the same genus as silver dirhams.
بر (barr): Righteous; refers to the swearer having fulfilled the oath correctly, avoiding sin (hanth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following would fulfill a man's oath to repay his debt of silver dirhams?
A) Paying with lead coins
B) The creditor gifting the debt to the debtor
C) Paying with counterfeit coins that are predominantly silver
D) Paying with copper coins

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines oath fulfillment by the predominant metal in the coin: only coins with dominant silver count as same genus and discharge the debt.
