Paying Debt with Various Coins and Its Effect on Oaths
Commentary explaining when payment with various coin types fulfills an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which types of coin payment fulfill a debt oath according to the Hanafi school and apply the principle of 'al-ghalib' (the dominant element) to determine validity.

Core Idea
Payment with a dirham fulfills a debt oath only if silver is dominant; lead and copper do not count.

When someone swears to pay a debt, what counts as payment? The Hanafi school examines the substance of the currency. This lesson unpacks the commentary on al-Wiqayah, which explains that the ruling depends on the dominant metal in the coin. Understanding this requires distinguishing genuine dirhams from inferior coins and knowing that a gift does not constitute repayment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The passage states that paying a debt with zuyuf and nabarjajah dirhams fulfills an oath, while paying with lead or copper, or gifting the debt, does not. Note that 'fulfills' means the oath-taker is not considered oath-breaking.
The ruling is about the effect on an oath, not the validity of the payment itself.
2
Understand the classification of coins
Coins are classified by the dominant metal. Zuyuf (rejected by treasury) and nabarjajah (rejected by merchants) have dominant silver, so they are from the genus of dirhams. Suqah has dominant alloy, so it is not dirham. Lead and copper are not dirhams at all.
The terms are defined in the passage: zuyuf, nabarjajah, suqah.
3
Apply the principle of 'al-ghalib'
The rule is that the dominant element determines the category. Since silver is dominant in zuyuf and nabarjajah, they are considered dirhams. This is supported by their permissibility in salam and currency exchange (sarf).
The passage uses the analogy of salam and sarf to reinforce the genus classification.
4
Distinguish sale from gift
If the debtor sells an item to the creditor in lieu of the debt, the oath is fulfilled immediately upon the contract (set-off occurs), even before delivery. But if the creditor gifts the debt back to the debtor, that is a waiver (isqat), not repayment, so the oath is not fulfilled.
The mention of 'taking' in the sale case is incidental; fulfillment happens at the contract.
5
Apply to oath context
The key question: Did the debtor repay with something from the genus of what he swore? If yes (dominant silver dirhams), he is barr (fulfilling). If no (lead, copper, or gift), he is hanith (oath-breaking).
This logic protects against manipulating oaths by paying with worthless items.

Source Text

Book of Oaths. The oath concerning clothes, hajj, prayer, and fasting: something and its taking fulfills, even if it was lead, or copper, or he gifted it to him does not fulfill. It will come in miscellaneous issues from the book of Judgments: Indeed, the zuyuf is what the treasury rejects, and the nabarjajah is what merchants reject, and the suqah is what overcomes its impurity. So the zuyuf and nabarjajah are those in which silver is dominant over the alloy so that they are from the type of dirhams, but they are rejected due to the alloy. As for the case of zuyuf and nabarjajah, because they are from the type of dirhams because silver dominates the alloy, and the ruling is for the dominant; therefore if one uses them in salam and currency exchange, it is permissible. If they were not from the type of dirhams, this would be a substitution, and that is not permissible in currency exchange and salam. This is contrary to lead and copper, for they are not considered from the type of dirhams; therefore using them in currency exchange and salam is not permissible, so paying with them is not payment of his debt, and he becomes oath-breaking. He only stated taking for the debt to be established on the creditor, so set-off occurs. This is contrary to when the creditor gifts the debt to the debtor, for the oath-taker is not considered fulfilling in it, because gift is a waiver, not set-off, so payment did not occur. As for lead and copper, they are not from the type of dirhams so using them in currency exchange and salam is not permissible, and as for gift, because there is no set-off. Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses the fulfillment of oaths regarding debt repayment. The author first states the ruling: if one swears to pay a debt and then pays with dirhams, the oath is fulfilled even if the dirhams are of the type called zuyuf or nabarjajah (where silver is dominant over alloy), but not if one pays with lead (ustuqah) or copper (rassas), nor if one simply gifts the debt to the creditor. The commentary explains the reasoning: zuyuf and nabarjajah are considered dirhams because silver is the dominant component, so they count as repayment. Paying with lead or copper does not, because those are not considered currency. Paying with dirhams that are subject to a claim by a third party is valid pending the claimant's permission. If one sells an item to the creditor in lieu of the debt, the oath is fulfilled immediately upon the sale, even before delivery, because the debt is settled by set-off (muqasah). Gifting the debt to the debtor does not constitute repayment because it is a waiver, not a transfer of property. The commentary also clarifies that the mention of 'taking' in the sale scenario is incidental; fulfillment occurs with the contract. The passage then digresses to explain the phrase 'miscellaneous issues' and the hierarchy of coin quality: zuyuf is rejected by the treasury, nabarjajah by merchants, and suqah has more alloy.

Key Terms
الزيف (al-zayf): Counterfeit or deficient dirhams that are rejected by the public treasury due to excess alloy; still considered dirhams because silver is dominant.
النبهرج (al-nabarjaj): Dirhams that are rejected by merchants but accepted by the treasury; better quality than suqah.
السوقة (al-sūqah): Dirhams where the alloy (copper) dominates silver; not considered from the genus of dirhams.
الستوقة (al-ustuqah): Lead; not a type of dirham.
الرصاص (al-raṣāṣ): Copper; also not a type of dirham.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following payments fulfills an oath to repay a debt in dirhams?
A) Paying with lead (ustuqah)
B) Gifting the debt to the debtor
C) Paying with nabarjajah dirhams where silver is dominant
D) Paying with copper (rassas)

Key Takeaway
Payment with zuyuf and nabarjajah dirhams fulfills a debt oath because silver is dominant; lead, copper, and gift do not.

When Does an Oath Not to Receive Debt in Installments Break?
Clarifies when an oath not to receive debt in parts is broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which an oath not to receive debt in installments is broken, and apply the Hanafi parsing rule to distinguish between different oath wordings.

Core Idea
An oath not to receive debt 'dirham by dirham' is only broken when the entire debt is received in installments.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a common oath formulation: 'I will not receive my debt dirham by dirham.' The legal consequence hinges on whether the phrase attaches to the entire debt or a part. Understanding this parse is essential for correctly determining oath violations in daily financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath Wording
Recognize the phrase: 'لا يقبضُ دينه درهماً دون درهم' – literally 'he does not receive his debt dirham after dirham'.
This is a standard phrasing meaning 'in installments'.
2
Parse the Grammatical Structure
The word 'dirham' is in the accusative, and 'dūna' indicates separation. The phrase describes a state: each dirham separate from the next, i.e., one after another.
Compare to 'yadan bi-yad' (hand to hand) which describes simultaneous exchange.
3
Determine the Object of the Oath
The oath uses 'dīnī' (my debt), which refers to the entire debt, not a part. Thus, the condition applies to the whole sum.
If the wording were 'min dīnī' (from my debt), it would apply to any part.
4
Apply the Condition for Breaking the Oath
The oath is broken only when the entire debt is received in separate installments. Receiving only a part does not break the oath.
The condition is receiving the 'whole in parts' (kulluh mutafarriqan).
5
Consider the Exception of Necessary Separation
If the debt is received in two weighings with only the act of weighing in between, this is not considered 'separation' customarily, so the oath is not broken.
This nuance prevents trivial oath-breaking due to weighing delays.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي: لا يقبضُ دينه درهماً دون درهم حنث بقبض كله متفرقاً، لا ببعضه دون باقيه، أو كله بوزنين لم يتخلّلهما إلا عمل الوزن. ولا في : إن كان لي إلا مئة فكذا، ولا يملك إلا خمسين. [1] قوله : درهماً دون درهم؛ أي لا يقبضه حال كون درهم منه مخالفاً لدرهم آخر في كونه غير مقبوض: أي لا يقبضه متفرقاً، بل جملة، فالمجموع في تأويل حال مشتقة، فهو مثل بعته يداً بيد : أي متقابضين، كذا في رد المحتار. [2] قوله : لا ببعضه دون باقيه ؛ أي لا يحنث بمجرد قبض البعض، بل يتوقف حنثه على قبض باقيه، فإذا قبضه حنث ؛ وذلك لأنه أضاف القبض المتفرق إلى كل الدين حيث قال : ديني ؛ وهو اسم للمجموع ، ولا يصدق قبض الكل متفرقاً إلا بعد قبض الكلّ فلا يقبض البعض. حنث بقبض البعض ؛ لأن شرط الحنث هنا قبض البعض فلو قال : من ديني ؛ من الدين متفرقاً، ولو قيد باليوم، فقبض البعض فيه متفرقاً، أو لم يقبض شيئاً لم يحنث ؛ لأن الشرط أخذه متفرقاً ولم يوجد كذا في «الفتح») و«البحر». [3] قوله : أو كله ؛ عطف على قوله : بعضه؛ أي لا يحنث بقبض بتفرق ضروري ؛ لأنه لا يعد تفرّقاً عرفاً إن كان يقبضه كله بوزنين لم يفصل بينهما كله متفرّقاً.
And in: 'He will not receive his debt dirham by dirham' – he breaks the oath only by receiving all of it in parts, not by receiving some without the rest, or all of it in two weighings with nothing separating them except the act of weighing. Nor in: 'If I only have one hundred, then so-and-so', while he only owns fifty. [1] He said: 'dirham by dirham' means he does not receive it while one dirham is different from another in being unaccepted; i.e., he does not receive it in separate parts, but as a lump sum. So the total is in the interpretation of a derived state, like 'I sold it hand to hand', i.e., both taking simultaneously. This is in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] He said: 'not by some without the rest' means he does not break the oath by merely receiving some; rather his breaking the oath depends on receiving the rest. When he receives it, he breaks it because he applied the separate receipt to the entire debt by saying 'my debt', which is a name for the whole. Receiving the whole in parts is not true except after receiving the whole, so receiving some does not break it. He breaks by receiving some because the condition of breaking here is receiving some. So if he said 'from my debt' (part of the debt) in separate parts, and if he restricted it to a day, and he received some of it separately, or did not receive anything, he does not break the oath because the condition is taking it separately and it did not occur. This is in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr. [3] He said: 'or all of it' is conjuncted to his saying 'some of it'. i.e., he does not break by receiving with necessary separation, because it is not considered separation customarily if he receives all of it in two weighings, with nothing separating them, all of it in separate form?
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses an oath in which a person swears: 'I will not receive my debt dirham by dirham' (meaning in installments). The ruling is that this oath is broken only when the entire debt is received in separate installments (i.e., the whole amount is taken in parts). Receiving only a part without the rest does not break the oath. The reasoning is that the phrase 'dirham by dirham' applies to the entire debt, so the condition for breaking is receiving all of it in that manner. If someone swears 'from my debt' (i.e., some of it), then receiving any part in installments does break the oath. The commentary also discusses the scenario of receiving the entire debt in two weighings with only the act of weighing in between; this is not considered a separation customarily, so the oath is not broken. The passage also mentions a related case: if someone swears 'If I have only one hundred, then so-and-so...' while he owns fifty, that is not addressed here.

Key Terms
درهماً دون درهم (dirhaman dūna dirham): An idiom meaning 'one dirham after another' or 'dirham by dirham', i.e., in installments.
دون (dūna): In this context, 'other than' or 'separate from' – meaning each dirham separate from the next.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears, 'I will not receive my debt dirham by dirham.' He receives 20 dirhams of a 100 dirham debt in installments. Has he broken his oath?
A) Yes, because he received some in installments.
B) No, because he did not receive the entire debt.
C) Yes, because dirham by dirham refers to any part.
D) No, because receiving in installments is not forbidden.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to receive debt 'dirham by dirham' is broken only when the entire debt is received in installments, not by partial receipt.

Oath Not to Receive Debt Piecemeal: When Is It Broken?
Ruling on oath-breaking condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath not to receive debt dirham by dirham is broken (hanith) and apply the customary exception for weighings.

Core Idea
An oath not to receive debt piecemeal is broken only when the entire debt is received in separate parts.

If someone swears, 'I will not receive my debt dirham by dirham,' does receiving one dirham break the oath? Or does he need to receive the full debt? This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies that the oath is broken only when the entire debt is taken in separate parts, not with partial receipt. Additionally, receiving the whole amount in two weighings, with no interruption except the act of weighing itself, is not considered 'separate' by custom ('urf). We will walk through the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formulation
The oath is: 'I will not receive my debt dirham by dirham.' The key is the phrase 'dirham by dirham' which implies separate, piecemeal receipt.
The term 'dirhaman duna dirham' means literally 'a dirham without a dirham,' i.e., one dirham separate from another.
2
Determine the condition for hanith (oath-breaking)
Explain that hanith occurs only when the entire debt is received in separate parts. The word 'dayn' (debt) refers to the whole, so receiving only a part does not violate the oath.
If the oath were 'from my debt,' different rules might apply.
3
Explain why partial receipt does not trigger hanith
The oath is attached to the entire debt; receiving a part does not fulfill the condition of receiving the whole separately. Thus, no hanith until the full debt is collected piecemeal.
This is based on linguistic interpretation: 'dayn' denotes the totality.
4
Apply the customary exception (two weighings)
If the entire debt is received in two weighings with no interruption except the act of weighing, custom ('urf) does not consider this 'separate receipt.' Hence, no hanith.
The act of weighing itself is not an interruption; only if other actions intervene would it be considered separate.
5
Recognize the side notes on counterfeit coins
The commentary briefly distinguishes between zayf (counterfeit, rejected by treasury) and nabharajah (substandard, rejected by merchants). Though not central, it shows attention to terminology.
This is an expansion note from al-Mughrib.

Source Text

[1] قوله : درهماً دون درهم؛ أي لا يقبضه حال كون درهم منه مخالفاً لدرهم آخر في كونه غير مقبوض: أي لا يقبضه متفرقاً، بل جملة، فالمجموع في تأويل حال مشتقة، فهو مثل بعته يداً بيد : أي متقابضين، كذا في رد المحتار. [2]قوله : لا ببعضه دون باقيه؛ أي لا يحنث بمجرد قبض البعض، بل يتوقف حنثه على قبض باقيه، فإذا قبضه حنث؛ وذلك لأنه أضاف القبض المتفرق إلى كل الدين حيث قال : ديني؛ وهو اسم للمجموع، ولا يصدق قبض الكل متفرقاً إلا بعد قبض الكلّ فلا يقبض البعض. حنث بقبض البعض؛ لأن شرط الحنث هنا قبض البعض فلو قال : من ديني؛ من الدين متفرقاً، ولو قيد باليوم، فقبض البعض فيه متفرقاً، أو لم يقبض شيئاً لم يحنث؛ لأن الشرط أخذه متفرقاً ولم يوجد كذا في «الفتح» و«البحر». [3]قوله : أو كله؛ عطف على قوله : بعضه؛ أي لا يحنث بقبض بتفرق ضروري؛ لأنه لا يعد تفرقاً عرفاً إن كان يقبضه كله بوزنين لم يفصل بينهما كله متفرقاً.
And in: He does not receive his debt dirham by dirham, he is guilty (of oath-breaking) only by receiving it all separately, not by some of it without the rest, or all of it in two weighings without anything intervening except the act of weighing. And in al-Mughrib: It is said that the counterfeit coin (zayf) is inferior to the substandard coin (nabharajah) in badness; because the treasury rejects it, and nabharajah is what merchants reject. [1] The author says: dirhaman duna dirham means he does not receive it while one dirham is different from another in being not received: i.e., he does not receive it separately, but as a whole, so the whole is in the meaning of a derived state, like selling hand to hand: i.e., taking delivery together, as in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] The author says: la biba'dihi duna baqihi, i.e., he is not guilty merely by receiving part, rather his guilt depends on receiving the rest; when he receives it he is guilty. That is because he attached the separate receipt to the entire debt when he said: my debt, and it is a name for the whole, and receiving the whole separately is only true after receiving the whole, so he does not receive part. He would be guilty by receiving part? The condition for guilt here is receiving part? Actually the text says he is not guilty by just part. The explanation: if he restricted it to the day, and he received part of it separately in the day, or did not receive anything, he is not guilty. [3] The author says: or all of it; it is conjuncted to his saying: some of it; i.e., he is not guilty by a receipt that is necessarily separate; because it is not considered separate customarily if he receives all of it in two weighings without anything between them, i.e., all of it separately.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn from al-Wiqayah. It explains the ruling on an oath where a person swears not to receive his debt dirham by dirham (i.e., separately). The ruling is that he only breaks his oath (hanith) if he receives the entire debt in separate parts, not if he receives only a part. However, if he receives the entire debt but in two weighings without any significant interruption other than the act of weighing itself, that does not count as receiving separately according to customary understanding ('urf). The commentary also includes a linguistic note from al-Mughrib distinguishing between zayf (counterfeit coin) and nabharajah (substandard coin). The Hanafi position is based on the interpretation of the oath: the word 'dayn' (debt) refers to the whole, so receiving it partially does not violate the oath until the whole is received separately. This is a ruling from the Zahir al-Riwayah tradition.

Key Terms
حنث (hanith): Legal guilt or breaking of an oath; here, the moment when the oath becomes violated.
تفرق (tafarruq): Receiving in separate parts; the condition that triggers hanith in this oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When he receives the entire debt in separate installments
C) When he receives the entire debt at once
D) When he receives half of the debt

Key Takeaway
An oath not to receive debt piecemeal is broken only when the entire debt is received in separate parts, with the exception of weighings that are not considered separate by custom.

The Oath of 'Nothing But a Hundred': Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Istithna'
Explaining Hanafi ruling on oath with istithna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on an oath with an exception (istithna') when the swearer owns less than the excepted amount, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
istithna' in negation does not affirm the excepted; it clarifies the remainder, so an oath 'nothing but a hundred' is not broken if you own fifty.

This lesson examines a specific oath scenario where a person swears 'If I have nothing but a hundred, then so-and-so' while actually owning only fifty. The Hanafi school, based on its grammatical principles, holds that he does not break his oath because the intention is to negate having more than a hundred, not to affirm having exactly a hundred. We will explore the linguistic reasoning and the contrast with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the oath scenario
Identify the ruling: the person does not break his oath (hanith) when he owns fifty or exactly a hundred. The intention is to negate having more than a hundred.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school argues that istithna' in a negation affirms the excepted item, so 'I have nothing but a hundred' would imply he affirms having a hundred.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi'i proof: In 'he does not owe me anything except ten', the exception is said to affirm the ten. This is based on the Basran grammarians' view.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi response: Istithna' is speaking about the remainder after the exclusion, not affirming the excepted. The verse 'a thousand years except fifty' means nine hundred, not that fifty remained.
Hanafi follows Kufan grammarians.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In this oath, Hanafi: not hanith if owning ≤100; Shafi'i: could be hanith if the exception is seen as affirming existence of a hundred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في : إن كان لي إلا مئة فكذا، ولا يملك إلا خمسين) هذا بناء على أن الاستثناء عندنا تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا) ، وليس الاستثناء من النفي إثباتاً، فإن قوله : إن كان لي إلا مئة فكذا معناه ليس لي إلا مئة بشيء، فإنه ما دام في عمل الوزن لا يسمّى متفرّقاً. [1] قوله : ولا يملك إلا خمسين؛ أي والحال أنه ليس في ملكه إلا خمسون، والمراد به أنه يملك أقل من المئة، وكذا الحال إذا كان مالكاً للمائة بطريق الأولى، والوجه في ذلك : أن غرض القائل بهذا الكلام نفي الزيادة على المئة، لا نفي القلة، فلا يحنث إذا كان مالكاً للمئة، أو أقل منها، فإن كان مالكاً للزيادة حنث لو كانت الزيادة من جنس ما تجب فيه الزكاة : كالنقدين والسائمة وعروض التجارة. وإن قلت الزيادة ولو كانت من غيره لم يحنث؛ لأن المستثنى منه عرفاً هو المال لا الدراهم، ومطلق المال ينصرف إلى ما تجب فيه الزكاة، كما لو قال : والله ليس لي مال، وقال : مالي في المساكين صدقة، وهذا بخلاف ما لو أوصى بثلث ماله أو استأمن الحربي على ماله، حيث يعم جميع الأموال؛ لأن الوصية خلافة كالميراث، ومقصود الحربي الغنية له بماله كذا في شرح تلخيص الجامع الكبير». [2] قوله عندنا؛ قال أبو البقاء الكوفي في «كلياته»: الاستثناء من النفي إثبات، كقولك : ليس له على شيء إلا عشر فتلزمه عشرة، وبالعكس كقولك : له على عشرة إلا خمسة، فتلزمه خمسة، هذا عند الشافعي وقال أبو حنيفة : الاستثناء تكلّم بالباقي بعد الثنيا، بمعنى أنه استخراج صوري وبيان معنوي إذا المستثنى لم يرد أولاً نحو قوله : فَلَيتَ فِيهِمْ أَلْفَ سَنَةٍ إِلَّا خمسين عاما، والمراد تسعمئة سنة. قال البرماوي ما قاله الشافعي له موافق لقول سيبويه والبصريين، وهو مذهب الجمهور، وما قال أبو حنيفة له موافق لقول نحاة الكوفة؛ لأنه كوفي.
And not in: If I have nothing but a hundred then so-and-so, and he only owns fifty. This is based on the fact that exception (istithna) according to us is speaking of the remainder after the exclusion, and the exception in a negation is not an affirmation. For his saying: If I have nothing but a hundred means I have nothing but a hundred for something, as long as it is in the act of weighing it is not called separate. [1] His saying: nor does he own except fifty, i.e., while he only owns fifty, and it means he owns less than a hundred, and likewise if he owns a hundred by way of priority. The reason is that the intention of the speaker is to negate increase over a hundred, not to negate decrease, so he is not guilty if he owns a hundred or less. But if he owns more, he is guilty if the increase is of the type on which zakat is due: like gold, silver, grazing livestock, and trade goods. And if the increase is small even if from other types, he is not guilty, because the excluded category customarily is wealth (mal), not dirhams, and absolute wealth refers to what zakat is due on, as if he said: by Allah, I have no wealth, and said: my wealth is for the poor in charity. This is contrary to if he bequeaths a third of his wealth or a warrier seeks protection for his wealth, where it includes all wealth, because a bequest is a succession like inheritance, and the aim of the warrier is to be enriched by his wealth. So in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir. [2] His saying: according to us; Abu al-Baqa al-Kufi said in his Kulliyat: exception from negation is affirmation, as in your saying: he does not owe me anything except ten, so ten is due, and conversely: he owes me ten except five, so five is due. This is according to al-Shafi'i. And Abu Hanifa said: exception is speaking of the remainder after the exclusion, meaning it is a formal extraction and virtual clarification when the excepted was not intended first, as in the verse: 'He remained among them a thousand years except fifty years,' and the meaning is nine hundred years. Al-Barmawi said: what al-Shafi'i said agrees with the statement of Sibawayh and the Basrans, and it is the position of the majority. And what Abu Hanifa said agrees with the statement of the Kufan grammarians because he was Kufan.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi position on an oath where the swearer says 'If I have nothing but a hundred, then so-and-so,' and he actually owns less than a hundred. The ruling is that he does not break his oath (hanith) because the intention is to negate having more than a hundred, not to negate having less. The Hanafi school holds that istithna' (exception) in a negative statement does not affirm the opposite; rather it is a way of speaking about the remainder after the exclusion. This contrasts with the Shafi'i position which treats istithna' as affirmation of the excepted item. The passage also clarifies that if the swearer owns more than a hundred, he breaks the oath only if the excess is of a type subject to zakat. The commentary cites the grammatical schools: Abu Hanifa follows the Kufan grammarians, while al-Shafi'i follows the Basrans and the majority. The verse from Surah al-Ankabut is used to illustrate: 'a thousand years except fifty' means nine hundred years, not that fifty remained.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): A grammatical and legal term for an exception; in oaths, it specifies a portion excluded from the general statement.
المستثنى منه (al-mustathna minhu): The thing from which the exception is made; in the oath, it is 'wealth' (mal) customarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'If I have nothing but a hundred' and he actually owns fifty dirhams, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he owns more than the exception
B) No, because he owns less than a hundred
C) Yes, because the exception affirms he has a hundred
D) No, because the exception is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istithna' in a negation does not affirm the excepted item; it expresses the remainder, so an oath 'nothing but a hundred' is not broken by owning less than a hundred.

Exception from Negation in Oaths: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on the linguistic and legal effect of exception in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on exception from negation in oaths and evaluate the practical difference from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Exception from negation only negates the opposite; it does not affirm the specific – a crucial difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i rulings on oaths.

When someone swears 'I have only one hundred dirhams,' does that mean they are affirming exactly one hundred, or simply denying having more? The answer shapes whether the oath is broken if they actually have less. This seemingly linguistic nuance leads to a significant inter-madhhab dispute. In this lesson, we will trace the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments and see how a grammatical difference yields different fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on exception from negation: it does not affirm the opposite; it only negates what is beyond the excepted item.
Core phrase: 'al-istithna min al-nafi laysa ithbatan'
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State Imam al-Shafi'i's position: exception from negation does affirm the opposite. For example, 'I have only one hundred' means exactly one hundred.
The Shafi'i view aligns with Basran grammarians like Sibawayh.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the Basran linguistic tradition and the consensus that 'la ilaha illa Allah' affirms tawhid after negation.
This is a strong objection – how can Hanafis reconcile?
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain the Hanafi rebuttal: the affirmation in 'la ilaha illa Allah' is by shar'i custom (urf shar'i), not linguistic convention. The linguistic principle itself does not affirm.
Hanafis rely on Kufan grammar and the purpose of the speaker.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if a man swears 'I have only one hundred' but has fifty, Hanafis rule the oath not broken; Shafi'is rule it broken.
This affects whether kaffarah (expiation) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان
اليمين في الثياب والحج والصلاة والصوم
ولا في : إن كان لي إلا مئة فكذا، ولا يملك إلا خمسين
ولا في : لا يشم ريحاناً إن شم ورداً، أو ياسمينا فهو لنفي ما فوق المئة، وأما إثبات المئة فغير لازم عندنا
هذا بناء على أن الاستثناء عندنا تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا، وليس الاستثناء من النفي إثباتاً، فإن قوله : إن كان لي إلا مئة فكذا معناه ليس لي إلا مئة بشيء، فإنه ما دام في عمل الوزن لا يسمّى متفرّقاً.
قوله : ولا يملك إلا خمسين؛ أي والحال أنه ليس في ملكه إلا خمسون، والمراد به أنه يملك أقل من المئة، وكذا الحال إذا كان مالكاً للمائة بطريق الأولى، والوجه في ذلك: أن غرض القائل بهذا الكلام نفي الزيادة على المئة، لا نفي القلة، فلا يحنث إذا كان مالكاً للمئة، أو أقل منها، فإن كان مالكاً للزيادة حنث لو كانت الزيادة من جنس ما تجب فيه الزكاة: كالنقدين والسائمة وعروض التجارة. وإن قلت الزيادة ولو كانت من غيره لم يحنث؛ لأن المستثنى منه عرفاً هو المال لا الدراهم، ومطلق المال ينصرف إلى ما تجب فيه الزكاة، كما لو قال: والله ليس لي مال، وقال: مالي في المساكين صدقة، وهذا بخلاف ما لو أوصى بثلث ماله أو استأمن الحربي على ماله، حيث يعم جميع الأموال؛ لأن الوصية خلافة كالميراث، ومقصود الحربي الغنية له بماله. كذا في شرح تلخيص الجامع الكبير.
قوله عندنا؛ قال أبو البقاء الكوفي في كلياته: الاستثناء من النفي إثبات، كقولك: ليس له على شيء إلا عشر فتلزمه عشرة، وبالعكس كقولك: له على عشرة إلا خمسة، فتلزمه خمسة، هذا عند الشافعي.
وقال أبو حنيفة: الاستثناء تكلّم بالباقي بعد الثنيا، بمعنى أنه استخراج صوري وبيان معنوي إذا المستثنى لم يرد أولاً نحو قوله: فَلَيتَ فِيهِمْ أَلْفَ سَنَةٍ إِلَّا خمسين عاما، والمراد تسعمئة سنة. قال البرماوي: ما قاله الشافعي موافق لقول سيبويه والبصريين، وهو مذهب الجمهور، وما قال أبو حنيفة موافق لقول نحاة الكوفة؛ لأنه كوفي.
وأما الإجماع المنعقد على أن لا الله إلا الله تفيد التوحيد، ولو من الدهري، وذلك لا يحصل إلا بالإثبات بعد النفي.
فالجواب: إن إفادة كلمة التوحيد الإثبات بعد النفي بالعرف الشرعي، وكلامنا في الوضع اللغوي. انتهى.
قوله: فهو لنفي ما فوق المئة، فإن صدر الكلام: أي بالمستثنى منه، وهو المال، تناول المئة وما فوقها، والاستثناء دل على نفي ما عدا المستثنى، فكأنه قال: لا أملك ما فوق المئة، فإن كنت مالكاً له فكذا.
قوله: فغير لازم عندنا؛ فلا يضر ملك الأقلّ، فلا يحنث بملك الأقل، كما لا يحنث بملك المئة.
قوله: لا يشم؛ - بفتح الياء والشين المعجمة وتشديد الميم – من شممت الطيب بكسر الميم في الماضي، وجاء في لغة فتح الميم في الماضي وضمها في المضارع. كذا في النهر.
قوله: ريحاناً؛ قيل: هو ما طاب ريحه من النبات، وقيل: هو ما لساقه رائحة طيبة كالورد، قيل: هو ما لا ساق له من البقول مما له رائحة مستلذة، والمدار في ذلك على العرف، ولذا قالوا: يحنث في لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً بشراء ورقهما لا بشراء دهنهما. كذا في الفتح.
قوله: ياسميناً؛ أي ياسمين - بفتح الياء بعدها ألف، ثم سين مفتوحة، ثم ميم مكسورة، ثم ياء ساكنة، ثم نون -، كلي ست زروخو شبودار.
The Book of Oaths: The oath concerning garments, pilgrimage, prayer, and fasting, and not: 'If I have only one hundred, then such-and-such,' while he does not possess except fifty. And not: 'He does not smell basil if he smells a rose or jasmine,' for it is for negating what is above one hundred, and as for affirming one hundred, it is not necessary according to us. This is based on the fact that exception with us is speech about the remainder after the exception, and exception from negation is not affirmation. For his saying: 'If I have only one hundred, then such-and-such' means 'I do not have anything except one hundred,' for as long as it is in the work of weight, it is not called scattered. His saying: 'and he does not possess except fifty' means that the situation is that he does not possess except fifty, and the intent is that he possesses less than one hundred, and similarly the case if he possesses one hundred a fortiori. The point in that is that the purpose of the speaker with this speech is to negate excess over one hundred, not to negate scarcity. So he does not break the oath if he possesses one hundred or less. If he possesses excess, he breaks the oath if the excess is of the type that zakat is obligatory on it, like the two currencies, grazing livestock, and trade goods. And if the excess is small, even if from other than that, he does not break the oath because the thing excepted from, according to custom, is wealth, not dirhams, and absolute wealth is understood to refer to that upon which zakat is obligatory, just as if he said: 'By Allah, I have no wealth,' and he said: 'My wealth is charity for the poor.' And this is contrary to if he bequeathed a third of his wealth or gave protection to a harbi regarding his wealth, where it includes all types of wealth, because a bequest is a succession like inheritance, and the harbi's purpose is enrichment with his wealth. Thus in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir. His saying: 'according to us'; Abu al-Baqa' al-Kufi said in his Kulliyat: Exception from negation is affirmation, as in your saying: 'He does not have anything upon me except ten' so ten are obligatory for him, and vice versa, as in your saying: 'He has upon me ten except five,' so five are obligatory. This is according to al-Shafi'i. And Abu Hanifa said: Exception is speech about the remainder after the exception, meaning that it is a formal extraction and a conceptual clarification, since the thing excepted was not intended first, as in His saying: 'So he remained among them a thousand years except fifty years,' and the intended meaning is nine hundred years. Al-Barmawi said: What al-Shafi'i said agrees with the statement of Sibawayh and the Basrans, and it is the position of the majority, and what Abu Hanifa said agrees with the statement of the Kufan grammarians, because he is Kufan. As for the established consensus that 'there is no god but Allah' indicates tawhid, even from a materialist, and that is not achieved except by affirmation after negation. The answer: The indication of the word of tawhid of affirmation after negation is by the Islamic custom, and our discussion is about linguistic convention. End. His saying: 'for it is for negating what is above one hundred,' for the first part of the speech, i.e., with the thing excepted from, which is wealth, encompasses one hundred and what is above it, and the exception indicates the negation of what is other than the excepted thing, so it is as if he said: 'I do not possess what is above one hundred; if I possess it, then such-and-such.' His saying: 'so it is not necessary according to us'; so possession of less does not harm, so he does not break the oath by possessing less, just as he does not break the oath by possessing one hundred. His saying: 'he does not smell' - with fatha on the ya and shin with tashdid on the mim - from 'shamaytu al-tib' with kasra on the mim in the past tense, and in one language it has fatha on the mim in the past and damma in the present. Thus in al-Nahr. His saying: 'basil'; some say it is what has a good smell from plants, some say it is what has a good smell from its stalk like roses, some say it is what has no stalk from herbs that have a pleasant smell, and the criterion in that is custom, and because of this they said: He breaks the oath if he does not buy violets or roses by buying their leaves, not by buying their oil. Thus in al-Fath. His saying: 'jasmine'; i.e., yasmin - with fatha on the ya followed by alif, then a fatha on the sin, then a kasra on the mim, then a sukun on the ya, then a nun - all six zaru khwa shubadar.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the legal meaning of exception (istithna) in oaths. The Hanafi school holds that exception from negation does not affirm the opposite; rather, it merely indicates what remains after excluding the excepted item. Thus, when someone swears 'I have only one hundred' (meaning 'I do not have except one hundred'), the purpose is to negate having more than one hundred, not to affirm that he has exactly one hundred. Therefore, if he actually has less than one hundred, he does not break his oath. Conversely, if he has more than one hundred, he breaks the oath only if the excess is of a type upon which zakat is obligatory (gold, silver, grazing livestock, or trade goods). The text explains that the noun 'wealth' (mal) in such contexts customarily refers to zakatable assets. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that exception from negation affirms the opposite, so swearing 'I have only one hundred' means he has exactly one hundred. The Shafi'i view is supported by the Basran grammarians (Sibawayh), while the Hanafi view aligns with the Kufan grammarians. The text also addresses an objection based on the consensus that the phrase 'la ilaha illa Allah' (there is no god but Allah) affirms tawhid after negation. The Hanafi response is that this affirmation is due to shar'i custom, not linguistic convention. Finally, the passage applies these principles to specific cases: swearing about smelling basil, rose, or jasmine, where the definition of 'rayhan' (basil) depends on custom, and the effect of purchasing the flowers or their derivatives. The Hanafi position is that exception only negates what is excluded, so having less does not break the oath.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Linguistic exception using 'illa' (except); in fiqh, the legal effect of such exception in oaths and contracts.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah; breaking it incurs kaffarah (expiation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man swears 'I do not have except one hundred dirhams' but actually possesses fifty dirhams, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because he has less than one hundred.
B) He does not break his oath because exception from negation does not affirm the opposite.
C) He breaks his oath only if the fifty dirhams are from zakatable assets.
D) He does not break his oath only if he intended exactly one hundred.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that exception from negation only negates the opposite, not affirm the exact amount; thus having less than the excepted amount does not break an oath.

Interpreting 'Rayhan' in Oaths: Custom and Linguistic Analysis
Commentary on the interpretation of 'rayhan' in oaths, emphasizing custom and the stem distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the interpretation of an oath is based on custom ('urf) to determine whether an oath is broken, and distinguish between rayhan (plants without stems) and flowers with stems like roses and jasmine.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'rayhan' excludes stemmed flowers like roses and jasmine by custom.

When a person swears not to smell 'rayhan', does he break that oath by smelling a rose or jasmine? The Hanafi school teaches that the meaning of 'rayhan' in oaths is determined by common usage, not botanical classification. This lesson unpacks the commentary on the phrase 'لا يشم ريحاناً' from the Hanafi tradition, clarifying the role of custom and the distinction based on the presence of a stem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic rule about 'fi' in oaths
The passage first clarifies that the preposition 'fi' in an oath like 'لا في مالي مئة' (I have no one hundred in my wealth) negates amounts above the specified number, not the number itself. Thus, having exactly one hundred does not break the oath; having more does.
This rule is specific to the Hanafi school's interpretation of exception (istithna) in oaths.
2
Define 'rayhan' according to custom
The commentary states that 'rayhan' is defined by custom as plants that have a pleasant scent and no stem. This definition excludes roses and jasmine, which have stems.
Multiple linguistic definitions are mentioned, but the ultimate criterion is common usage ('urf).
3
Apply the distinction to the oath scenario
If someone swears 'I will not smell rayhan' and then smells a rose or jasmine, he does not break his oath because these are not considered rayhan according to custom.
The same logic applies to buying: buying leaves of violets or roses counts as buying the plant (breaking an oath), but buying their oil does not.
4
Address the objection from the kalimah
A potential objection is raised: the kalimah (testimony of faith) also uses negation and affirmation, but its affirmation is by legal custom, not linguistic. The discussion here is purely linguistic.
This clarifies that the rule for oaths is based on linguistic convention, not religious terminology.

Source Text

ولا في : لا يشم ريحاناً إن شم ورداً، أو ياسمينا فهو لنفي ما فوق المئة ، وأما إثبات المئة فغير لازم عندنا". (ولا) في : لا يشم ريحانا" إن شم ورداً، أو ياسمينا ) ؛ لأنَّ الرَّيحانَ ما لا ساق له ، والورد والياسمين لهما ساق وأما الإجماع المنعقد على أن لا الله الا الله تفيد التوحيد، ولو من الدهري ، وذلك لا يحصل إلا بالإثبات بعد النفي. فالجواب : إن إفادة كلمة التوحيد الإثبات بعد النفي بالعرف الشرعي، وكلامنا في الوضع اللغوي. انتهى. قوله : فهو لنفي ما فوق المئة، فإن صدر الكلام : أي بالمستثنى منه، وهو المال، تناول المئة وما فوقها، والاستثناء دل على نفي ما عدا المستثنى ، فكأنه قال : لا أملك ما فوق المئة، فإن كنت مالكاً له فكذا. قوله : فغير لازم عندنا ؛ فلا يضر ملك الأقلّ، فلا يحنث بملك الأقل، كما لا يحنث بملك المئة. قوله : لا يشم ؛ - بفتح الياء والشين المعجمة وتشديد الميم – من شممت الطيب بكسر الميم في الماضي، وجاء في لغة فتح الميم في الماضي وضمها في المضارع. كذا في «النهر» قوله : ريحاناً ؛ قيل : هو ما طاب ريحه من النبات ، وقيل : هو ما لساقه رائحة طيبة كالورد، قيل : هو ما لا ساق له من البُقُول مما له رائحة مستلذة، والمدار في ذلك على العرف، ولذا قالوا : يحنث في لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً بشراء ورقهما لا بشراء دهنهما. كذا في «الفتح»). [0] قوله : ياسميناً ؛ أي ياسمين - بفتح الياء بعدها ألف، ثم سين مفتوحة، ثم ميم مكسورة، ثم ياء ساكنة ، ثم نون - ، كلی ست زروخو شبودار.
And 'fi': he does not smell a sweet-smelling plant if he smells a rose or jasmine; it (the word 'fi') is for negating what is above one hundred. As for affirming one hundred, it is not necessary according to us. (And 'fi'): he does not smell a sweet-smelling plant if he smells a rose or jasmine, because a sweet-smelling plant is that which has no stem, while the rose and jasmine have stems. As for the consensus that 'la ilaha illa Allah' conveys Tawheed, even from a Dahri (atheist), and that is not obtained except by affirmation after negation. The answer: The phrase of Tawheed gives affirmation after negation by the law-based custom (al-'urf al-shar'i), but our discussion is about the linguistic (wad'i) meaning. End. His saying: 'It is for negating what is above one hundred': the beginning of the statement, i.e., by the excluded thing (mustathna minhu), which is wealth, includes one hundred and above it. The exception indicates the negation of what is other than the excepted thing, so it is as if he said: 'I do not possess what is above one hundred; if I own it, then it is thus.' His saying: 'It is not necessary according to us': so possessing less does not harm, and he does not break the oath by possessing less, just as he does not break the oath by possessing one hundred. His saying: 'does not smell' – with fatha on the ya and the mu'jama shin and tashdid on the mim – from 'shamt al-tib' with kasra on the mim in the past, and in a language it comes with fatha on the mim in the past and damma on it in the present. This is in 'al-Nahr'. His saying: 'sweet-smelling plant': it is said: it is what has a good scent from plants, and it is said: it is what has a good-smelling stem like the rose, and it is said: it is what has no stem among herbs that have a pleasant scent. The criterion in this is custom ('urf). That is why they said: He breaks the oath in 'he does not buy violets or roses' by buying their leaves, not by buying their oil. This is in 'al-Fath'. [0] His saying: 'jasmine' – meaning jasmine – with fatha on the ya, then alif, then a fat-hah sin, then a kasra mim, then a sukun ya, then noon.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the Hanafi school on the interpretation of an oath involving the phrase 'لا يشم ريحاناً' (he does not smell a sweet-smelling plant). The author explains that the word 'في' in the original oath ('لا في مالي مئة') negates amounts above one hundred, not exactly one hundred. Thus, if someone swears 'I do not have one hundred in my wealth,' he only breaks the oath if he has more than one hundred; having exactly one hundred does not break it. The commentary then addresses a potential objection regarding the kalimah (testimony of faith), which also involves negation and affirmation, but clarifies that the discussion here is linguistic, not legal. The passage then provides lexical and customary definitions: 'rayhan' refers to sweet-smelling plants without stems, such as certain herbs, while roses and jasmine have stems and are not included. This distinction is based on common usage ('urf). Therefore, swearing not to smell 'rayhan' does not cover roses or jasmine. The commentary also notes that in the context of buying violets or roses, the oath applies to the leaves, not the oil, as per custom.

Key Terms
ريحان (rayhan): A sweet-smelling plant without a stem, as defined by custom in the context of oaths.
ساق (saq): The stem of a plant; the presence of a stem distinguishes roses and jasmine from rayhan.
في (fi): In the context of oaths, the preposition 'fi' is interpreted to negate only amounts above the mentioned number, not the number itself.
عرف (urf): Custom or common usage; the criterion for defining terms in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The botanical classification
C) Customary usage ('urf)
D) The intention of the person swearing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the interpretation of terms in oaths is governed by prevailing custom, not literal or scientific definitions.

Scope of Oath on Violets and Roses: Leaves Only
Commentary on the matn's ruling that the oath covers only the leaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the scope of an oath regarding violets or roses and apply the principle of custom ('urf) in interpreting such oaths.

Core Idea
An oath concerning flowers applies to their leaves only, not to stems or derived oils — custom defines the object.

When someone swears not to buy a specific flower, what exactly is included in the oath? The Hanafi school rules that such an oath applies only to the leaves (or petals) of the flower, not to the stems or any extracted oil. This ruling is based on common usage ('urf) and is a classic example of how custom shapes legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under consideration? Swearing not to buy violets or roses. The ruling is that the oath is valid and applies only to the leaves (waraq) of these flowers.
The matn states 'على الورق' meaning upon the leaves.
2
Recognize the scope limitation
The oath does not cover the stems (a'jaz) or the oil extracted from the flowers. The commentator explicitly excludes stems.
أعجاز: stems, plural of 'ajz.
3
Understand the basis: custom
The ruling is grounded in custom ('urf). In common parlance, when one says 'violets' or 'roses,' one refers to the flowers themselves (leaves/petals), not the stems or oil.
Custom is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh, especially in oaths and contracts.
4
Note the practical consequence
If someone buys stems or rose oil, they do not break their oath; only buying the leaves does.
This prevents unnecessary hardship.
5
Consider the broader principle
Custom ('urf) determines the meaning of ambiguous terms in oaths. This principle applies beyond flowers to any such case.
The Hanafi school gives weight to 'urf in many areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان اليمين في الثياب والحج والصلاة والصوم (والبنفسج والورد على الورق) (۱) : أي ورد الورد دون أعجاز الورد التي عليها الورق. [٢] [١] قوله : والبنفسج ؛ - بفتح الباء الموحدة والنون وسكون الفاء وفتح السين المهملة آخره جيم – معرب بنفشة. [۲] قوله : أعجاز الورد ؛ - بفتح الهمزة - جمع عجز، – بفتح العين المهملة وكسر الجيم وضمها بالفارسية يس هر حيز. (۱) أي يقعان على ورقهما، فلو حلف أن لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً يقع على الورق. ينظر : «شرح ملا مسكين»(ص١٥٣).
Book of Oaths: Oaths regarding clothes, Hajj, prayer, and fasting. (And violets and roses apply to the leaves): i.e., the leaves of the rose, not the stems of the rose upon which the leaves are. [2] [1] His saying: 'And violets' – with fatha on the ba, sukun on the nun, sukun on the fa, fatha on the sin, ending with jim – is arabicized from 'banafshah'. [2] His saying: 'stems of the rose' – with fatha on the hamza – plural of 'ajz, with fatha on the 'ayn and kasra on the jim and damma on it; in Persian: 'يس هر حيز'. (1) i.e., they apply to their leaves; so if he swears not to buy violets or roses, it applies to the leaves. Reference: 'Sharh Mulla Miskeen' (p. 153).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the Hanafi school on a ruling regarding oaths related to specific flowers. The matn states: 'والبنفسج والورد على الورق' meaning that when one swears not to buy violets (banafsaj) or roses (ward), the oath applies to the leaves (waraq) of these flowers, not to the stems (a'jaz) or the extracted oil. The commentator clarifies that 'أعجاز الورد' refers to the stems, and the oath only covers the leaves. This interpretation is based on common usage (urf) and is supported by references to other Hanafi works like Sharh Mulla Miskeen. The linguistic notes provide the correct pronunciation and origin of the word 'بنفسج' (from Persian). Thus, if someone buys the stems or oil of these flowers, he does not break his oath.

Key Terms
بنفسج (banafsaj): Violet (flower); a loanword from Persian (بنفشه).
ورد (ward): Rose (flower).
ورق (waraq): Leaves of a plant; in this context, the petals or leaves of the flower.
أعجاز (a'jaz): Stems (plural of 'ajz); the woody part of the flower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Buying rose stems
B) Buying rose leaves
C) Buying rose oil
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the scope of an oath regarding flowers is determined by custom ('urf), limiting it to the leaves only.

Oath on Violet or Rose Covers the Leaves
Defining the scope of an oath on flowers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the scope of an oath concerning violets or roses and identify common usage ('urf) as its basis.

Core Idea
An oath on a flower includes its leaves — common usage defines the scope.

When someone swears not to buy a violet or a rose, what exactly does the oath cover? The Hanafi school relies on common understanding ('urf), not a strict literal reading of the blossom alone. In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator clarifies that the oath falls on the leaves of the plant, explicitly excluding the stems. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this definitional ruling and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the oath
The oath concerns violets (banafsaj) and roses (ward). Determine what the common name refers to in everyday speech.
Hanafis give weight to 'urf in oath interpretations.
2
Locate the explicit scope in the passage
The commentator states the oath falls 'upon the leaves' (على الورق). This is the direct ruling.
3
Exclude what is not covered
The passage explicitly excludes the stems (أعجاز) of the rose. Understand why stems are not included.
Stems are not typically considered part of the flower in common parlance.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The basis for this ruling is common usage ('urf). When people talk about buying a rose, they generally include the leaves.
This principle is applied in other Hanafi fiqh rulings on oaths.
5
Apply the rule to similar cases
The same logic extends to other flowers or plants where the common name includes leaves. Test with a hypothetical oath on a tulip or daisy.
Always check if there is a specific exclusion in the wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان. اليمين في الثياب والحج والصلاة والصوم. (والبنفسج والورد على الورق) (1) : أي ورق الورد دون أعجاز الورد التي عليها الورق. [1] قوله : والبنفسج ؛ - بفتح الباء الموحدة والنون وسكون الفاء وفتح السين المهملة آخره جيم – معرب بنفشة. [2] قوله : أعجاز الورد ؛ - بفتح الهمزة - جمع عجز، – بفتح العين المهملة وكسر الجيم وضمها بالفارسية يس هر حيز. (1) أي يقعان على ورقهما، فلو حلف أن لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً يقع على الورق.
Book of Oaths. The oath concerning clothing, Hajj, prayer, and fasting. (And violet and rose upon the leaves) (1): meaning the leaves of the rose, not the stems of the rose upon which the leaves are. [1] His saying: 'violet' - with fatha on the ba and nun, sukun on the fa, fatha on the sin, and jīm at the end - it is Arabized from 'banafshah'. [2] His saying: 'stems of the rose' - with fatha on the hamza - plural of 'ajz, with fatha on the 'ayn and kasra on the jīm, and in Persian 'yis har hīz'. (1) i.e., they apply to their leaves. So if one swears not to buy violet or rose, it applies to the leaves.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from Al-Wiqayah regarding the scope of an oath involving violets or roses. The author explains that when someone swears an oath concerning these flowers, the ruling applies specifically to their leaves (ورق). The text clarifies that the term 'rose' (ورد) includes its leaves, not the stems (أعجاز) on which the leaves grow. Similarly, for violets, the oath covers the leaves. This is a definitional ruling based on common usage in oaths. The commentator also provides linguistic notes: 'violet' (بنفسج) is an Arabized Persian word (بنفشة), and 'stems' (أعجاز) is the plural of 'ajz, defined in Persian as 'base' or 'stem'. The key point is that in Hanafi jurisprudence, when an oath is taken on a general name of a flower, it encompasses the leaves associated with it, not merely the petals or the flower head.

Key Terms
البنفسج (al-banafsaj): Violet flower; an Arabized Persian word (بنفشة). In this ruling, it refers to the plant including its leaves.
الورد (al-ward): Rose; the passage specifies that the oath on roses applies to the leaves (ورق) and not the stems (أعجاز).
الورق (al-waraq): Leaves; the subject of the oath when flowers are mentioned.
أعجاز (a'jāz): Stems or bases of the rose; plural of 'ajz. Excluded from the scope of the oath.

Oath Not to Speak: Sleeping Person and Permission Exception
Intra-Hanafi dispute on knowledge requirement for permission in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi conditions for breaking an oath not to speak, specifically regarding speaking to a sleeping person and the exception 'except with permission', and distinguish the Hanafi position from that of Abu Yusuf on the knowledge requirement.

Core Idea
An oath not to speak is broken only if the listener can hear; permission requires notification, not mere internal consent.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah commentary on al-Wiqayah examines two subtle issues in oath law: speaking to a sleeping person and the phrase 'except with his permission'. The Hanafi school holds that waking the sleeping person is necessary for the oath to be broken, because speech is only effective when the listener can hear. Furthermore, a distinction is drawn between permission (idhn) and pleasure (rida): permission requires actual communication to the swearer, while pleasure does not. Abu Yusuf disagreed on the latter point, leading to an important intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the sleeping person scenario
Identify the ruling: the oath is broken only if the swearer wakes the sleeping person before speaking. Understand the reasoning: speech requires the listener to be capable of hearing.
The chosen view (al-mukhtar) requires waking.
2
Examine the 'except with permission' exception
Identify the two sub-cases: 'except with his permission' and 'except with his pleasure'. Note the different rulings for each.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues by analogy: just as pleasure does not require knowledge, permission should also not require knowledge. Identify his proof.
Opposing school is Abu Yusuf, not a different madhhab.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (Abu Hanifa's view) relies on linguistic derivation: idhn comes from adhan (informing) or wuqu' fi al-udhun (entering the ear), both requiring hearing. Thus, permission without knowledge is ineffective. Contrast with rida which is internal.
This is a talil argument (reasoning based on linguistic derivation).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view, as endorsed by al-Nihaya and al-Inaya. The chosen view requires waking for the sleeping person case.
The commentary states 'al-mukhtar' for waking and 'al-Nihaya wa al-Inaya' for idhn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالقول
افصل اليمين في الكلام والبيع والشراء والتزوج وغير ذلك] وحنث في : حلف لا يكلّمُهُ إن كلّمه نائماً بشرط إيقاظه. وفي : إلا بإذنه
أي وحنث في : حلف لا يكلمه إلا بإذنه

[١] قوله : بشرط إيقاظه ؛ أي ذلك النائم، وكثير من المشايخ لم يشترطوا الإيقاظ ووجهـوه عـلـى مـا في «النهاية»: إن التكلّم عبارة عن استماعه كلامه كما في تكلمه نفسه، فإنه عبارة عن إسماع نفسه، إلا أن إسماع الغير أمر باطن لا يوقف عليه، فأقيم السبب المؤدي إليه مقامه. وهو أن يكون بحيث لو أصغى إليه أذنه، ولا يكون به مانع من السماع لسمع، ودار الحكم معه، وسقط اعتبار حقيقة الإسماع، والمختار أنه لو أيقظه حنث، وإلا لا ؛ لأنه إذا لم يتنبه كان كما إذا ناداه من بعيد وهو بحيث لا يسمع صوته. لا.

[٢] قوله : وفي ؛ عطف على قوله : «في حلف لا يكلمه.... الخ، وكذا نظائره الآتية، وقوله : الآتي وبفعل وكيله... الخ.

[٣] قوله : إلا بإذنه ؛ ولو قال : إلا برضاه فرضي المحلوف عليه بالاستثناء، ولم يعلمه الحالف لا يحنث اتفاقاً، واستشهد به أبو يوسف الله فيما نحن فيه، فقال لا يحنث في صورة الإذن أيضاً إذا لم يعلمه الحالف، ونحن نقول : الرضـا يـتـم بالتراضي ولا يتوقف وجوده على علم الغير، فلا يحنث بوجوده عَلِمَ أو لم يعلم.
بخلاف الإذن فإنه مشتق من الأذان الذي بمعنى الإعلام، أو من الوقوع في الإذن، وكلّ ذلك لا يتحقق إلا بالسماع، وما في حكمـه فـمـا لـم يـصـل خـبر الإذن إلى الحالف لا يُسمّى ذلك إذناً. كذا في «النهاية» و«العناية»(۱).
(۱) (العناية (٥) : ١٤٥).
Chapter on oath by speech.
He differentiates the oath in speech, sale, purchase, marriage, and other matters. And he breaks the oath if he swears not to speak to him and then speaks to him while asleep, with the condition of waking him. And (he breaks the oath) in: except with his permission.
Meaning: He breaks the oath if he swears not to speak to him except with his permission.

[1] His saying: with the condition of waking him; that is, that sleeping person. And many of the mashayikh did not stipulate waking, and they explained it based on what is in al-Nihaya: that speech (takallum) is expression of hearing his speech, just as in one's own speech it is expression of making oneself hear. However, making another hear is an internal matter that cannot be known, so the cause leading to it is established in its place. And that is that he be in a state that if he lent his ear to him, and there is no obstacle to hearing due to [lack of] hearing, and the ruling revolves with it, and consideration of the reality of making hear is dropped. The chosen (al-mukhtar) is that if he wakes him, he breaks the oath, otherwise no; because if he did not become alert, it is as if he called him from afar while he cannot hear his voice. No.

[2] His saying: And; is conjuncted to his saying: 'in he swears not to speak to him... etc.' and similarly its parallels coming, and his saying coming: 'and by his agent... etc.'

[3] His saying: except with his permission; and if he said: except with his pleasure, and the person sworn about was pleased with the exception, but the swearer did not know it, he does not break the oath by agreement. And Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) used this as evidence in our subject, saying: he does not break the oath in the case of permission as well if the swearer did not know it. And we say: Pleasure (rida) is completed by mutual consent and its existence does not depend on the knowledge of others, so he does not break the oath by its existence whether he knew or not. Unlike permission (idhn), for it is derived from adhan which means informing, or from occurrence in the ear (al-wuqu' fi al-udhun), and all that is not realized except through hearing, and what is in its ruling; so as long as the news of the permission does not reach the swearer, that is not called permission. Thus in al-Nihaya and al-Inaya.
(1) (al-Inaya (5): 145).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from the chapter on oaths related to speech. The matn states two scenarios where an oath not to speak to someone is broken: (1) if the swearer speaks to the person while he is asleep, but with the condition that he wakes him up; (2) if the swearer says 'except with his permission' and then speaks without permission. The commentator first clarifies the condition of waking the sleeping person. Many early scholars did not require waking, but the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that waking is necessary because speech requires the listener to be capable of hearing. If the sleeping person is not woken, it is like calling from afar where the person cannot hear. Thus, the oath is only broken if the swearer wakes him first; otherwise, no.

The commentator then explains the word 'Wa' (and) as a conjunction linking to the previous case. Finally, he discusses the phrase 'except with his permission'. He contrasts it with 'except with his pleasure' (rida). If the swearer says 'except with his pleasure', and the other person is pleased but the swearer does not know, there is agreement that the oath is not broken. Abu Yusuf analogizes this to the case of permission, arguing that if the swearer does not know of the permission, he should not break the oath either. However, the Hanafi school (as represented by the commentator) rejects this analogy. Pleasure is internal and does not require notification; permission by contrast is derived from 'adhan' (announcement) or from the idea of entering the ear, both of which require actual hearing. Thus, unless the swearer learns of the permission, it is not considered permission, and he breaks the oath if he speaks. This is the position of the authors of al-Nihaya and al-Inaya.

Key Terms
الحنث (al-hanth): The violation or breaking of an oath by doing the thing sworn not to do.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission; in this context, it requires the permission to be communicated to the swearer to be effective.
الرضا (al-rida): Pleasure or consent; internal satisfaction that does not require external communication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view in the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'I will not speak to Zaid except with his permission,' and Zaid gives permission but does not inform the swearer, then the swearer speaks. What is the ruling?
A) The oath is not broken because permission was given.
B) The oath is broken because permission must be communicated.
C) The oath is not broken because the swearer could have known.
D) The oath is broken only if the swearer spoke intentionally.

Key Takeaway
For an oath not to speak, the exception 'except with permission' requires actual notification to the swearer; internal consent alone does not avert breaking the oath.

Swearing Freedom on Sale or Purchase with Conditional Option
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath of freedom with khiyar al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths of freedom tied to sale/purchase with khiyar al-shart and explain the internal difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
An oath of freedom is fulfilled even with a conditional option — the ruling is unanimous, but the reasoning diverges.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores a nuanced case: a person swears 'if I sell this slave, he is free' or 'if I buy him, he is free' and then contracts with a conditional option (khiyar al-shart). Despite the option's impact on ownership, all Hanafi imams hold that the slave is freed. However, they reach this conclusion via different juristic paths. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how khiyar al-shart affects ownership transfer and the operation of suspended oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath scenarios
The oath can be 'if I sell' or 'if I buy' the slave, with freedom as the consequence. Identify exactly which condition triggers the freedom.
These are distinct scenarios with different implications for ownership.
2
State the unanimous ruling
In both scenarios, when the contract is made with khiyar, the slave becomes free. This is the agreed outcome.
Despite the internal difference, the practical result is the same.
3
Analyze the sale scenario and its reasoning
For sale with option, the slave does not leave the seller's ownership during the option period (if option is for seller). The condition (sale) is met while ownership remains, so freedom takes effect. The hadith 'no freedom in what one does not own' is not violated.
If the sale were binding, ownership would transfer immediately, and the slave would not be freed.
4
Analyze the purchase scenario and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For purchase with option, all agree freedom occurs, but the reasoning differs: Abu Hanifa bases freedom on the contract itself (the act of purchase), while the two companions base it on entry into ownership (which they say occurs despite the option).
This difference matters in scenarios like rescission of the option.
5
Apply the motivator principle
The description (ba'ith) behind the oath is considered: if the motive is the sale/purchase itself, the option does not negate the oath. If the motive were something else (e.g., transferring ownership), the outcome might differ.
This principle helps interpret similar oath situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : هذا حر إن بعثه أو اشتريته إن عقد بالخيار. لأنَّ الوصف المذكور لا يصلح مانعاً من التكلم فيرادُ الذات. ( وفي : هذا حرّ إن بعثه أو اشتريته إن عقد بالخيار ) : أي إذا قال : إن بعته فهو حرّ، فباعَهُ على أنه بالخيار يعتق ؛ لأنه لم يخرج عن ملكه ، وقد وُجِدَ الشَّرط ، وهو البيع ، ولو قال : إن اشتريته ، فهو حر فشراه على أنه بالخيار عُتِق. أما على أصلهما ؛ فلأنه دخل في ملك المشتري. وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة له ، فلأنه علق العتق بالشراء ، فكأنه قال بعد الشراء بالخيار، فهو حر فيعتق. [؟] وجهل فحلف بعدما الكلام معه ، أو عَرَفَ أن الكلام مع الشاب ما دام شاباً يضر في دينه أو دنياه فامتنع منه تحمل هذه الأيمان على مدة بقاء تلك الأوصاف، كذا حققه في «الفتح» قوله : لا يصلح مانعاً... الخ ؛ أشار به إلى ضابطة مر ذكرها غير مرة : وهي أن الوصف إذا كان باعثاً على اليمين كان معتبراً ، وإذا لم يكن باعثاً صار لغواً. قوله : إن عقد ؛ أي باع أو اشترى بالخيار، وأما إذا عقد عقداً تاماً ، ففي صورة البيع لا يعتق لزوال العبد عن ملكه بمجرد البيع ولا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم بنص الحديث، وفي صورة الشراء يعتق لوجود الشراء والملك، كذا في «العناية»، وغيرها قوله : بالخيار ؛ أي خيار الشرط ثلاثة أيام فما دونها، وحكمه أنه يمنع خروج المبيع إن كان الخيار للبائع اتفاقاً، وإن كان للمشري لا يمنع خروجه عن ملك البائع اتفاقا، وهل يمنع دخوله في ملك المشتري، فيه خلاف، فعنده يمنع، وعندهما: لا، وستطلع على تفصيل هذا البحث في موضعه من كتاب البيوع» إن شاء الله تعالى. قوله : لم يخرج عن ملكه ؛ بناء على أن خيار البائع يمنع خروجه عن ملكه. قوله : فلأنه علق العتق ... الخ ؛ حاصلة أنه علّق العتق بالشراء حيث قال : إن اشتريه فهو حرّ، والمعلّق كالمنجز بعد وجود الشرط، ونحو نجز العتق بعد الشراء بالخيار يثبت الملك سابقا عليه بفسخ الخيار فكذا هذا. أما إن باعه بيعاً لازماً أو باطلاً لم يعتق، أما في البات فلأنه كما تم البيع زال الملك والجزاء لا ينزل في غير الملك.
And: 'This is free if I send him or if I buy it if he contract with option.' Because the mentioned description is not suitable as a preventer from speaking, so the essence is intended. (And: 'This is free if I send him or if I buy it if he contract with option'): meaning if he says: 'If I sell him, he is free', then he sells him with the understanding that he has the option, he becomes free because it did not leave his ownership, and the condition (which is the sale) has been found. And if he says: 'If I buy him, he is free', then he buys him with the understanding that he has the option, he becomes free. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because it entered the ownership of the buyer. And according to Abu Hanifa's principle, because he tied the freedom to the purchase, so it is as if he said after the purchase with option: 'He is free', so he becomes free. [?] And ignorance [he swore?] after the speech with him, or he knew that speaking with the young man as long as he is young harms his religion or his world, so he refrained from it; these oaths are taken upon the duration of the remaining of those descriptions, so it is verified in al-Fath. His statement: 'does not suit as a preventer... etc.'; he pointed to a principle mentioned repeatedly: that the description if it is a motivator for the oath is considered, and if it is not a motivator becomes superfluous. His statement: 'if he contract'; i.e., sells or buys with option. But if he concluded a complete contract, in the case of sale he does not become free because the slave left his ownership by mere sale, and there is no freedom in what one does not own according to the text of the hadith. In the case of purchase, he becomes free due to the existence of purchase and ownership, as in al-Inayah and others. His statement: 'with option'; i.e., option of condition for three days or less. Its ruling is that it prevents the exit of the sold item if the option is for the seller by agreement. If it is for the buyer, it does not prevent its exit from the seller's ownership by agreement. But does it prevent its entry into the ownership of the buyer? There is disagreement: according to him (Abu Hanifa) it prevents, and according to them (the two companions) it does not. You will see the details of this discussion in its place in the book of sales, God willing. His statement: 'did not leave his ownership'; based on that the seller's option prevents its exit from his ownership. His statement: 'because he tied the freedom... etc.'; its summary is that he tied the freedom to the purchase when he said: 'If I buy him, he is free', and the conditional is like the immediate after the existence of the condition, and similar to the immediate freedom after the purchase with option, ownership is established retroactively by cancellation of the option, so this is also the case. As for if he sold it with a binding or void sale, he does not become free. As for the binding sale, because as the sale is completed, ownership is removed, and the recompense does not descend in non-ownership.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths of freedom tied to sale or purchase with khiyar al-shart (option of condition). The base text states that the slave becomes free whether the oath is 'if I sell him' or 'if I buy him' when the contract is made with option. The commentator explains that for sale, the slave does not leave the seller's ownership during the option period, so the condition is met and the slave is freed. For purchase, all agree the slave is freed, but there is a difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the slave enters the buyer's ownership and thus is freed; Abu Hanifa says the option prevents entry into ownership, but the slave is freed because the oath was tied to the act of purchase (the contract) and not to ownership. The commentary then expands on the definition of khiyar al-shart (three days or less) and its effect on ownership transfer: it prevents the seller's ownership from ending, but regarding the buyer's ownership, Abu Hanifa says it prevents entry, the two companions say it does not. The passage also notes that if the sale is binding (without option) or void, the slave is not freed. The principle is that the description (motivation) behind the oath is considered; if the description is not a motivator, it is ignored.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The right to cancel a sale within three days or less, as stipulated in the contract.
باعث (ba'ith): The motivation that prompted the oath; if present, the description is considered effective.
معلّق (mu'allaq): A suspended legal action (e.g., freeing) that becomes effective upon fulfillment of a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'If I buy this slave, he is free' and then buys him with the option of three days, what happens according to all Hanafi imams in this passage?
A) The slave is not free because the option prevents ownership.
B) The slave becomes free, though the reasoning differs among imams.
C) The slave becomes free only if the option is for the seller.
D) The slave becomes free only according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
An oath of freedom tied to a sale or purchase is fulfilled even when the contract includes a conditional option; the Hanafi school is unanimous on the result, with an internal difference in juristic reasoning.
